and Marie laugh a she pull the goat from the hedge where she be nibble the young leaf .
We follow the child a she go clatter down the stony path , and soon come into the narrow street bound on one side by the row of low , stone house , and on the other by the green wet meadow full of willow , and the rapid mill-stream .
All along this side of the road sit woman and child , strip the bark from willow twig to be use in basket-making .
A busy sight and a cheerful one ; for the woman gossip in their high , clear voice , the child sang and laugh , and the baby creep about as freely a young lamb .
We find Marie 's home a very poor one .
Only two room in the little hut , the low one with it earthen floor , bed in the wall , smoky fire , and single window where the loom stand .
At it sit a pale , dark man who stop work a we enter , and seem glad to rest while we talk to him , or rather while Kate do , for I could not understand his odd French , and prefer to watch Marie during the making of the bargain .
Yvon , a stout lad of twelve , be cut up brush with an old sickle , and little Bebe , look like a Dutch doll in her tiny round cap , tight blue gown , and bit of sabot , clung to Marie a she get the supper .
I wonder what the child at home would have say to such a supper .
A few cabbage leaf make the soup , and this , with the dry black bread and a sip of sour wine , be all they have .
There be no plate or bowl , but little hollow place in the heavy wooden table near the edge , and into these fix cup Marie ladle the soup , give each a wooden spoon from a queer rack in the middle ; the kettle stand at one end , the big loaf lay at the other , and all stand round eat out of their little trough , with Nannette and a rough dog close by to receive any crust that might be leave .
Presently the mother come in , a true Breton woman ; rosy and robust , neat and cheery , though her poor clothes be patch all over , her hand more rough and worn with hard work than any I ever saw , and the fine hair under her picturesque cap gray at thirty with much care .
I saw then where Marie get the brightness that seem to shine in every feature of her little face , for the mother 's come be like a ray of sunshine in that dark place , and she have a friendly word and look for every one .
Our little arrangement be soon make , and we leave them all smile and nod a if the few franc we be to pay would be a fortune to them .
Early next morning we be waken by Françoise , the maid , who come up to announce that the goat 's milk have arrive .
Then we hear a queer , quick , tap sound on the stair , and to our great amusement , Nannette walk into the room , straight up to my bedside , and stand there look at me with her mild yellow eye a if she be quite use to see night-caps .
Marie follow with a pretty little bowl in her hand , and say , laugh at our surprise , ` See , dear mademoiselle ; in this way I make sure that the milk be quite fresh and warm ; ' and kneel down , she milk the bowl full in a twinkling , while Nannette quietly chew her cud and sniffed at a plate of roll on the table .
The warm draught be delicious , and we drink each our portion with much merriment .
` It be our custom , ' say Françoise ; who stand by with her arm fold , and look on in a lofty manner .
` What have you for your own breakfast ? '
I ask , a I catch Marie 's eye hungrily fix on the roll and some tempting little cake of chocolate leave from our lunch the day before .
` My good bread , a usual , mademoiselle , also sorrel salad and -- and water , ' answer Marie , a if try to make the most of her scanty meal .
` Will you eat the roll and put the chocolate in your pocket to nibble at school ?
You must be tire with this long walk so early . '
She hesitate , but could not resist ; and say in a low tone , a she hold the bread in her hand without eat it , -- ` Would mademoiselle be angry if I take it to Bebe ?
She have never taste the beautiful white bread , and it would please her much . '
I empty the plate into her basket , tuck in the chocolate , and add a gay picture for baby , which unexpected treasure cause Marie to clasp her hand and turn quite red with delight .
After that she come daily , and we have merry time with old Nannette and her little mistress , whom we soon learn to love , so busy , blithe , and grateful be she .
We soon find a new way to employ her , for the boy who drive our donkey do not suit u , and we get the donkey-woman to let u have Marie in the afternoon when her lesson be do .
She like that , and so do we ; for she seem to understand the nature of donkey , and could manage them without so much beating and shouting a the boy think necessary .
Such pleasant drive a we have , we two big woman in the droll wagon , draw by the little gray donkey that look a if make of an old trunk , so rusty and rough be he a he go trot along , his long ear wag , and his small hoof clattering over the fine hard road , while Marie sit on the shaft with a long whip , talk and laughing , and give Andrè a poke now and then , cry ` E ! E ! houp la ! '
to make him go .
We find her a capital little guide and story-teller , for her grandmother have tell her all the tale and legend of the neighbourhood , and it be very pleasant to hear her repeat them in pretty peasant French , a we sit among the ruin , while Kate sketch , I take note , and Marie hold the big parasol over u .
Some of these stone be charm ; at least a she tell them , with her little face change from gay to sad a she gesticulate most dramatically .
The romance of ` Gilles de Bretagne ' be one of her favourite .
How he carry off his child-wife when she be only twelve , how he be imprison and poison , and at last left to starve in a dungeon , and would stand at his window cry , ` Bread , bread ; for the love of God ! '
yet no one dare to give him any , till a poor peasant woman go in the night and give him half her black loaf .
Not once , but every night for six month , though she rob her child to do it .
And when he be die , it be she who take a priest to him , that he might confess through the bar of his cell .
` So good , ah , so good , this poor woman !
It be beautiful to hear of that , mademoiselle ! '
little Marie would say , with her black eye full and her lip tremble .
But the story she like best of all be about the peasant girl and her grandmother .
` See then , dear lady , it be in this way .
In the time of the great war many poor people be shoot because it be fear they would burn the chateau .
In one of these so sad party be drive to St. Malo to be shot , be this young girl .
Only fifteen , dear lady , behold how young be this !
and see the brave thing she do !
With her go the old grandmother whom she love next the good God .
They go slowly , she be so old , and one of the officer who guard them have pity on the pretty girl , and say to her a they be a little apart from the rest , `` Come , you be young , and can run .
I will save you ; it be a pity so fine a little girl should be shoot . ''
` Then she be glad and thank him much , say , `` And the grandmother also ?
You will save her with me ? ''
`` It be impossible , '' say the officer .
`` She be too old to run .
I can save but one , and her life be nearly over ; let her go , and do you fly into the next wood .
I will not betray you , and when we come up with the gang it will be too late to find you . ''
` Then the great temptation of Satan come to this girl .
She have no wish to suffer , but she could not leave the good old grandmere to die alone .
She weep , she pray , and the saint give her courage . '
`` No , I will not go , '' she say ; and in the morning at St. Malo she be shoot with the old mother in her arm . '
` Could you do that for your grandmere ? '
I once ask , a she stop for breath , because this tale always excite her .
She cross herself devoutly , and answer with fire in her eye , and a resolute gesture of her little brown hand , -- ' I should try , mademoiselle . '
I think she would , and succeed , too , for she be a brave and tender-hearted child , a she soon after prove .
A long drought parch the whole country that summer , and the garden suffer much , especially the little plat in Lehon , for most of them be on the steep hillside behind the hut ; and unless it rain , water have to be carry up from the stream below .
The cabbage and onion on which these poor people depend , when fresh salad be go , be die in the baked earth , and a hard winter be before them if this little store fail .
The priest pray for rain in the church , and long procession stream out of the gate to visit the old stone cross call the ` Croix de Saint Esprit , ' and , kneel there in crowd , the people implore the blessing of rain to save their harvest .
We felt great pity for them , but like little Marie 's way of pray best .
She do not come one morning , but send her brother , who only laugh , and say Marie have hurt her foot , when we inquire for her .
Anxious to know if she be really ill , we go to see her in the afternoon , and hear a pretty little story of practical Christianity .
Marie lay asleep on her mother 's bed in the wall , and her father , sit by her , tell the tale in a low voice , pause now and then to look at her , a if his little daughter have do something to be proud of .
It seem that in the village there be an old woman frightfully disfigure by fire , and not quite sane a the people think .
She be harmless , but never show herself by day , and only come out at night to work in her garden or take the air .
Many of the ignorant peasant fear her , however ; for the country abound in fairy legend , and strange tale of ghost and goblin .
But the more charitable left bread at her door , and take in return the hose she knit or the thread she spin .
During the drought it be observe that her garden , though the steepest and stony , be never dry ; her cabbage flourish when her neighbour ' wither , and her onion stand up green and tall a if some special rain-spirit watch over them .
People wonder and shake their head , but could not explain it , for Mother Lobineau be too infirm to carry much water up the steep path , and who would help her unless some of her own goblin friend do it ?
This idea be suggest by the story of a peasant returning late at night , who have see something white flitting to and fro in the garden-patch , and when he call to it saw it vanish most mysteriously .
This make quite a stir in the town ; others watch also , saw the white phantom in the starlight , and could not tell where it go when it vanish behind the chestnut tree on the hill , till one man , braver than the rest , hid himself behind these tree and discover the mystery .
The sprite be Marie , in her little shift , who step out of the window of the loft where she sleep on to a bough of the tree , and thence to the hill , for the house be build so close against the bank that it be ` but a step from garret to garden , ' a they say in Morlaix .
In try to escape from this inquisitive neighbour , Marie hurt her foot , but be catch , and confess that it be she who go at night to water poor Mother Lobineau 's cabbage ; because if they fail the old woman might starve , and no one else remember her destitute and helpless state .
The good-hearted people be much touch by this silent sermon on love one 's neighbour a one 's self , and Marie be call the ` little saint , ' and tend carefully by all the good woman .
Just a the story end , she wake up , and at first seem inclined to hide under the bedclothes .
But we have her out in a minute , and presently she be laugh over her good deed , with a true child 's enjoyment of a bit of roguery , say in her simple way , -- ` Yes ; it be so droll to go run about en chemise , like the girl in the tale of the ` Midsummer Eve , ' where she pull the Saint Johns-wort flower , and have her wish to hear all the creature talk .
I like it much , and Yvon sleep so like the dormouse that he never hear me creep in and out .
It be hard to bring much water , but the poor cabbage be so glad , and Mother Lobineau felt that all have not forget her .
We take care that little Saint Marie be not forget , but quite well , and all ready for her confirmation when the day come .
This be a pretty sight , and for her sake we go to the old church of St. Sauveur to see it .
It be a bright spring day , and the garden be full of early flower , the quaint street gay with proud father and mother in holiday dress , and flock of stranger pause to see the long procession of little girl with white cap and veil , glove and gown , prayer-books and rosary , wind through the sunny square into the shadowy church with chanting and candle , garland and cross .
The old priest be too ill to perform the service , but the young one who take his place announce , after it be over , that if they would pass the house the good old man would bless them from his balcony .
That be the best of all , and a sweet sight , a the feeble fatherly old priest lean from his easy-chair to stretch his tremble hand over the little flock so like a bed of snowdrop , while the bright eye and rosy face look reverently up at him , and the fresh voice chant the response a the curly head under the long veil bow and pass by .
We learn afterwards that our Marie have be call in and praise for her secret charity -- a great honour , because the good priest be much beloved by all his flock , and take a most paternal interest in the little one .
That be almost the last we saw of our little friend , for we leave Dinan soon after , bid the Lehon family good-bye , and leave certain warm souvenir for winter-time .
Marie cry and clung to u at part , then smile like an April day , and wave her hand a we go away , never expect to see her any more .
But the next morning , just a we be step on board the steamer to go down the Rance to St. Malo , we saw a little white cap come bobbing through the market-place , down the steep street , and presently Marie appear with two great bunch of pale yellow primrose and wild blue hyacinth in one hand , while the other hold her sabot , that she might run the faster .
Rosy and smile and breathless with haste she come race up to u , cry , -- ` Behold my souvenir for the dear lady .
I do not cry now .
No ; I be glad the day be so fine .
Bon voyage !
bon voyage !
' We thank and kiss and leave her on the shore , bravely try not to cry , a she wave her wooden shoe and kiss her hand till we be out of sight , and have nothing but the soft colour and sweet breath of our nosegay to remind u of Little Marie of Lehon .
MY MAY-DAY AMONG CURIOUS BIRDS AND BEASTS .
Being alone in London , yet wish to celebrate the day , I decide to pay my respect to the lion at the Zoological Gardens .
A lovely place it be , and I enjoy myself immensely ; for May-day in England be just what it should be , mild , sunny , flowery , and spring-like .
As I walk along the well-kept path , between white and rosy hawthorn hedge , I keep come upon new and curious sight ; for the bird and beast be so skilfully arrange that it be more like travel through a strange and pleasant country than visit a menagerie .
The first thing I saw be a great American bison ; and I be so glad to meet with any one from home , that I 'd have pat him with pleasure if he have show any cordiality toward me .
He do n't , however , but star savagely with his fiery eye , and put down his immense head with a sullen snort , a if he 'd have toss me with great satisfaction .
I do not blame him , for the poor fellow be homesick , doubtless , for his own wide prairie and the free life he have lose .
So I throw him some fresh clover , and go on to the pelican .
I never know before what handsome bird they be ; not graceful , but with such snowy plumage , ting with pale pink and faint yellow .
They have just have their bath , and stand arrange their feather with their great bill , utter a queer cry now and then , and nod to one another sociably .
When feed , they gobble up the fish , never stop to swallow it till the pouch under their bill be full ; then they leisurely empty them , and seem to enjoy their lunch with the grave deliberation of regular Englishmen .
Being in a hurry to see the lion , I go on to the long row of cage , and there find a splendid sight .
Six lion and lioness , in three or four different cage , sit or stand in dignified attitude , and eye the spectator with a mild expression in their fine eye .
One lioness be ill , and lay on her bed , look very pensive , while her mate move restlessly about her , evidently anxious to do something for her , and much afflict by her suffering .
I like this lion very much , for , though the big , he be very gentle , and have a noble face .
The tiger be rush about , a tiger usually be ; some creep noiselessly to and fro , some leap up and down , and some wash their face with their velvet paw .
All look and act so like cat that I be n't at all surprised to hear one of them purr when the keeper scratch her head .
It be a very loud and large purr , but no fireside pussy could have do it good , and every one laugh at the sound .
There be pretty spotted leopard , panther , and small variety of the same specie .
I sit watch them a long time , long to let some of the wild thing out for a good run , they seem so unhappy bar in those small den .
Suddenly the lion begin to roar , the tiger to snarl , and all to get very much excite about something , sniff at the opening , thrust their paw through the bar , and lash their tail impatiently .
I could n't imagine what the trouble be , till , far down the line , I saw a man with a barrowful of lump of raw meat .
This be their dinner , and a they be feed but once a day they be ravenous .
Such roar and howl and cry a arose while the man go slowly down the line , give one a good idea of the sound to be hear in Indian forest and jungle .
The lion behave best , for they only pace up and down , with an occasional cry ; but the tiger be quite frantic ; for they tumble one over the other , shake the cage , and try to reach the bystander , just out of reach behind the bar that keep u at a safe distance .
One lady have a fright , for the wind blow the end of her shawl within reach of a tiger 's great claw , and he clutch it , try to drag her nearer .
The shawl come off , and the poor lady run away scream , a if a whole family of wild beast be after her .
When the lump of meat be throw in , it be curious to see how differently the animal behave .
The tiger snarl and fought and tore and get so savage I be very grateful that they be safely shut up .
In a few minute , nothing but white bone remain , and then they howl for more .
One little leopard be well breed than the others , for he go up on a shelf in the cage , and eat his dinner in a quiet , proper manner , which be an example to the rest .
The lion eat in dignified silence , all but my favourite , who carry his share to his sick mate , and by every gentle mean in his power try to make her eat .
She be too ill , however , and turn away with a plaintive moan which seem to grieve him sadly .
He would n't touch his dinner , but lay down near her , with the lump between his paw , a if guard it for her ; and there I leave him patiently wait , in spite of his hunger , till his mate could share it with him .
As I take a last look at his fine old face , I name him Douglas , and walk away , hum to myself the line of the ballad , -- Douglas , Douglas , Tender and true .
As a contrast to the wild beast , I go to see the monkey , who live in a fine large house all to themselves .
Here be every variety , from the great ugly chimpanzee to the funny little fellow who play like boy , and cut up all sort of caper .
A mamma sit tend her baby , and look so like a little old woman that I laugh till the gray monkey with the blue nose scold at me .
He be a cross old party , and sit huddle up in the straw , scowl at every one , like an ill-tempered old bachelor .
Half-a-dozen little one tease him capitally by drop bit of bread , nut-shells , and straw down on him from above , a they climb about the perch , or swung by their tail .
One poor little chap have lose the curly end of his tail , -- I 'm afraid the gray one bit it off , -- and keep try to swing like the others , forget that the strong , curly end be what he hold on with .
He would run up the bare boughs , and give a jump , expect to catch and swing , but the lame tail would n't hold him , and down he 'd go , bounce on to the straw .
At first he 'd sit and stare about him , a if much amaze to find himself there ; then he 'd scratch his little round head and begin to scold violently , which seem to delight the other monkey ; and , finally , he 'd examine his poor little tail , and appear to understand the misfortune which have befall him .
The funny expression of his face be irresistible , and I enjoy see him very much , and give him a bun to comfort him when I go away .
The snake-house come next , and I go in , on my way to visit the rhinoceros family .
I rather like snake , since I have a tame green one , who live under the door-step , and would come out and play with me on sunny day .
These snake I find very interesting , only they get under their blanket and would n't come out , and I be n't allow to poke them ; so I miss see several of the most curious .
An ugly cobra laid and blink at me through the glass , look quite as dangerous a he be .
There be big and little snake , -- black , brown , and speckle , lively and lazy , pretty and plain one , -- but I like the great boa best .
When I come to his cage , I do n't see anything but the branch of a tree , such a I have see in other cage , for the snake to wind up and down .
` Where be he , I wonder ?
I hope he have n't get out , ' I say to myself , think of a story I read once of a person in a menagerie , who turn suddenly and saw a great boa glide towards him .
As I stand wonder if the big worm could be under the little flat blanket before me , the branch begin to move all at once , and with a start , I saw a limb swing down to stare at me with the boa 's glittering eye .
He be so exactly the colour of the bare bough , and lay so still , I have not see him till he come to take a look at me .
A very villainous-looking reptile he be , and I felt grateful that I do n't live in a country where such unpleasant neighbour might pop in upon you unexpectedly .
He be kind enough to take a promenade and show me his size , which seem immense , a he stretch himself , and then knot his rough grayish body into a great loop , with the fiery-eyed head in the middle .
He be not one of the large kind , but I be quite satisfied , and leave him to his dinner of rabbit , which I have n't the heart to stay and see him devour alive .
I be walk toward the camel 's pagoda , when , all of a sudden , a long , dark , curl thing come over my shoulder , and I felt warm breath in my face .
` It 's the boa ; ' I think , and give a skip which carry me into the hedge , where I stick , much to the amusement of some child rid on the elephant whose trunk have frighten me .
He have politely try to tell me to clear the way , which I certainly have do with all speed .
Picking myself out of the hedge I walk beside him , examine his clumsy foot and peer up at his small , intelligent eye .
I 'm very sure he wink at me , a if enjoy the joke , and keep poke his trunk into my pocket , hop to find something eatable .
I felt a if I have get into a foreign country a I look about me and saw elephant and camel walk among the tree ; flock of snow-white crane stalk over the grass , on their long scarlet leg ; strip Zebras race in their paddock ; queer kangaroos hop about , with little one in their pouch ; pretty antelopes chase one another ; and , in an immense wire-covered aviary , all sort of brilliant bird be fly about as gaily a if at home .
One of the curiosity be a sea-cow , who live in a tank of salt water , and come at the keeper 's call to kiss him , and flounder on it flipper along the margin of the tank after a fish .
It be very like a seal , only much large , and have four fin instead of two .
Its eye be lovely , so dark and soft and liquid ; but it mouth be not pretty , and I decline one of the damp kiss which it be ready to dispense at word of command .
The great polar bear live next door , and spend his time splash in and out of a pool of water , or sit on a block of ice , pant , a if the mild spring day be blaze midsummer .
He look very unhappy , and I think it a pity that they do n't invent a big refrigerator for him .
These be not half of the wonderful creature I saw , but I have not room to tell more ; only I advise all who can to pay a visit to the Zoological Gardens when they go to London , for it be one of the most interesting sight in that fine old city .
OUR LITTLE NEWSBOY .
Hurrying to catch a certain car at a certain corner late one stormy night , I be suddenly arrest by the sight of a queer-looking bundle lie in a door-way .
` Bless my heart , it 's a child !
O John !
I 'm afraid he 's frozen ! '
I exclaim to my brother , a we both bent over the bundle .
Such a little fellow a he be , in the big , ragged coat ; such a tired , baby face , under the fuzzy cap ; such a purple , little hand , still hold fast a few paper ; such a pathetic sight altogether be the boy , lie on the stone step , with the snow drifting over him , that it be impossible to go by .
` He be asleep ; but he 'll freeze , if leave so long .
Here !
wake up , my boy , and go home , as fast a you can , ' cry John , with a gentle shake , and a very gentle voice ; for the memory of a dear little lad , safely tuck up at home , make him fatherly kind to the small vagabond .
The moment he be touch , the boy tumble up , and , before he be half awake , begin his usual cry , with an eye to business .
` Paper , sir ?
`` Herald ! ''
`` Transkip ! ''
Last ' -- a great gape swallow up the ` last edition , ' and he stand blink at u like a very chilly young owl .
` I 'll buy 'em all if you 'll go home , my little chap ; it 's high time you be abed , ' say John , whisk the damp paper into one pocket , and his purse out of another , a he speak .
` All of 'em ?
-- why there 's six ! '
croak the boy , for he be as hoarse a a raven .
` Never mind , I can kindle the fire with 'em .
Put that in your pocket ; and trot home , my man , as fast a possible . '
` Where do you live ? '
I ask , pick up the fifty cent that fell from the little finger , too benumb to hold it .
` Mills Court , out of Hanover .
Cold , ai n't it ? '
say the boy , blow on his purple hand , and hop feebly from one leg to the other , to take the stiffness out .
` He ca n't go all that way in this storm -- such a mite , and so use up with cold and sleep , John . '
` Of course he ca n't ; we 'll put him in a car , ' begin John ; when the boy wheeze out , -- ` No ; I 've get ter wait for Sam .
He 'll be along a soon 's the theatre 's do .
He say he would ; and so I 'm waitin ' . '
` Who be Sam ? '
I ask .
` He 's the feller I live with .
I ai n't get any folk , and he take care o ' me . '
` Nice care , indeed ; leave a baby like you to wait for him here such a night a this , ' I say crossly .
` Oh , he 's good to me Sam be , though he do knock me round sometimes , when I ai n't spry .
The big feller shove me back , you see ; and I get cold , and ca n't sing out loud ; so I do n't sell my paper , and have to work 'em off late . '
` Hear the child talk !
One would think he be sixteen , instead of six , ' I say , half laughing .
` I 'm most ten .
Hi !
ai n't that a oner ? '
cry the boy , a a gust of sleet slap him in the face , when he peep to see if Sam be come .
` Hullo !
the light be out !
Why , the play 's do , and the folk go , and Sam 's forgot me . '
It be very evident that Sam have forget his little protégé ; and a strong desire to shake Sam possess me .
` No use waitin ' any longer ; and now my paper be sell , I ai n't afraid to go home , ' say the boy , step down like a little old man with the rheumatism , and prepare to trudge away through the storm .
` Stop a bit , my little Casabianca ; a car will be along in fifteen minute ; and while wait you can warm yourself over there , ' say John , with the purple hand in his .
` My name 's Jack Hill , not Cassy Banks , please , sir , ' say the little party , with dignity .
` Have you have your supper , Mr. Hill ? '
ask John , laugh . '
I have some peanut , and two suck of Joe 's orange ; but it war n't very fillin ' , ' he say , gravely . '
I should think not .
Here !
one stew ; and be quick , please , ' cry John , a we sit down in a warm corner of the confectioner 's opposite .
While little Jack shovel in the hot oyster , with his eye shut up now and then in spite of himself , we look at him and think again of little Rosy-face at home safe in his warm nest , with mother-love watching over him .
Nodding towards the ragged , grimy , forlorn , little creature , drop asleep over his supper like a tired baby , I say , -- ` Can you imagine our Freddy out alone at this hour , try to ` work off ' his paper , because afraid to go home till he have ? '
` I 'd rather not try , ' answer brother John , wink hard , a he stroke the little head beside him , which , by the bye , look very like a ragged , yellow door-mat .
I think brother John wink hard , but I ca n't be sure , for I know I do ; and for a minute there seem to be a dozen little newsboys dance before my eye .
` There go our car ; and it 's the last , ' say John , look at me .

` Here be his car .

` Good-night , ma'am !
thankee , sir ! '
croak the grateful little voice , a the child be catch up in John 's strong hand and set down on the car-step .
With a word to the conductor , and a small business transaction , we leave Jack coil up in a corner to finish his nap as tranquilly a if it be n't midnight , and a ` knocking-round ' might not await him at his journey 's end .
We do n't mind the storm much a we plod home ; and when I tell the story to Rosy-face , next day , his interest quite reconcile me to the sniff and sneeze of a bad cold .
` If I saw that poor little boy , Aunt Jo , I 'd love him lots ! '
say Freddy , with a world of pity in his beautiful child 's eye .
And , believe that others also would be kind to little Jack , and such a he , I tell the story .
When busy father hurry home at night , I hope they 'll buy their paper of the small boy , who get ` shove back ; ' the feeble one , who grow hoarse , and ca n't ` sing out ; ' the shabby one , who evidently have only forgetful Sams to care for them ; and the hungry-looking one , who do n't get what be ` fillin ' . '
For love of the little son and daughter safe at home , say a kind word , buy a paper , even if you do n't want it ; and never pas by , leave them to sleep forgotten in the street at midnight , with no pillow but a stone , no coverlet but the pitiless snow , and not even a tender-hearted robin to drop leaf over them .
PATTY 'S PATCHWORK .
' I perfectly hate it !
and something dreadful ought to be do to the woman who invent it , ' say Patty , in a pet , send a shower of gay piece fly over the carpet a if a small whirlwind and a rainbow have get into a quarrel .
Puss do not agree with Patty , for , after a surprised hop when the flurry come , she calmly lay herself down on a red square , purr comfortably and wink her yellow eye , a if she thank the little girl for the bright bed that set off her white fur so prettily .
This cool performance make Patty laugh , and say more pleasantly -- ` Well , it be tiresome , be n't it , Aunt Pen ? '
` Sometimes ; but we all have to make patchwork , my dear , and do the best we can with the piece give u . '
` Do we ? '
and Patty open her eye in great astonishment at this new idea .
` Our life be patchwork , and it depend on u a good deal how the bright and dark bit get put together so that the whole be neat , pretty , and useful when it be do , ' say Aunt Pen soberly .
` Deary me , now she be go to preach , ' thought Patty ; but she rather like Aunt Pen 's preachment , for a good deal of fun get mixed up with the moralising ; and she be so good herself that child could never say in their naughty little mind , ` You be just as bad a we , so you need n't talk to u , ma'am . ' '
I give you that patchwork to see what you would make of it , and it be as good a a diary to me , for I can tell by the different square how you felt when you make them , ' continue Aunt Pen , with a twinkle in her eye a she glance at the many-coloured bit on the carpet .
` Can you truly ?
just try and see , ' and Patty look interested at once .
Pointing with the yard-measure , Aunt Pen say , tap a certain dingy , pucker , brown and purple square -- ` That be a bad day ; do n't it look so ? '
` Well , it be , I do declare !
for that be the Monday piece , when everything go wrong and I do n't care how my work look , ' cry Patty , surprise at Aunt Pen 's skill in read the calico diary .
` This pretty pink and white one so neatly sew be a good day ; this funny mixture of red , blue , and yellow with the big stitch be a merry day ; that one with spot on it be one that get cry over ; this with the gay flower be a day full of good little plan and resolution ; and that one made of dainty bit , all star and dot and tiny leaf , be the one you make when you be think about the dear new baby there at home . '
` Why , Aunt Pen , you be a fairy !
How do you know ?
they truly be just a you say , as near a I can remember .
I rather like that sort of patchwork , ' and Patty sit down upon the floor to collect , examine , and arrange her discarded work with a new interest in it . '
I see what be go on , and I have queer play in my mind just a you little folk do .
Suppose you make this a moral bed-quilt , a some people make album quilt .
See how much patience , perseverance , good nature , and industry you can put into it .
Every bit will have a lesson or a story , and when you lie under it you will find it a real comforter , ' say Aunt Pen , who want to amuse the child and teach her something well even than the good old-fashioned accomplishment of needlework . '
I do n't see how I can put that sort of thing into it , ' answer Patty , a she gently lift pus into her lap , instead of twitch the red bit roughly from under her .
` There go a nice little piece of kindness this very minute , ' laugh Aunt Pen , point to the cat and the red square .
Patty laugh also , and look pleased a she stroke Mother Bunch , while she say thoughtfully -- ' I see what you mean now .
I be make two kind of patchwork at the same time ; and this that I see be to remind me of the other kind that I do n't see . '
` Every task , no matter how small or homely , that get well and cheerfully do , be a fine thing ; and the sooner we learn to use up the dark and bright bit -LRB- the pleasure and pain , the care and duty -RRB- into a cheerful , useful life , the sooner we become real comforter , and every one like to cuddle about u .
Do n't you see , deary ? '
` That 's what you be , Aunt Pen ; ' and Patty put up her hand to hold fast by that other strong , kind , helpful hand that do so much , yet never be tire , cold , or empty .
Aunt Pen take the chubby little one in both her own , and say , smile , yet with meaning in her eye , a she tap the small fore-finger , rough with impatient and unskilful sewing -- ` Shall we try and see what a nice little comforter we can make this month , while you wait to be call home to see mamma and the dear new baby ? '
` Yes , I 'd like to try ; ' and Patty give Aunt Pen 's hand a hearty shake , for she want to be good , and rather think the new fancy would lend a charm to the task which we all find rather tiresome and hard .
So the bargain be make , and the patch Patty sew that day be beautiful to behold ; for she be in a delightfully moral state of mind , and felt quite sure that she be go to become a model for all child to follow , if they could .
The next day her ardour have cool a little , and be in a hurry to go out to play , she slight her work , think no one would know .
But the third day she get so angry with her patch that she tear it in two , and declare it be all nonsense to fuss about be good and thorough and all the rest of it .
Aunt Pen do not say much , but make her mend and finish her patch and add it to the pile .
After she go to bed that night Patty thought of it , and wish she could do it over , it look so badly .
But a it could not be , she have a penitent fit , and resolve to keep her temper while she sew , at any rate , for mamma be to see the little quilt when it be do , and would want to know all about it .
Of course she do not devote herself to be good all the time , but spend her day in lesson , play , mischief , and fun , like any other lively , ten-year-older .
But somehow , whenever the sewing-hour come , she remember that talk ; and a she work she fell into the way of wonder whether Aunt Pen could guess from the patch what sort of day she have pass .
She want to try and see , but Aunt Pen refuse to read any more calico till the quilt be do : then , she say in a queer , solemn way , she should make the good and bad day appear in a remarkable manner .
This puzzle Patty very much , and she quite ache to know what the joke would be ; meantime the pile grow steadily , and every day , good or bad , add to that other work call Patty 's life .
She do not think much about that part of it , but unconsciously the quiet sewing-time have it influence on her , and that little ` conscience hour , ' a she sometimes call it , help her very much .
One day she say to herself a she take up her work , ` Now I 'll puzzle Aunt Pen .
She think my naughty trick get into the patch ; but I 'll make this very nicely and have it gay , and then I do n't see how she will ever guess what I do this morning . '
Now you must know that Tweedle-dee , the canary , be let out every day to fly about the room and enjoy himself .
Mother Bunch never try to catch him , though he often hop temptingly near her .
He be a droll little bird , and Patty like to watch his promenade , for he do funny thing .
That day he make her laugh by try to fly away with a shawl , pick up the fringe with which to line the nest he be always try to build .
It be so heavy he tumble on his back and lay kicking and pulling , but have to give it up and content himself with a bit of thread .
Patty be forbid to chase or touch him at these time , but always felt a strong desire to have just one grab at him and see how he felt .
That day , be alone in the dining-room , she find it impossible to resist ; and when Tweedle-dee come trip pertly over the table-cloth , cock his head on one side with shrill chirp and little prancings , she catch him , and for a minute hold him fast in spite of his wrathful pecking .
She put her thimble on his head , laugh to see how funny he look , and just then he slip out of her hand .
She clutch at him , miss him , but ala , ala !
he leave his little tail behind him .
Every feather in his blessed little tail , I do assure you ; and there sat Patty with the yellow plume in her hand and dismay in her face .
Poor Tweedle-dee retire to his cage much afflict , and sing no more that day , but Patty hide the lose tail and never say a word about it .
` Aunt Pen be so near-sighted she wo n't mind , and maybe he will have another tail pretty soon , or she will think he be moult .
If she ask of course I shall tell her . '
Patty settle it in that way , forget that the slide be open and Aunt Pen in the kitchen .
So she make a neat blue and buff patch , and put it away , mean to puzzle aunty when the reading-time come .
But Patty get the bad of it , a you will see by-and-bye .
Another day she stroll into the store-room and saw a large tray of fresh bun stand there .
Now , it be against the rule to eat between meal , and new hot bread or cake be especially forbidden .
Patty remember both these thing , but could not resist temptation .
One plump , brown bun , with a lovely plum right in the middle , be so fascinate it be impossible to let it alone ; so Patty whip it into her pocket , run to the garden , and hide behind the big lilac-bush , eat it in a great hurry .
It be just out of the oven , and so hot it burn her throat , and lay like a live coal in her little stomach after it be down , make her very uncomfortable for several hour .
` Why do you keep sigh ? '
ask Aunt Pen , a Patty sit down to her work . '
I do n't feel very well . '
` You have eat something that disagree with you .
Did you eat hot biscuit for breakfast ? '
` No , ma'am , I never do , ' and Patty give another little gasp , for the bun lay very heavily on both stomach and conscience just then . '
A drop or two of ammonia will set you right , ' and Aunt Pen give her some .
It do set the stomach right , but the conscience still worry her , for she could not make up her mind to ` fess ' the sly , greedy thing she have do .
` Put a white patch in the middle of those green one , ' say Aunt Pen , a Patty sit soberly sew her daily square .
` Why ? '
ask the little girl , for aunty seldom interfere in her arrangement of the quilt .
` It will look pretty , and match the other three square that be go at the corner of that middle piece . '
` Well , I will , ' and Patty sew away , wonder at this sudden interest in her work , and why Aunt Pen laugh to herself a she put away the ammonia bottle .
These be two of the naughty little thing that get work into the quilt ; but there be good one also , and Aunt Pen 's sharp eye saw them all .
At the window of a house opposite , Patty often saw a little girl who sit there play with an old doll or a torn book .
She never seem to run about or go out , and Patty often wonder if she be sick , she look so thin and sober , and be so quiet .
Patty begin by make face at her for fun , but the little girl only smile back , and nod so good-naturedly that Patty be ashamed of herself .
` Is that girl over there poor ? '
she ask suddenly a she watch her one day .
` Very poor : her mother take in sew , and the child be lame , ' answer Aunt Pen , without look up from the letter she be write .
` Her doll be nothing but an old shawl tie round with a string , and she do n't seem to have but one book .
Wonder if she 'd like to have me come and play with her , ' say Patty to herself , a she stand her own big doll in the window , and nod back at the girl , who bob up and down in her chair with delight at this agreeable prospect .
` You can go and see her some day if you like , ' say Aunt Pen , scribble away .
Patty say no more then , but later in the afternoon she remember this permission , and resolve to try if aunty would find out her good doings as well a her bad one .
So , tuck Blanch Augusta Arabella Maud under one arm , her best picture-book under the other , and gather a little nosegay of her own flower , she slip across the road , knock , and march boldly upstairs .
Mrs. Brown , the sewing-woman , be out , and no one there but Lizzie in her chair at the window , look lonely and forlorn .
` How do you do ?
My name be Patty , and I live over there , and I 've come to play with you , ' say one child in a friendly tone .
` How do you do ?
My name be Lizzie , and I 'm very glad to see you .
What a lovely doll ! '
return the other child gratefully ; and then the ceremony of introduction be over , and they begin to play a if they have know each other for ever so long .
To poor Lizzie it seem a if a little fairy have suddenly appear to brighten the dismal room with flower and smile and pretty thing ; while Patty felt her pity and good-will increase a she saw Lizzie 's crippled foot , and watch her thin face brighten and glow with interest and delight over book and doll and posy .
` It felt good , ' a Patty say afterwards ; ` sort of warm and comfortable in my heart , and I like it ever so much . '
She stay an hour , make sunshine in a shady place , and then run home , wonder if Aunt Pen would find that out .
She find her sit with her hand before her , and such a sad look in her face that Patty run to her , say anxiously -- ` What 's the matter , aunty ?
Are you sick ? '
` No dear ; but I have sorrowful news for you .
Come , sit in my lap and let me tell you as gently a I can . '
` Mamma be dead ! '
Cried Patty with a look of terror in her rosy face .
` No , thank God !
but the dear , new baby only stay a week , and we shall never see her in this world . '
With a cry of sorrow Patty throw herself into the arm outstretched to her , and on Aunt Pen 's love bosom sob away the first bitterness of her grief and disappointment .
` Oh , I want a little sister so much , and I be go to be so fond of her , and be so glad she come , and now I ca n't see or have her even for a day !
I 'm so disappointed I do n't think I can bear it , ' sob Patty .
` Think of poor mamma , and bear it bravely for her sake , ' whisper Aunt Pen , wipe away her own and Patty 's tear .
` Oh , dear me !
there 's the pretty quilt I be go to make for baby , and now it be n't any use , and I ca n't bear to finish it ; ' and Patty break out afresh at the thought of so much love 's labour lose .
` Mamma will love to see it , so I would n't give it up .
Work be the best cure for sorrow ; and I think you never will be sorry you try it .
Let u put a bright bit of submission with this dark trouble , and work both into your little life as patiently a we can , deary . '
Patty put up her tremble lip , and kiss Aunt Pen , grateful for the tender sympathy and the helpful word .
` I 'll try , ' be all she say ; and then they sit talk quietly together about the dear , dead baby , who only stay long enough to make a place in every one 's heart , and leave them ache when she go .
Patty do try to bear her first trouble bravely , and get on very well after the first day or two , except when the sewing-hour come .
Then the sight of the pretty patchwork recall the memory of the cradle it be mean to cover , and remind her that it be empty now .
Many quiet tear drop on Patty 's work ; and sometimes she have to put it down and sob , for she have long so for a little sister , it be very hard to give her up , and put away all the loving plan she have make for the happy time when baby come .
A great many tender little thought and feeling get sew into the gay square ; and if a small stain show here and there , I think they only add to it beauty in the eye of those who know what make them .
Aunt Pen never suggest pick out certain puckered bit and grimy stitch , for she know that just there the little finger tremble , and the blue eye get dim a they touch and saw the delicate , flowery bit leave from baby 's gown .
Lizzie be full of sympathy , and come hop over on her crutch with her only treasure , a black rabbit , to console her friend .
But of all the comfort give , Mother Bunch 's share be the great and best ; for that very first sad day , a Patty wander about the house disconsolately , pus come hurry to meet her , and in her dumb way beg her mistress to follow and see the fine surprise prepared for her -- four plump kit as white a snow , with four gray tail all wagging in a row , a they lay on their proud mamma 's downy breast , while she purr over them , with her yellow eye full of supreme content .
It be in the barn , and Patty lay for an hour with her head close to Mother Bunch , and her hand softly touch the charming little Bunches , who squeak and tumble and sprawl about with their dim eye blink , their tiny pink paw fumbling , and their dear gray tail waggle in the sweet way .
Such a comfort a they be to Patty no word could tell , and nothing will ever convince me that Mrs . Bunch do not know all about baby , and so lay herself out to cheer up her little mistress like a motherly loving old pus , a she be .
As Patty lay on the rug that even while Aunt Pen sing softly in the twilight , a small , white figure come pattering over the straw carpet , and drop a soft , warm ball down by Patty 's cheek , say , as plainly a a loud , confide purr could say it -- ` There , my dear , this be a lonely time for you , I know , so I 've bring my best and prettiest darling to comfort you ; ' and with that Mother Bunch sit down and wash her face , while Patty cuddle little Snowdrop , and forget to cry about baby .
Soon after this come a great happiness to Patty in the shape of a letter from mamma , say she must have her little girl back a week early than they have plan .
` I 'm sorry to leave you , aunty , but it be so nice to be want , and I 'm all mamma have now , you know , so I must hurry and finish my work to surprise her with .
How shall we finish it off ?
There ought to be something regularly splendid to go all round , ' say Patty , in a great bustle , a she lay out her piece , and find that only a few more be need to complete the ` moral bed-quilt . ' '
I must try and find something .
We will put this white star , with the blue round it , in the middle , for it be the neat and pretty piece , in spite of the stain .
I will sew in this part , and you may finish put the long strip together , ' say Aunt Pen , rummage her bag and bundle for something fine to end off with . '
I know !
I 've get something ! '
and away hurried Lizzie , who be there , and much interested in the work .
She come hop back again , presently , with a roll in her hand , which she proudly spread out , say -- ` There !
mother give me that ever so long ago , but I never have any quilt to use it for , and now it 's just what you want .
You ca n't buy such chintz now-a-days , and I 'm so glad I have it for you . '
` It 's regularly splendid ! '
cry Patty , in a rapture ; and so it be , for the pink and white be all cover with animal , and the blue be full of bird and butterfly and bee fly about as naturally a possible .
Really lovely be the little figure and the clear , soft colour , and Aunt Pen clap her hand , while Patty hug her friend , and declare that the quilt be perfect now .
Mrs. Brown beg to be allow to quilt it when the patch be all nicely put together , and Patty be glad to have her , for that part of the work be beyond her skill .
It do not come home till the morning Patty leave , and Aunt Pen pack it up without ever unroll it .
` We will look at it together when we show it to mamma , ' she say : and Patty be in such a hurry to be off that she make no objection .
A pleasant journey , a great deal of hugging and kissing , some tear and tender lament for baby , and then it be time to show the quilt , which mamma say be just what she want to throw over her foot a she lay on the sofa .
If there be any fairy , Patty would have be sure they have do something to her bed-cover , for when she proudly unroll it , what do you think she saw ?
Right in the middle of the white star , which be the centre-piece , delicately draw with indelible ink , be a smiling little cherub , all head and wing , and under it these line -- ` While sister dear lie asleep , Baby careful watch will keep . '
Then in each of the four gay square that be at the corner of the strip that frame the star , be a white bit bear other picture and couplet that both pleased and abashed Patty a she saw and read them .
In one be see a remarkably fine bun , with the line -- ` Who steal the hot bun And get burnt well ?
Go ask the lilac bush , Guess it can tell . '
In the next be a plump , tailless bird , who seem to be say mournfully -- ` My little tail , my little tail !
This bitter loss I still bewail ; But rather ne'er have tail again Than Patty should deceive Aunt Pen . '
The third be less embarrassing , for it be a pretty bunch of flower so daintily draw one could almost think they smelt them , and these line be underneath -- ` Every flower to others give , Blossoms fair and sweet in heaven . '
The fourth be a picture of a curly-haired child sewing , with some very large tear roll down her cheek and tumble off her lap like marble , while some tiny sprite be catch and fly away with them a if they be very precious -- ` Every tender drop that fell , Loving spirit catch and keep ; And Patty 's sorrow lighter grow , For the gentle tear she weep . '
` Oh , aunty !
what do it all mean ? '
cry Patty , who have look both pleased and ashamed a she glance from one picture to the other .
` It mean , dear , that the good and bad get into the bed-quilt in spite of you , and there they be to tell their own story .
The bun and the lost tail , the posy you take to poor Lizzie , and the trouble you bore so sweetly .
It be just so with our life , though we do n't see it quite as clearly a this .
Invisible hand paint our fault and virtue , and by-and-bye we have to see them , so we must be careful that they be good and lovely , and we be not ashamed to let the eye that love u best read there the history of our life . '
As Aunt Pen speak , and Patty listen with a thoughtful face , mamma softly draw the pictured coverlet over her , and whisper , a she hold her little daughter close -- ` My Patty will remember this ; and if all her year tell as good a story a this month , I shall not fear to read the record , and she will be in truth my little comforter . '

I .
AN OLD-FASHIONED THANKSGIVING .
Sixty year ago , up among the New Hampshire hill , live Farmer Bassett , with a house full of sturdy son and daughter grow up about him .
They be poor in money , but rich in land and love , for the wide acre of wood , corn , and pasture land feed , warm , and clothe the flock , while mutual patience , affection , and courage make the old farm-house a very happy home .
November have come ; the crop be in , and barn , buttery , and bin be overflow with the harvest that reward the summer 's hard work .
The big kitchen be a jolly place just now , for in the great fireplace roar a cheerful fire ; on the wall hang garland of dried apple , onion , and corn ; up aloft from the beam shine crook-necked squash , juicy ham , and dry venison -- for in those day deer still haunt the deep forest , and hunter flourish .
Savory smell be in the air ; on the crane hung steam kettle , and down among the red ember copper sauce-pans simmer , all suggestive of some approach feast .
A white-headed baby lay in the old blue cradle that have rock seven other baby , now and then lift his head to look out , like a round , full moon , then subside to kick and crow contentedly , and suck the rosy apple he have no teeth to bite .
Two small boy sit on the wooden settle shell corn for popping , and pick out the big nut from the goodly store their own hand have gather in October .
Four young girl stand at the long dresser , busily chop meat , pound spice , and slice apple ; and the tongue of Tilly , Prue , Roxy , and Rhody go as fast a their hand .
Farmer Bassett , and Eph , the old boy , be `` chorin ' ` round '' outside , for Thanksgiving be at hand , and all must be in order for that time-honored day .
To and fro , from table to hearth , bustle buxom Mrs. Bassett , flush and floury , but busy and blithe a the queen bee of this busy little hive should be .
`` I do like to begin seasonable and have thing to my mind .
Thanksgivin ' dinner ca n't be drive , and it do take a sight of victual to fill all these hungry stomicks , '' say the good woman , a she give a vigorous stir to the great kettle of cider apple-sauce , and cast a glance of housewifely pride at the fine array of pie set forth on the buttery shelf .
`` Only one more day and then it will be time to eat .
I do n't take but one bowl of hasty pudding this morning , so I shall have plenty of room when the nice thing come , '' confide Seth to Sol , a he crack a large hazel-nut as easily a a squirrel .
`` No need of my starvin ' beforehand .
I always have room enough , and I 'd like to have Thanksgiving every day , '' answer Solomon , gloat like a young ogre over the little pig that lay near by , ready for roast .
`` Sakes alive , I do n't , boys !
It 's a marcy it do n't come but once a year .
I should be wear to a thread-paper with all this extra work atop of my winter weavin ' and spinnin ' , '' laugh their mother , a she plunge her plump arm into the long bread-trough and begin to knead the dough a if a famine be at hand .
Tilly , the old girl , a red-cheeked , black-eyed lass of fourteen , be grind briskly at the mortar , for spice be costly , and not a grain must be waste .
Prue keep time with the chopper , and the twin slice away at the apple till their little brown arm ache , for all know how to work , and do so now with a will .
`` I think it 's real fun to have Thanksgiving at home .
I 'm sorry Gran ` ma be sick , so we ca n't go there as usual , but I like to mess ` round here , do n't you , girl ? ''
ask Tilly , pause to take a sniff at the spicy pestle .
`` It will be kind of lonesome with only our own folk . ''
`` I like to see all the cousin and aunt , and have game , and sing , '' cry the twin , who be regular little romp , and could run , swim , coast and shout as well a their brother .
`` I do n't care a mite for all that .
It will be so nice to eat dinner together , warm and comfortable at home , '' say quiet Prue , who love her own cozy nook like a cat .
`` Come , girl , fly ` round and get your chore do , so we can clear away for dinner jest as soon a I clap my bread into the oven , '' call Mrs. Bassett presently , a she round off the last loaf of brown bread which be to fee the hungry mouth that seldom taste any other .
`` Here 's a man comin ' up the hill , lively ! ''
`` Guess it 's Gad Hopkins .
Pa tell him to bring a dezzen orange , if they war n't too high ! ''
shout Sol and Seth , run to the door , while the girl smack their lip at the thought of this rare treat , and Baby throw his apple overboard , a if get ready for a new cargo .
But all be doom to disappointment , for it be not Gad , with the much-desired fruit .
It be a stranger , who throw himself off his horse and hurry up to Mr. Bassett in the yard , with some brief message that make the farmer drop his ax and look so sober that his wife guess at once some bad news have come ; and cry , `` Mother 's wuss !
I know she be ! ''
out run the good woman , forgetful of the flour on her arm and the oven waiting for it most important batch .
The man say old Mr. Chadwick , down to Keene , stop him a he pass , and tell him to tell Mrs. Bassett her mother be failin ' fast , and she 'd better come to-day .
He know no more , and have deliver his errand he ride away , say it look like snow and he must be jog , or he would n't get home till night .
`` We must go right off , Eldad .
Hitch up , and I 'll be ready in less 'n no time , '' say Mrs. Bassett , waste not a minute in tear and lamentation , but pull off her apron a she go in , with her mind in a sad jumble of bread , anxiety , turkey , sorrow , haste , and cider apple-sauce .
A few word tell the story , and the child leave their work to help her get ready , mingle their grief for `` Gran ` ma '' with regret for the lost dinner .
`` I 'm dreadful sorry , dear , but it ca n't be help .
I could n't cook nor eat no way , now , and if that bless woman get good sudden , a she have before , we 'll have cause for thanksgivin ' , and I 'll give you a dinner you wo n't forget in a hurry , '' say Mrs. Bassett , a she tie on her brown silk pumpkin-hood , with a sob for the good old mother who have make it for her .
Not a child complain after that , but run about helpfully , bring moccasin , heat the footstone , and get ready for a long drive , because Gran ` ma live twenty mile away , and there be no railroad in those part to whisk people to and fro like magic .
By the time the old yellow sleigh be at the door , the bread be in the oven , and Mrs. Bassett be wait , with her camlet cloak on , and the baby do up like a small bale of blanket .
`` Now , Eph , you must look after the cattle like a man , and keep up the fire , for there 's a storm brewin ' , and neither the child nor dumb critter must suffer , '' say Mr. Bassett , a he turn up the collar of his rough coat and put on his blue mitten , while the old mare shake her bell a if she prefer a trip to Keene to haul wood all day .
`` Tilly , put extry comfortables on the bed to-night , the wind be so searchin ' up chamber .
Have the baked bean and Injun-puddin ' for dinner , and whatever you do , do n't let the boy git at the mince-pies , or you 'll have them down sick .
I shall come back the minute I can leave Mother .
Pa will come to-morrer , anyway , so keep snug and be good .
I depend on you , my darter ; use your jedgment , and do n't let nothin ' happen while Mother 's away . ''
`` Yes 'm , yes 'm -- good-bye , good-bye ! ''
call the child , a Mrs. Bassett be pack into the sleigh and drive away , leave a stream of direction behind her .
Eph , the sixteen-year-old boy , immediately put on his big boot , assume a sober , responsible manner , and survey his little responsibility with a paternal air , drolly like his father 's .
Tilly tie on her mother 's bunch of key , roll up the sleeve of her homespun gown , and begin to order about the young girl .
They soon forget poor Granny , and find it great fun to keep house all alone , for Mother seldom leave home , but rule her family in the good old-fashioned way .
There be no servant , for the little daughter be Mrs. Bassett 's only maid , and the stout boys help their father , all work happily together with no wage but love ; learn in the best manner the use of the head and hand with which they be to make their own way in the world .
The few flake that cause the farmer to predict bad weather soon increase to a regular snow-storm , with gust of wind , for up among the hill winter come early and linger long .
But the child be busy , gay , and warm in-doors , and never mind the rise gale nor the whirl white storm outside .
Tilly get them a good dinner , and when it be over the two elder girl go to their spinning , for in the kitchen stand the big and little wheel , and basket of wool-rolls , ready to be twist into yarn for the winter 's knitting , and each day bring it stint of work to the daughter , who hop to be as thrifty a their mother .
Eph keep up a glorious fire , and superintend the small boy , who pop corn and whittle boat on the hearth ; while Roxy and Rhody dress corn-cob doll in the settle corner , and Bose , the brindled mastiff , lay on the braided mat , luxuriously warm his old leg .
Thus employ , they make a pretty picture , these rosy boy and girl , in their homespun suit , with the rustic toy or task which most child nowadays would find very poor or tiresome .
Tilly and Prue sang , a they step to and fro , draw out the smoothly twisted thread to the musical hum of the great spinning-wheels .
The little girl chatter like magpie over their doll and the new bed-spread they be plan to make , all white dimity star on a blue calico ground , a a Christmas present to Ma .
The boy roar at Eph 's joke , and have rough and tumble game over Bose , who do n't mind them in the least ; and so the afternoon wear pleasantly away .
At sunset the boy go out to fee the cattle , bring in heap of wood , and lock up for the night , a the lonely farm-house seldom have visitor after dark .
The girl get the simple supper of brown bread and milk , baked apple , and a doughnut all ` round a a treat .
Then they sit before the fire , the sister knitting , the brother with book or game , for Eph love reading , and Sol and Seth never fail to play a few game of Morris with barley corn , on the little board they have make themselves at one corner of the dresser .
`` Read out a piece , '' say Tilly , from Mother 's chair , where she sit in state , finish off the sixth woolen sock she have knit that month .

`` Yes , read that .
I always like to hear about the Lady Matildy I be name for , and Lord Bassett , Pa 's great-great-great-grandpa .
He 's only a farmer now , but it 's nice to know that we be somebody two or three hundred year ago , '' say Tilly , bridling and toss her curly head a she fancy the Lady Matilda might have do .

`` Well , a long time ago , when Charles the First be in prison , Lord Bassett be a true friend to him , '' begin Eph , plunge into his story without delay .
`` The lord have some paper that would have hang a lot of people if the king 's enemy get hold of 'em , so when he hear one day , all of a sudden , that soldier be at the castle-gate to carry him off , he have just time to call his girl to him , and say : ' I may be go to my death , but I wo n't betray my master .
There be no time to burn the paper , and I can not take them with me ; they be hide in the old leathern chair where I sit .
No one know this but you , and you must guard them till I come or send you a safe messenger to take them away .
Promise me to be brave and silent , and I can go without fear . '
You see , he be n't afraid to die , but he be to seem a traitor .
Lady Matildy promise solemnly , and the word be hardly out of her mouth when the men come in , and her father be carry away a prisoner and send off to the Tower .
`` But she do n't cry ; she just call her brother , and sit down in that chair , with her head lean back on those paper , like a queen , and wait while the soldier hunt the house over for 'em : be n't that a smart girl ? ''
cry Tilly , beam with pride , for she be name for this ancestress , and know the story by heart .
`` I reckon she be scar , though , when the men come swearin ' in and ask her if she know anything about it .
The boy do his part then , for he do n't know , and fire up and stand before his sister ; and he say , say he , as bold a a lion : ` If my lord have tell u where the paper be , we would die before we would betray him .
But we be child and know nothing , and it be cowardly of you to try to fright u with oath and drawn sword ! ' ''
As Eph quote from the book , Seth plant himself before Tilly , with the long poker in his hand , say , a he flourish it valiantly : `` Why do n't the boy take his father 's sword and lay about him ?
I would , if any one be ha ` sh to Tilly . ''
`` You bantam !
He be only a bit of a boy , and could n't do anything .
Sit down and hear the rest of it , '' command Tilly , with a pat on the yellow head , and a private resolve that Seth should have the large piece of pie at dinner next day , a reward for his chivalry .
`` Well , the men go off after turn the castle out of window , but they say they should come again ; so faithful Matildy be full of trouble , and hardly dare to leave the room where the chair stand .
All day she sit there , and at night her sleep be so full of fear about it , that she often get up and go to see that all be safe .
The servant think the fright have hurt her wit , and let her be , but Rupert , the boy , stood by her and never be afraid of her queer way .
She be ' a pious maid , ' the book say , and often spend the long evening read the Bible , with her brother by her , all alone in the great room , with no one to help her bear her secret , and no good news of her father .
At last , word come that the king be dead and his friend banish out of England .
Then the poor child be in a sad plight , for they have no mother , and the servant all run away , leave only one faithful old man to help them . ''
`` But the father do come ? ''
cry Roxy , eagerly .
`` You 'll see , '' continue Eph , half telling , half reading .
`` Matilda be sure he would , so she sit on in the big chair , guard the paper , and no one could get her away , till one day a man come with her father 's ring and tell her to give up the secret .
She know the ring , but would not tell until she have ask many question , so a to be very sure , and while the man answer all about her father and the king , she look at him sharply .
Then she stand up and say , in a tremble , for there be something strange about the man : ` Sir , I doubt you in spite of the ring , and I will not answer till you pull off the false beard you wear , that I may see your face and know if you be my father 's friend or foe . '
Off come the disguise , and Matilda find it be my lord himself , come to take them with him out of England .
He be very proud of that faithful girl , I guess , for the old chair still stand in the castle , and the name keep in the family , Pa say , even over here , where some of the Bassetts come along with the Pilgrims . ''
`` Our Tilly would have be a brave , I know , and she look like the old picter down to Grandma 's , do n't she , Eph ? ''

The young folk delightedly troop in to destroy the order of that prim apartment with housekeep under the black horse-hair sofa , `` horseback rider '' on the arm of the best rocking-chair , and an Indian war-dance all over the well-waxed furniture .

Now Tilly and Prue be in their glory , and as soon a the breakfast thing be out of the way , they prepare for a grand cooking-time .

She forget to tie down his leg and wing , but she set him by till his hour come , well satisfied with her work .

`` I be mad about the mistake , but do n't care enough to cry .
I 'm laugh to think how Gad fool Eph and I find him out .
I think Mose and Amos would have die over it when I tell them , it be so funny , '' explain Tilly , when she get her breath .
`` I be so scared that when the first orange hit me , I think it be a bullet , and scrabble into the cradle as fast a I could .
It be real mean to frighten the little one so , '' laugh Prue , a Tilly give a growl .
Here a smart rap on the wall of the next room cause a sudden lull in the fun , and Mrs. Bassett 's voice be hear , say warningly , `` Girls , go to sleep immediate , or you 'll wake the baby . ''
`` Yes 'm , '' answer two meek voice , and after a few irrepressible giggle , silence reign , break only by an occasional snore from the boy , or the soft scurry of mouse in the buttery , take their part in this old-fashioned Thanksgiving .
II .
HOW IT ALL HAPPENED .
It be a small room , with nothing in it but a bed , two chair , and a big chest .
A few little gown hung on the wall , and the only picture be the wintry sky , sparkle with star , frame by the uncurtained window .
But the moon , pause to peep , saw something pretty and hear something pleasant .
Two head in little round nightcap lay on one pillow , two pair of wide-awake blue eye star up at the light , and two tongue be go like mill clapper .
`` I 'm so glad we get our shirt do in time !
It seem a if we never should , and I do n't think six cent be half enough for a great red flannel thing with four button-holes -- do you ? ''
say one little voice , rather wearily .
`` No ; but then we each make four , and fifty cent be a good deal of money .
Are you sorry we do n't keep our quarter for ourselves ? ''
ask the other voice , with an under-tone of regret in it .
`` Yes , I be , till I think how please the child will be with our tree , for they do n't expect anything , and will be so surprised .
I wish we have more toy to put on it , for it look so small and mean with only three or four thing . ''
`` It wo n't hold any more , so I would n't worry about it .
The toy be very red and yellow , and I guess the baby wo n't know how cheap they be , but like them as much a if they cost heap of money . ''
This be a cheery voice , and a it speak the four blue eye turn toward the chest under the window , and the kind moon do her best to light up the tiny tree stand there .
A very pitiful little tree it be -- only a branch of hemlock in an old flower-pot , prop up with bit of coal , and hung with a few penny toy earn by the patient finger of the elder sister , that the little one should not be disappoint .
But in spite of the magical moonlight the broken branch , with it scanty supply of fruit , look pathetically poor , and one pair of eye fill slowly with tear , while the other pair lose their happy look , a if a cloud have come over the sunshine .
`` Are you cry , Dolly ? ''
`` Not much , Polly . ''
`` What make you , dear ? ''
`` I do n't know how poor we be till I saw the tree , and then I could n't help it , '' sob the elder sister , for at twelve she already know something of the care of poverty , and miss the happiness that seem to vanish out of all their life when father die .
`` It 's dreadful !
I never think we 'd have to earn our tree , and only be able to get a broken branch , after all , with nothing on it but three stick of candy , two squeak dog , a red cow , and an ugly bird with one feather in it tail ; '' and overcome by a sudden sense of destitution , Polly sob even more despairingly than Dolly .
`` Hush , dear ; we must cry softly , or mother will hear , and come up , and then we shall have to tell .
You know we say we would n't seem to mind not have any Christmas , she felt so sorry about it . ''
`` I must cry , but I 'll be quiet . ''
So the two head go under the pillow for a few minute , and not a sound betray them a the little sister cry softly in one another 's arm , l mother should discover that they be no long careless child , but brave young creature try to bear their share of the burden cheerfully .
When the shower be over , the face come out shin like rose after rain , and the voice go on again a before .
`` Do n't you wish there really be a Santa Claus , who know what we want , and would come and put two silver half-dollars in our stocking , so we could go and see Puss in Boots at the Museum to-morrow afternoon ? ''
`` Yes , indeed ; but we do n't hang up any stocking , you know , because mother have nothing to put in them .
It do seem a if rich people might think of poor people now and then .
Such little bit of thing would make u happy , and it could n't be much trouble to take two small girl to the play , and give them candy now and then . '' ''
I shall when I 'm rich , like Mr. Chrome and Miss Kent .
I shall go round every Christmas with a big basket of goody , and give all the poor child some . ''
`` P ` r ` aps if we sew ever so many flannel shirt we may be rich by-and-by .
I should give mother a new bonnet first of all , for I hear Miss Kent say no lady would wear such a shabby one .
Mrs. Smith say fine bonnet do n't make real lady .
I like her best , but I do want a locket like Miss Kent 's . ''
`` I should give mother some new rubber , and then I should buy a white apron , with frill like Miss Kent 's , and bring home nice bunch of grape and good thing to eat , a Mr. Chrome do .
I often smell them , but he never give me any ; he only say , ` Hullo , chick ! '
and I 'd rather have orange any time . ''
`` It will take u a long while to get rich , I 'm afraid .
It make me tired to think of it .
I guess we 'd well go to sleep now , dear . ''
`` Good-night , Dolly . ''
`` Good-night , Polly . ''
Two soft kiss be heard , a nestle sound follow , and presently the little sister lay fast asleep cheek against cheek , on the pillow wet with their tear , never dream what be go to happen to them to-morrow .
Now Miss Kent 's room be next to theirs , and a she sit sew she could hear the child 's talk , for they soon forget to whisper .
At first she smile , then she look sober , and when the prattle cease she say to herself , a she glance about her pleasant chamber : `` Poor little thing !
they think I 'm rich , and envy me , when I 'm only a milliner earn my living .
I ought to have take more notice of them , for their mother have a hard time , I fancy , but never complains .
I 'm sorry they hear what I say , and if I know how to do it without offend her , I 'd trim a nice bonnet for a Christmas gift , for she be a lady , in spite of her old clothes .
I can give the child some of the thing they want anyhow , and I will .
The idea of those mite make a fortune out of shirt at six cent apiece ! ''
Miss Kent laugh at the innocent delusion , but sympathize with her little neighbor , for she know all about hard time .
She have good wage now , but spend them on herself , and like to be fine rather than neat .
Still , she be a good-hearted girl , and what she have overheard set her to think soberly , then to act kindly , a we shall see .
`` If I have n't spend all my money on my dress for the party to-morrow night , I 'd give each of them a half-dollar .
As I can not , I 'll hunt up the other thing they want , for it 's a shame they should n't have a bit of Christmas , when they try so hard to please the little one . ''
As she speak she stir about her room , and soon have a white apron , an old carnelian heart on a fresh blue ribbon , and two paper of bonbon ready .
As no stocking be hang up , she lay a clean towel on the floor before the door , and spread forth the small gift to look their best .
Miss Kent be so busy that she do not hear a step come quietly up stair , and Mr. Chrome , the artist , peep at her through the baluster , wonder what she be about .
He soon saw , and watch her with pleasure , think that she never look pretty than now .
Presently she catch him at it , and hasten to explain , tell what she have hear , and how she be try to atone for her past neglect of these young neighbor .
Then she say good-night , and both go into their room , she to sleep happily , and he to smoke a usual .
But his eye keep turn to some of the `` nice little bundle '' that lay on his table , a if the story he have hear suggest how he might follow Miss Kent 's example .
I rather think he would not have disturb himself if he have not hear the story tell in such a soft voice , with a pair of bright eye full of pity look into his , for little girl be not particularly interesting to him , and he be usually too tired to notice the industrious creature toil up and down stair on various errand , or sew at the long red seam .
Now that he know something of their small trouble , he felt a if it would please Miss Kent , and be a good joke , to do his share of the pretty work she have begin .
So presently he jump up , and , open his parcel , take out two orange and two bunch of grape , then he look up two silver half-dollars , and steal into the hall , lay the fruit upon the towel , and the money atop of the orange .
This addition improve the display very much , and Mr. Chrome be steal back , well pleased , when his eye fell on Miss Kent 's door , and he say to himself , `` She too shall have a little surprise , for she be a dear , kind-hearted soul . ''
In his room be a prettily paint plate , and this he fill with green and purple grape , tuck a sentimental note underneath , and leave it on her threshold , creep away as stealthily a a burglar .
The house be very quiet when Mrs. Smith , the landlady , come up to turn off the gas .
`` Well , upon my word , here 's fine doings , to be sure ! ''
she say , when she saw the state of the upper hall .
`` Now I would n't have think it of Miss Kent , she be such a giddy girl , nor of Mr. Chrome , he be so busy with his own affair .
I mean to give those child each a cake to-morrow , they be such good little thing .
I 'll run down and get them now , a my contribution to this fine set out . ''
Away trot Mrs. Smith to her pantry , and pick out a couple of tempt cake , shape like heart and full of plum .
There be a goodly array of pie on the shelf , and she take two of them , say , a she climb the stair again , `` They remember the child , so I 'll remember them , and have my share of the fun . ''
So up go the pie , for Mrs. Smith have not much to give , and her spirit be generous , though her pastry be not of the best .
It look very droll to see pie sit about on the threshold of closed door , but the cake be quite elegant , and fill up the corner of the towel handsomely , for the apron lay in the middle , with the orange right and leave , like two sentinel in yellow uniform .
It be very late when the flicker of a candle come up stair , and a pale lady , with a sweet sad face , appear , bring a pair of red and a pair of blue mitten for her Dolly and Polly .
