Poor Mrs. Blake do have a hard time , for she stand all day in a great store that she might earn bread for the poor child who staid at home and take care of one another .
Her heart be very heavy that night , because it be the first Christmas she have ever know without gift and festivity of some sort .
But Petkin , the young child , have be ill , time be very hard , the little mouth gap for food like the bill of hungry bird , and there be no tender mate to help fill them .
If any elf have be hover about the dingy hall just then , they would have see the mother 's tired face brighten beautifully when she discover the gift , and find that her little girl have be so kindly remember .
Something more brilliant than the mock diamond in Miss Kent 's best earring fell and glitter on the dusty floor a Mrs. Blake add the mitten to the other thing , and go to her lonely room again , smile a she think how she could thank them all in a sweet and simple way .
Her window be full of flower , for the delicate taste of the poor lady find great comfort in their beauty .
`` I have nothing else to give , and these will show how grateful I be , '' she say , a she rejoice that the scarlet geranium be so full of gay cluster , the white chrysanthemum star be all out , and the pink roses at their lovely .
They sleep now , dream of a sunny morrow a they sit safely shelter from the bitter cold .
But that night be their last , for a gentle hand cut them all , and soon three pretty nosegay stand in a glass , wait for dawn , to be lay at three door , with a few grateful word which would surprise and delight the receiver , for flower be rare in those hard-working life , and kind deed often come back to the giver in fairer shape than they go .
Now one would think that there have be gift enough , and no more could possibly arrive , since all have add his or her mite except Betsey , the maid , who be off on a holiday , and the baby fast asleep in their trundle-bed , with nothing to give but love and kiss .
Nobody dream that the old cat would take it into her head that her kitten be in danger , because Mrs. Smith have say she think they be nearly old enough to be give away .
But she must have understood , for when all be dark and still , the anxious mother go pat up stair to the child 's door , mean to hide her baby under their bed , sure they would save them from destruction .
Mrs. Blake have shut the door , however , so poor Puss be disappoint ; but find a soft , clean spot among a variety of curious article , she lay her kit there , and keep them warm all night , with her head pillow on the blue mitten .
In the cold morning Dolly and Polly get up and scramble into their clothes , not with joyful haste to see what their stocking hold , for they have none , but because they have the little one to dress while mother get the breakfast .
Dolly open the door , and start back with a cry of astonishment at the lovely spectacle before her .
The other people have take in their gift , so nothing destroy the magnificent effect of the treasure so curiously collect in the night .
Puss have leave her kit asleep , and go down to get her own breakfast , and there , in the middle of the ruffled apron , a if in a dainty cradle , lay the two Maltese darling , with white bib and boot on , and white tip to the tiny tail curl round their little nose in the sweet way .
Polly and Dolly could only clasp their hand and look in rapturous silence for a minute ; then they go down on their knee and revel in the unexpected richness before them .
`` I do believe there be a Santa Claus , and that he hear u , for here be everything we want , '' say Dolly , hold the carnelian heart in one hand and the plummy one in the other .
`` It must have be some kind of a fairy , for we do n't mention kitten , but we want one , and here be two darling , '' cry Polly , almost purr with delight a the downy bunch unroll and gap till their bit of pink tongue be visible .
`` Mrs. Smith be one fairy , I guess , and Miss Kent be another , for that be her apron .
I should n't wonder if Mr. Chrome give u the orange and the money : men always have lot , and his name be on this bit of paper , '' say Dolly .
`` Oh , I 'm so glad !
Now we shall have a Christmas like other people , and I 'll never say again that rich folk do n't remember poor folk .
Come and show all our treasure to mother and the baby ; they must have some , '' answer Polly , feel that the world be all right , and life not half as hard a she think it last night .
Shrieks of delight greet the sister , and all that morning there be joy and feasting in Mrs. Blake 's room , and in the afternoon Dolly and Polly go to the Museum , and actually saw Puss in Boots ; for their mother insist on their go , have discover how the hard-earned quarter have be spend .
This be such unhoped-for bliss that they could hardly believe it , and keep smile at one another so brightly that people wonder who the happy little girl in shabby cloak could be who clap their new mitten so heartily , and laugh till it be well than music to hear them .
This be a very remarkable Christmas-day , and they long remember it ; for while they be absorb in the fortune of the Marquis of Carabas and the funny cat , who tuck his tail in his belt , wash his face so awkwardly , and do n't know how to purr , strange thing be happen at home , and more surprise be in store for our little friend .
You see , when people once begin to do kindness , it be so easy and pleasant they find it hard to leave off ; and sometimes it beautify them so that they find they love one another very much -- a Mr. Chrome and Miss Kent do , though we have nothing to do with that except to tell how they make the poor little tree grow and blossom .
They be very jolly at dinner , and talk a good deal about the Blakes , who eat in their own room .
Miss Kent tell what the child say , and it touch the soft spot in all their heart to hear about the red shirt , though they laugh at Polly 's lament over the bird with only one feather in it tail .
`` I 'd give them a good tree if I have any place to put it , and know how to trim it up , '' say Mr. Chrome , with a sudden burst of generosity , which so pleased Miss Kent that her eye shine like Christmas candle .
`` Put it in the back parlor .
All the Browns be away for a week , and we 'll help you trim it -- wo n't we , my dear ? ''
cried Mrs. Smith , warmly ; for she saw that he be in a sociable mood , and think it a pity that the Blakes should not profit by it .
`` Yes , indeed ; I should like it of all thing , and it need n't cost much , for I have some skill in trimming , a you know . ''
And Miss Kent look so gay and pretty a she speak that Mr. Chrome make up his mind that millinery must be a delightful occupation .
`` Come on then , lady , and we 'll have a little frolic .
I 'm a lonely old bachelor , with nowhere to go to-day , and I 'd like some fun . ''
They have it , I assure you ; for they all fell to work as busy a bee , fly and buzz about with much laughter a they work their pleasant miracle .
Mr. Chrome act more like the father of a large family than a crusty bachelor , Miss Kent 's skillful finger fly a they never do before , and Mrs. Smith trot up and down as briskly a if she be sixteen instead of be a stout old woman of sixty .
The child be so full of the play , and tell all about it , that they forget their tree till after supper ; but when they go to look for it they find it go , and in it place a great paper hand with one finger point down stair , and on it these mysterious word in red ink : `` Look in the Browns ' back parlor ! ''
At the door of that interest apartment they find their mother with Will and Petkin , for another hand have suddenly appear to them point up .
The door fly open quite a if it be a fairy play , and they go in to find a pretty tree plant in a red box on the centre table , light with candle , hung with gilded nut , red apple , gay bonbon , and a gift for each .
Mr. Chrome be hide behind one folding-door , and fat Mrs. Smith squeeze behind the other , and they both think it a great improvement upon the old-fashioned Santa Claus to have Miss Kent , in the white dress she make for the party , with Mrs. Blake 's rose in her hair , step forward a the child gaze in silent rapture , and with a few sweet word welcome them to the little surprise their friend have make .
There be many Christmas tree in the city that night , but none which give such hearty pleasure a the one which so magically take the place of the broken branch and it few poor toy .
They be all there , however , and Dolly and Polly be immensely pleased to see that of all her gift Petkin choose the forlorn bird to carry to bed with her , the one yellow feather be just to her taste .
Mrs. Blake put on her neat bonnet , and be so gratified that Miss Kent think it the most successful one she ever trim .
She be well pay for it by the thanks of one neighbor and the admiration of another ; for when she go to her party Mr. Chrome go with her , and say something on the way which make her heart dance more lightly than her foot that night .
Good Mrs. Smith felt that her house have cover itself with glory by this event , and Dolly and Polly declare that it be the most perfect and delightful surprise party ever see .
It be all over by nine o'clock , and with good-night kiss for every one the little girl climb up to bed laden with treasure and too happy for many word .
But a they tie their round cap Dolly say , thoughtfully : `` On the whole , I think it 's rather nice to be poor when people be kind to you . ''
`` Well , I 'd rather be rich ; but if I ca n't be , it be very good fun to have Christmas tree like this one , '' answer truthful Polly , never guess that they have plant the seed from which the little pine-tree grow so quickly and beautifully .
When the moon come to look in at the window on her nightly round , two smile face lay on the pillow , which be no longer wet with tear , but rather knobby with the mine of rich hidden underneath , -- first fruit of the neighborly friendship which flourish in that house until another and a merry Christmas come .
III .
THE DOLLS ' JOURNEY FROM MINNESOTA TO MAINE .
Mr . Plum live in St. Paul , Minnesota , U.S.A. .
There be six little Plums , all girl , vary in age from fourteen to seven , and name Kate , Lucy , Susy , Lizzy , Marjory and Maggie .
There be no mamma , but Mrs. Gibbs , the housekeeper , be a kind old soul , and papa do everything he could to make the small daughter good and happy .
One stormy Saturday afternoon the child be all together in the school-room , and papa busy at his desk in the library , with the door open because he like to hear the pleasant voice and catch glimpse of the droll play that go on there .
Kate lay on the sofa read `` The Daisy Chain '' for the fourth time .
Susy , Lucy and Lizzie be have a select tea party in their own recess , the entrance to which be barricade with chair to keep out the `` baby , '' a they call the little one , who be much offend at be exclude and sit up in the cushioned window-seat pensively watch the rain .
`` If it have only wait till to-morrow we should have have time for our journey ; now we ca n't go till next Saturday .
Flora be so disappointed she would cry if I have not teach her to behave , '' say Maggie with a sigh , a she survey the doll on her knee in it new summer suit .
`` So be Dora .
Just see how sweet she look with her hat and cape on and her travelling-bag all ready .
Could n't we play travel in the house ?
It be such a pity to wait when the child be in such a hurry to go , '' answer Marjory , settle the tiny bag that hold Dora 's nightcap and gown as well a the morsel of cake that be to serve for her lunch .
`` No , '' say Maggie decidedly , `` we ca n't do it , because there be no room for carriage , and boat , and railroad , and hotel , and accident .
It be a long journey from Minnesota to Maine , and we could n't get it all into one room I 'm sure . ''
`` I do n't think papa would mind our come into the library , if we do n't ring the car bell very loud or scream much when the accident happen , '' say Marjory , who hat to give up the plan they have be cherish all the week .
`` What be it , little one ?
Come and tell me what be the matter , '' call Mr . Plum , hear his name and the magic word `` railroad , '' for he be the president of one and have his hand full just then .
Down jump the little girl and run to perch on either arm of his chair , pour out their small tribulation as freely a if he have be the most sympathizing of mother .
`` We plan to take a long , long journey round the garden with our doll to-day , and play go to Maine and see Aunt Maria .
You know she ask u , and we look out the way on the map and get all ready , and now it rain and we be dreadfully disappointed , '' say Maggie , while Marjory sigh a she look at the red D. work on the inch square travelling-bag .
`` As you ca n't go , why not send the doll to make aunty a visit , and she will send them back when they get homesick , '' propose Mr . Plum , smile , a if a sudden idea have pop into his head .
`` Really ? ''
cry Maggie .
`` How could we ? ''
ask Marjory .
`` They could go and come by mail , and tell you all about their adventure when they get back , '' say papa .
Both child be speechless for a moment , then a the full splendor of this proposition dawn upon them they clap their hand , cry eagerly : `` We will !
we will !
Let 's do it at once . ''
`` What ?
where ?
who ? ''
ask Susy , Lucy and Lizzie , forget their tea party to run and see what be go on .
They be tell , and in their turn exclaim so loudly that Kate come to join in the fun .
After a great deal of talk and laughing , the doll be prepare for the long journey .
They be common wooden-headed dolly , a hand long , with stuffed body and stout leg ornament with very small foot in red and blue boot .
Dora be a blonde and Flora a brunette , otherwise they be just alike and nearly new .
Usually when people go travel they put on their hat and cloak , but these pilgrim , by papa 's advice , leave all encumbrance behind them , for they be to travel in a peculiar way , and blue gingham dress be choose for the expedition .
`` It be possible that they may never come back .
Accidents will happen you know .
Are you prepare for that ? ''
ask Mr . Plum , pause with the brown paper spread out before him .
`` I be , '' answer Maggie firmly , a she lay Flora on the table , her black eye star a if rather alarm at this sudden start .
Marjory hesitate a moment , clasp Dora to her bosom with a face full of maternal anxiety .
But Susy , Lucy and Lizzie cry : `` Let her go , do let her go , and if she be lose papa will give you a new doll . ''
`` Good-by , my darling dear .
Have a splendid time , and be sure you come back to me , '' whisper Marjory , with a tender farewell kiss a she give up her child .
All stand watch silently while papa tie the doll back to back with the ribbon Kate pull from her neck , then fold them carefully in strong brown paper , leave their head out that they might see the world a they go along .
Being carefully fasten up with several turn of cord , Mr . Plum direct the precious parcel to `` Miss Maria Plum , Portland , Maine .
With care . ''
Then it be weigh , stamp , and pronounce ready for the post .
`` I shall write and tell aunty they be come , because she will want to be prepare for such distinguished visitor , '' say papa , take up his pen with a glance at the six excite little face round him .
Silence reign while the letter be write , and a he seal it up Mr . Plum say solemnly , with his hand on the parcel : `` For the last time , shall they go ? ''
`` Yes ! ''
answer the Spartan mother with one voice , while the other sister dance round them , and Kate pat the curly head approvingly .
`` Going , go , go ! ''
answered papa a he whisk on his coat and hat , and slam the door behind him .
The child cluster at the window to see him set out on this momentous errand , and he often look back wave his umbrella at them , till he vanish round the corner , with a reassure pat on the pocket out of which dear Do and Flo pop their head for a last look at their sweet home .
`` Now let u take out poor old Lucinda and Rose Augusta to play with .
I know their feeling be hurt at our leave them for the new doll , '' say Maggie , rummage in the baby-house , whither Margery soon follow her to reinstate the old darling in the place of the departed new one .
`` Safely off , '' report Mr . Plum , when he come into tea , `` and we may expect to hear from them in a week or two .
Parcels go more slowly than letter , and this be Aunty 's busy season , so wait patiently and see what will happen . ''
`` We will , '' say the little girl ; and they do , but week after week go by and nothing be hear of the wanderer .
We , however , can follow them and learn much that their anxious mother never know .
As soon a Flora and Dora recover from the bewilderment occasion by the confusion of the post office , they find themselves in one of the many leathern mail bag rumble Eastward .
As it be perfectly dark they could not see their companion , so listen to the whispering and rustling that go on about them .
The newspaper all talk politics , and some of them use such bad language that the doll would have cover their ear , if their hand have not be tie down .
The letter be good behave and more interesting , for they tell one another the news they carry , because nothing be private in America , and even gum envelope can not keep gossip from leak out .
`` It be very interesting , but I should enjoy it more if I be not grind my nose against the rough side of this leather bag , '' whisper Dora , who lay undermost just then .
`` So should I , if a heavy book be not pinch my toe .
I 've try to kick it away , but it wo n't stir , and keep drone on about report and tariff and such dull thing , '' answer Flora , with a groan .
`` Do you like travel ? ''
ask Dora , presently , when the letter and paper fell asleep , lull by the motion of the car .
`` Not yet , but I shall when I can look about me .
This bundle near by say the mail be often sort in the car , and in that way we shall see something of the world , I hope , '' answer Flora , cheer up , for , like her mamma , she be of an enquire turn .
The doll take a nap of some hour , and be rouse by a general tumble out on a long shelf , where many other parcel lay , and lively men send letter and paper fly here and there a if a whirlwind be blow .
A long box lay beside the doll who stand nearly erect lean against a pile of paper .
Several hole be cut in the lid , and out of one of them be thrust a little black nose , a if try to get air .
`` Dear me !
what can be in it ? ''
say Flora , who be nearest .
`` I 'm a poor little alligator , go to a boy in Chicago , if you please , and I want my mother , '' sob a voice from the box , and there be a rap on the lid a of an agitated tail .
`` Mercy on u !
I hope we shall not have to travel with the monster , '' whisper Dora , try to see over her shoulder .
`` I 'm not afraid .
He ca n't be very dreadful , for the box be not any long than we be .
Natural history be very useful ; I 've hear mamma say so , and I shall talk with him while we rest here , '' answer Flo , nod toward the eye which now take the place of the nose .
So the little alligator tell her something of his home on the bank of a great river , where he be just learn to play happily with his brother and sister , when he be catch and send away to pine in captivity .
The doll comfort him as well a they could , and a pair of baby 's shoe travel in an envelope sympathize with him , while a shabby bundle direct to `` Michael Dolan , at Mrs. Judy Quin 's , next door to Mr. Pat Murphy , Boston , North street , '' tell them to `` Whisht and slape quite till they come forninst the place . ''
`` Such low people ! ''
whisper Do to Flo , and both stand primly silent till they be tumble into another mail bag , and go rattle on again with a new set of companion .
`` I hope that poor baby will go safely and the boy be good to him , '' say Flora , for the little alligator go with the live stock in some other way .
`` Thank goodness he do n't go with u !
I shall dream about that black nose and wink eye , I 'm sure .
The danger of travel be great , but we be safe and comfortable now , I think , '' and Dora settle down in a cozy corner of the bag , wonder when they should reach Chicago .
`` I like adventure and hope we shall have some , '' answer Flora , briskly , little dream how soon her wish be to be grant .
A few hour later there come a bump , a crash , a cry , and then all the mail bag roll one over the other with the car down an embankment into a river .
`` Now we be dead ! ''
shriek the poor doll , cling together a they hear the splash of water , the shouting of men , the splintering of wood , and the hiss of steam .
`` Do n't be frighten , lady , mail bag be always look after , '' say a large envelope with an official seal and the name of a Senator on it .
`` Any bone break , dear madam ? ''
ask a jaunty pink letter , with a scent of musk about it , evidently a love-letter .
`` I think one foot be hurt , and my clothes be drip , '' sigh Dora , faintly .
`` Water wo n't hurt calico , '' call out a magazine full of fashion plate , add dolefully , a it gay color begin to run , `` I shall be in a nice mess if I ever get out of this .
People will wear odd fashion if they follow me this time . ''
`` Hope they will telegraph news of this accident in time for the evening paper , '' say a dingy sheet call the `` Barahoo Thunderbolt , '' a it lay atop of the heap in it yellow wrapper .
`` Be calm , my friend , and wait with fortitude for death or deliverance , a I do . ''
With which philosophic remark `` The St. Louis Cosmos '' fold the page which for the first time since the paper be start , be not dry .
Here the water rise over the topmost letter and a moist silence prevail till a sudden jerk fish up the bag , and before the doll could recover their wit they be spread out on the floor of a mail car to dry , while several busy men sort and save such paper and letter a still hold together .
`` Now we shall see something , '' say Flora , feel the warm air blow over her a they spin along , for a slight accident like this do not delay the energetic Westerners a moment long than absolutely necessary .
`` I ca n't see you , dear , but I hope you look good than I do , for the yellow of my hair have wash into my eye and the red of my cheek be quite go , I 'm sure , '' answer Dora , a her wet dress flop in the breeze and the broken foot stick up show her that her blue boot be ruin .
`` I do n't care a bit how I look .
It 's great fun now we be safe .
Pop up your head and see the wide prairie fly past .
I do hope that poor baby get away and swam home to his mother .
The upset into the river be quite to his taste , I fancy , '' say Flora , who be much excite by her adventure and eager for more .
Presently one of the men set the doll up in the corner of a window to dry , and there they stand view the fine landscape with one eye while the other watch the scene of devastation within .
Everything be in great confusion after the accident , so it be not strange that the doll be not miss when they slowly slid low and low till a sudden lurch of the car send them out of the window to roll into a green field where cow be feed and child pick strawberry .
`` This be the end of u !
Here we shall lie and mould forget by everybody , '' say Dora , who always take a tragical view of thing .
`` Not a bit of it !
I see cow eat toward u and they may give u a lift .
I 've hear of their tossing people up , though I do n't know just how it 's do .
If they do n't , we be in the path and some of those child be sure to find u , '' answer Flora cheerfully , though she stand on her head with a bunch of burr prick her nose .
She be right .
A bright-eyed little German girl presently come trot along the path with a great basket full of berry on her head arrange in pretty pottle ready for the market .
Seeing the red cow sniff at a brown paper parcel she drive her away , pick it up and peep in at the open end .
The sight of two doll in such a place make her feel a if fairy have drop them there for her .
She could not read the direction and hurry home to show her treasure to her brother and sister of whom there be eight .
`` What will become of u now ! ''
exclaim Dora , a eager hand slip them out of the wrapper and smooth their damp skirt in a room that seem swarm with boy and girl of all size .
`` Do n't worry , we shall get on nicely , I 'm sure , and learn German of these young person .
It be a great relief to be able to stretch one 's limb and stand up , be n't it ? ''
answer Flora , undismayed by anything that have happen a yet .
`` Yes , dear , I love you but I be tire of be tie to you all day .
I hope we shall live through this noise and get a little rest , but I give up the idea of ever see Portland , '' answer Dora , star with all her blue eye at the display of musical instrument about the room , and long to stop her ear , for several of the child be play on the violin , flute , horn or harp .
They be street musician , and even the baby seem to be get ready to take part in the concert , for he sit on the floor beside an immense bass horn taller than himself , with his rosy lip at the mouth piece and his cheek puff out in vain attempt to make a `` boom !
boom ! ''
a brother Fritz do .
Flora be delight , and give skip on her red boot in time to the lively tooting of the boy , while the girl gaze at the lovely doll and jabber away with their yellow braid quiver with excitement .
The wrapper be lay aside till a neighbor who read English come in to translate it .
Meantime they enjoy the new toy immensely , and even despondent Dora be cheer up by the admiration she receive ; while they in their turn be deeply interested in the pretty doll ' furniture some of the child make .
Beds , table and chair cover the long bench , and round it sit the neat-handed little maiden glue , tack and trim , while they sing and chat at their work a busy and happy a a hive of bee .
All day the boy go about the street play , and in the evening troop off to the beer garden to play again , for they live in Chicago , and the doll have get so far on their way to Aunt Maria , a they soon discover .
For nearly two month they live happily with Minna , Gretchen and Nanerl , then they set out on their travel again , and this be the way it happen .
A little girl come to order a set of furniture for her new baby-house , and see two shabby doll repose in a fine bed she ask about them .
Her mamma spoke German so Minna tell how they be find , and show the old wrapper , say that they always mean to send the doll on their way but grow so fond of them they keep put it off .
`` I be go as far a New York very soon and will take them along if you like , for I think little Miss Maria Plum must have be expect her doll all this time .
Shall I ? ''
ask the mamma , a she read the address and saw the dash under `` With care , '' a if the dolly be of great importance to some one .
`` Ja , ja , '' answer Minna , glad to oblige a lady who buy two whole set of their best furniture and paid for it at once .
So again the doll be put in their brown paper cover and send away with farewell kiss .
`` This now be genteel and just suit me , '' say Dora , a they drive along with little Clara to the handsome house where she be stay .
`` I have a feeling that she be a spoilt child , and we shall not be a happy with her a with the dear Poppleheimers .
We shall see , '' answer Flora , wisely , for Clara have soon toss the doll into a corner and be fret because mamma would not buy her the big horn to blow on .
The party start for New York in a day or two , and to the delight of Flo and Do they be leave out of the trunk for Clara to play with on the way , her own waxen Blanche Marie Annabel be too delicate to be use .
`` Oh my patience , this be bad than tumble about in a mail-bag , '' groan Dora , after hour of great suffering , for Clara treat the poor doll a if they have no feeling .
She amuse herself with knock their head together , shut them in the window with their poor leg hang out , swing them by one arm , and draw line with a pencil all over their face till they look a if tattoo by savage .
Even brave Flora be wear out and long for rest , find her only comfort in say , `` I tell you so , '' when Clara bang them about , or drop them on the dusty floor to be trample on by pass foot .
There they be leave , and would have be sweep away if a little dog have not find them a the passenger be leave the car and carry them after his master , trot soberly along with the bundle in his mouth , for fortunately Clara have put them into the paper before she leave them , so they be still together in the trial of the journey .
`` Hullo , Jip , what have you get ? ''
ask the young man a the little dog jump up on the carriage seat and lay his load on his master 's knee , pant and wag his tail a if he have do something to be praise for .
`` Dolls , I declare !
What can a bachelor do with the poor thing ?
Wonder who Maria Plum be ?
Midge will like a look at them before we send them along ; '' and into the young man 's pocket they go , tremble with fear of the dog , but very grateful for be rescue from destruction .
Jip keep his eye on them , and give an occasional poke with his cold nose to be sure they be there a they drive through the bustle street of New York to a great house with an inscription over the door .
`` I do hope Midge will be a nicer girl than Clara .
Children ought to be teach to be kind to dumb doll as well a dumb animal , '' say Dora , a the young man run up the step and hurry along a wide hall .
`` I almost wish we be at home with our own kind little mother , '' begin Flo , for even her spirit be depress by bad treatment , but just then a door open and she cry out in amazement , `` Bless my heart , this man have more child than even Mr. Poppleheimer ! ''
She might well think so , for all down both side of the long room stand little white bed with a small pale face on every pillow .
All the eye that be open brighten when Jip and his master come in , and several thin hand be outstretched to meet them .
`` I 've be good , Doctor , let me pat him first , '' cry one childish voice .
`` Did you bring me a flower , please ? ''
ask another feeble one .
`` I know he 's get something nice for u , I see a bundle in his pocket , '' and a little fellow who sit up among his pillow give a joyful cough a he could not shout .
`` Two dolly for Midge to play with .
Jip find them , but I think the little girl they be go to will lend them for a few day .
We shall not need them long I 'm afraid , '' add the young man to a rosy faced nurse who come along with a bottle in her hand .
`` Dear no , the poor child be very low to-day .
But she will love to look at the baby if she be n't strong enough to hold 'em , '' say the woman , lead the way to a corner where the palest of all the pale face lay smile on the pillow , and the thinnest of the thin hand be feebly put up to greet the Doctor .
`` So nice ! ''
she whisper when the doll be laid beside her , while Jip proudly beat his tail on the floor to let her know that she owe the welcome gift to him .
For an hour Flo and Do lay on the arm of poor Midge who never move except to touch them now and then with a tender little finger , or to kiss them softly , say , `` Dear baby , it be very nice not to be all alone .
Are you comfy , darling ? ''
till she fell asleep still smile .
`` Sister , do you think this can be the Heaven we hear people talk about ?
It be so still and white , and may be these child be angel , '' whisper Dora , look at the sweet face turn toward her with the long lash lie on the colorless cheek , and the arm outstretched like wing .
`` No , dear , it be a hospital , I hear that man say so , and those be sick child come to be cure .
It be a sweet place , I think , and this child much nicer than that horrid Clara , '' answer Flo , who be quicker to hear , see and understand what go on than Dora .
`` I love to lie here safe and warm , but there do n't seem to be much breath to rock me , '' say Do , who lay near the little bosom that very slowly rise and fell with the feeble flutter of the heart below .
`` Hush , we may disturb her , '' and lively Flo control her curiosity , content herself with look at the other child and listen to their quiet voice , for pain seem to have hush them all .
For a week the doll lay in Midge 's bed , and though their breast be full of saw-dust and their head be only wood , the sweet patience of the little creature seem to waken something like a heart in them , and set them think , for doll do n't live in vain , I be firmly persuade .
All day she tend them till the small hand could no longer hold them , and through the weary night she try to murmur bit of lullaby lest the dolly would not be able to sleep because of the cry or the moan some of the poor baby could not repress .
She often send one or the other to cheer up some little neighbor , and in this way Do and Flo become small sister of charity , welcome eagerly , reluctantly return , and love by all , although they never utter a word and their dingy face could not express the emotion that stir their saw-dust bosom .
When Saturday night come they be lay in their usual place on Midge 's arm .
She be too weak to kiss them now , and nurse lay their battered cheek against the lip that whisper faintly , `` Be sure you send 'em to the little girl , and tell her -- tell her -- all about it . ''
Then she turn her cheek to the pillow with a little sigh and lay so still the doll think she have go to sleep .
She have , but the sweet eye do not open in the morning , and there be no breath in the little breast to rock the doll any more .
`` I know she be an angel , and now she have fly away , '' say Dora softly , a they watch the white image carry out in the weeping nurse 's arm , with the early sunshine turn all the pretty hair to gold .
`` I think that be what they call die , sister .
It be a much lovely way to end than a we do in the dust bin or rag-bag .
I wonder if there be a little Heaven anywhere for good doll ? ''
answer Flora , with what look like a tear on her cheek ; but it be only a drop from the violet send by the kind Doctor last night .
`` I hope so , for I think the soul of little child might miss u if they love u a dear Midge do , '' whisper Dora , try to kiss the blue flower in her hand , for the child have share her last gift with these friend .
`` Why do n't you let her take them along , poor motherless baby ? ''
ask the doctor when he saw the doll lie a she have leave them .
`` I promise her they should go to the girl they be send to , and please , I 'd like to keep my word to the little darling , '' answer Nurse with a sob .
`` You shall , '' say the Doctor , and put them in his breast pocket with the faded violet , for everybody love the pauper child send to die in a hospital , because Christian charity make every man and woman father and mother to these little one .
All day the doll go about in the busy Doctor 's pocket , and I think the violet do them good , for the soft perfume clung to them long afterward like the memory of a lovely life , a short and sweet a that of the flower .
In the evening they be fold up in a fresh paper and re-directed carefully .
The Doctor write a little note tell why he have keep them , and be just about to put on some stamp when a friend come in who be go to Boston in the morning .
`` Anything to take along , Fred ? ''
ask the newcomer .
`` This parcel , if you will .
I have a feeling that I 'd rather not have it knock about in a mail-bag , '' and the Doctor tell him why .
It be pleasant to see how carefully the traveller put away the parcel after that , and to hear him say that he be go through Boston to the mountain for his holiday , and would deliver it in Portland to Miss Plum herself .
`` Now there be some chance of our get there , '' say Flora , a they set off next day in a new Russia leather bag .
On the way they overhear a long chat between some New York and Boston lady which impress them very much .
Flora like to hear the fashionable gossip about clothes and people and art and theatre , but Dora prefer the learned conversation of the young Boston lady , who seem to know a little of everything , or think they do .
`` I hope Mamma will give me an entirely new wardrobe when I get home ; and we will have doll ' wedding and ball , and a play , and be a fine and fashionable a those lady down there , '' say Flora , after listen a while .
`` You have get your head full of dressy idea and high life , sister .
I do n't care for such thing , but mean to cultivate my mind a fast a I can .
That girl say she be in college , and name over more study than I can count .
I do wish we be to stop and see a little of the refined society of Boston , '' answer Dora , primly .
`` Pooh ! ''
say Flo , `` do n't you try to be intellectual , for you be only a wooden-headed doll .
I mean to be a real Westerner , and just enjoy myself a I please , without care what other folk do or think .
Boston be no good than the rest of the world , I guess . ''
Groans from every article in the bag greet this disrespectful speech , and an avalanche of Boston paper fell upon the audacious doll .
But Flo be undaunted , and shout from underneath the pile : `` I do n't care !
Minnesota forever ! ''
till her breath give out .
Dora be so mortified that she never say a word till they be let out in a room at the Parker House .
Here she admire everything , and read all the evening in a volume of Emerson 's Poems from the bag , for Mr. Mt . Vernon Beacon be a Boston man , and never go anywhere without a wise book or two in his pocket .
Flo turn up her nose at all she saw , and devote herself to a long chat with the smart bag which come from New York and be full of gossip .
The next afternoon they really get to Portland , and as soon a Mr. Beacon have make his toilet he set out to find little Miss Plum .
When the parlor door open to admit her he be much embarrass , for , advance with a paternal smile and the doll extend to the expect child , he find himself face to face with a pretty young lady , who look a if she think him a little mad .
A few word explain the errand , however , and when she read the note Aunt Maria 's bright eye be full of tear a she say , hug the dilapidated doll : `` I 'll write the story of their travel , and send the dear old thing back to the child as soon a possible . ''
And so she do with Mr. Beacon 's help , for he decide to try the air of Portland , and spend his vacation there .
The doll be re-painted and re-dressed till they be more beautiful than ever , and their clothes fine enough to suit even Flo .
They be a good while do this , and when all be ready , Aunt Maria take it into her head to run out to St. Paul and surprise the child .
By a singular coincidence Mr. Beacon have railroad business in that direction , so they set off together , with two splendid doll do up in a gay box .
All that be ever know about that journey be that these traveller stop at the hospital in New York , and go on good friend than before after hear from the good Doctor all the pathetic story of little Midge .
The young Plums have long ago give up the hope of ever see Do and Flo again , for they start in June and it be early in September when Aunt Maria appear before them without the least warning , accompany by a pleasant gentleman from Boston .
Six kiss have hardly resound from Aunty 's blooming cheek when a most attractive box be produce from the Russia leather bag , and the wandering doll restore to the arm of their enraptured mamma .
A small volume neatly write and adorn with a few picture of the most exciting incident of the trip also appear .
`` Every one write or print a book in Boston , you know , so we do both , '' say Aunt Maria , laugh , a she hand over the remarkable history which she have compose and Mr. Beacon illustrate .
It be read with intense interest , and be as true a most story be nowadays .
`` Nothing more delightful can happen now ! ''
exclaim the child , a they lay by the precious work and enthrone the travelled doll in the place of honor on the roof of the baby-house .
But something much more delightful do happen ; for at Thanksgiving time there be a wedding at the Plums ' .
Not a doll 's wedding , a Flo have plan , but a real one , for the gentleman from Boston actually marry Aunt Maria .
There be six bridesmaid , all in blue , and Flora and Dora , in the lovely of new pink gown , be set aloft among the rose on the wedding-cake , their proper place a everyone say , for there never would have be any marriage at all but for this Doll 's Journey From Minnesota to Maine .
VI .
MORNING-GLORIES .
`` What 's that ? ''
-- and Daisy sit up in her little bed to listen ; for she have never hear a sound like it before .
It be very early , and the house be still .
The sun be just rise , and the morning-glories at the window be turn their blue and purple cup to catch the welcome light .
The sky be full of rosy cloud ; dew shone like diamond on the waving grass , and the bird be sing a they only sing at dawn .
But soft , sweet than any bird-voice be the delicate music which Daisy heard .
So airy and gay be the sound , it seem impossible to lie still with that fairy dancing-tune echo through the room .
Out of bed scramble Daisy , her sleepy eye open wider and wider with surprise and pleasure a she listen and wonder .
`` Where be it ? ''
she say , pop her head out of the window .
The morning-glories only dance lightly on their stem , the robin chirp shrilly in the garden below , and the wind give Daisy a kiss ; but none of them answer her , and still the lovely music sound close beside her .
`` It 's a new kind of bird , perhaps ; or maybe it 's a fairy hidden somewhere .
Oh , if it be how splendid it will be ! ''
cried Daisy ; and she begin to look carefully in all the colored cup , under the leaf of the woodbine , and in the wren 's nest close by .
There be neither fairy nor bird to be see ; and Daisy stand wondering , when a voice cry out from below : `` Why , little nightcap , what bring you out of your bed so early ? ''
`` O Aunt Wee !
do you hear it -- that pretty music playing somewhere near !
I ca n't find it ; but I think it 's a fairy , do n't you ? ''
say Daisy , look down at the young lady stand in the garden with her hand full of rose .
Aunt Wee listen , smile , and shake her head .
`` Do n't you remember you say last night that you think the world a very stupid , grown-up place , because there be no giant and fairy in it now ?
Well , perhaps there be fairy , and they be go to show themselves to you , if you watch well . ''
Daisy clap her hand , and dance about on her little bare foot ; for , of all thing in the world , she most want to see a fairy .
`` What must I do to find them , Aunt Wee ? ''
she cry , pop out her head again with her cap half off , and her curly hair blow in the wind .
`` Why , you see , they frolic all night , and go to sleep at dawn ; so we must get up very early , if we want to catch the elf awake .
They be such delicate , fly-away little thing , and we be so big and clumsy , we shall have to look carefully , and perhaps hunt a long time before we find even one , '' reply Aunt Wee , very gravely .
`` Mamma say I 'm quick at find thing ; and you know all about fairy , so I guess we 'll catch one .
Ca n't we begin now ?
It 's very early , and this music have wake me up ; so I do n't want to sleep any more .
Will you begin to hunt now ? ''
`` But you do n't like to get up early , or to walk in the field ; and , if we mean to catch a fairy , we must be up and out by sunrise every fair morning till we get one .
Can you do this , lazy Daisy ? ''
And Aunt Wee smile to herself a if something please her very much .
`` Oh !
I will , truly , get up , and not fret a bit , if you 'll only help me look .
Please come now to dress me , and see if you can find what make the music . ''
Daisy be very much in earnest , and in such a hurry to be off that she could hardly stand still to have her hair brush , and think there be a great many unnecessary button and string on her clothes that day .
Usually she lay late , get up slowly and fret at every thing a little girl be apt to do when they have have too much sleep .
She be n't a rosy , stout Daisy ; but have be ill , and have fall into a way of think she could n't do anything but lie about , read fairy-tales , and be pet by every one .
Mamma and papa have try all sort of thing to amuse and do her good ; for she be their only little daughter , and they love her very dearly .
But nothing please her long ; and she lounge about , pale and fretful , till Aunt Laura come .
Daisy call her `` Wee '' when she be a baby , and could n't talk plainly ; and she still use the name because it suit the cheery little aunt so well .
`` I do n't see anything , and the music have stop .
I think some elf just come to wake you up , and then fly away ; so we wo n't waste any more time in look here , '' say Wee , a she finish dress Daisy , who fly about like a Will-o ' - the-wisp all the while .
`` Do you think it will come again to-morrow ? ''
ask Daisy anxiously .
`` I dare say you 'll hear it , if you wake in time .
Now get your hat , and we will see what we can find down by the brook .
I saw a great many firefly there last night , and fancy there be a ball ; so we may find some drowsy elf among the buttercup and clover . ''
Away rush Daisy for her hat , and soon be walk gayly down the green lane , look about her a if she have never be there before ; for every thing seem wonderfully fresh and lovely .
`` How pink the cloud be , and how the dew twinkle in the grass !
I never saw it so before , '' she say .
`` Because by the time you be up the pretty pink cloud be go , and the thirsty grass have drink the dew , or the sun have draw it up to fall again at night for the flower ' even bath , '' reply Wee , watch the soft color that begin to touch Daisy 's pale cheek .
`` I think we 'd better look under that cobweb spread like a tent over the white clover .
A fairy would be very likely to creep in there and sleep . ''
Daisy kneel down and peep carefully ; but all she saw be a little brown spider , who look very much surprise to see visitor so early .
`` I do n't like spider , '' say Daisy , much disappoint .
`` There be thing about spider a interesting to hear a fairy tale , '' say Wee .
`` This be Mrs. Epeira Diadema ; and she be a respectable , industrious little neighbor .
She spread her tent , but sits under a leaf near by , wait for her breakfast .
She wrap her egg in a soft silken bag , and hide them in some safe chink , where they lie till spring .
The egg be prettily carve and ornament , and so hard that the baby spider have to force their way out by bite the shell open and poke their little head through .
The mother die as soon a her egg be safely place , and the spiderlings have to take care of themselves . ''
`` How do you know about it , Aunt Wee ?
You talk a if Mrs. Eppyra -- or whatever her name be -- have tell you herself .
Did she ? ''
ask Daisy , feel more interested in the brown spider .
`` No ; I read it in a book , and saw picture of the egg , web , and family .
I have a live one in a bottle ; and she spin silken ladder all up and down , and a little room to sleep in .
She eat worm and bug , and be very amiable and interesting till she fell ill and die . ''
`` I should like to see the book ; and have a spider-bottle , so I could take care of the poor little orphan when they be bear .
Good-by , ma'am .
I shall call again ; for you be ` most as good a a fairy there in your pretty tent , with a white clover for your bed . ''
Daisy walk on a few step , and then stop to say : `` What do that bird mean by call ` Hurry up , hurry up ? '
He keep fly before u , and look back a if he want to show me something . ''
`` Let me hear what he say .
I may be able to understand him , or the bob-o-link that swing on the alder by the brook . ''
Wee listen a moment , while the bird twitter and chirp with all their heart .
Presently Wee sang in a tone very like the bob-o-link 's : `` Daisy and Wee , Come here , and see What a dainty feast be spread : Down in the grass Where fairies pas , Here be berry ripe and red .
`` All wet with dew , They wait for you : Come hither , and eat your fill , While I gayly sing , In my airy swing , And the sun climb up the hill . ''
`` Did he really say that ? ''
cry Daisy , watch the bob-o-link , who sit sway up and down on the green bough , and nod his white-capped head at her in the most friendly manner .
`` Perhaps I do n't translate it rightly ; for it be very hard to put bird-notes into our language , because we have n't word soft and sweet enough .
But I really think there be berry over there , and we will see if what he say be true , '' say Wee .
Over the wall they go , and there , on a sunny bank , find a bed of the reddest , ripest berry ever see .
`` Thank you , thank you , for tell me to hurry up , and show me such a splendid feast , '' say Daisy , with her mouth full , a she nod back at the bird .
`` These be so much sweet than those we buy .
I 'd carry some home to mamma , if I only have a basket . ''
`` You can pick this great leaf full , while I make you a basket , '' say Wee .
Daisy soon fill the leaf , and then sit watch her aunt plait a pretty basket of rush .
While she wait she look about , and keep find something curious or pleasant to interest and amuse her .
First she saw a tiny rainbow in a dewdrop that hung on a blade of grass ; then she watch a frisky calf come down to drink on the other side of the brook , and laugh to see him scamper away with his tail in the air .
Close by grow a pitcher-plant ; and a yellow butterfly sit on the edge , bath it foot , Daisy say .
Presently she discover a little ground bird sit on her nest , and peep anxiously , a if undecided whether to fly away or trust her .
`` I wo n't hurt you , little mother .
Do n't be afraid , '' whisper the child ; and , a if it understand , the bird settle down on her nest with a comfortable chirp , while it mate hop up to give her a nice plump worm for breakfast .
`` I love bird .
Tell me something about them , Aunt Wee .
You must know many thing ; for they like you , and come when you call . ''
`` Once upon a time , '' begin Wee , while her finger fly and the pretty basket grow , `` there be a great snow-storm , and all the country be cover with a thick white quilt .
It freeze a little , so one could walk over it , and I go out for a run .
Oh , so cold it be , with a sharp wind , and no sun or any thing green to make it pleasant !
I go far away over the field , and sit down to rest .
While I sit there , a little bird come by , and stop to rest also . ''
` How do you do ? '
say I. '' ` Chick-a-dee-dee , ' say he . '' '
A cold day , ' say I. '' ` Chick-a-dee-dee , ' say he . ''
` Are n't you afraid of starve , now the ground be cover and the tree be bare ? ' ''
` Chick-a-dee-dee , ma'am , chick-a-dee-dee ! ' ''
answer the bird in the same cheerful tone .
And it sound a if he say , ' I shall be care for .
I 'm not afraid . ' ''
` What will you eat ?
There 's nothing here or for mile round .
I really think you 'll starve , birdie , ' say I . `` Then he laugh , and give me a merry look a he light on a tall , dry weed near by .
He shake it hard with his little bill ; when down fell a shower of seed , and there be dinner all ready on a snow-white cloth .
All the while he eat he keep look up at me with his quick , bright eye ; and , when he have do , he say , as plainly a a bird could say it : '' ` Cold wind may blow , And snow may fall , But well we know God care for all . ' ''
`` I like that little story , and shall always think of it when I hear the chick-a-dee-dee . ''
Daisy sit a moment with a thoughtful look in her eye ; then she say slowly , a if sorry for the word : `` It be n't a stupid , grown-up world .
It 's a very pleasant , young world ; and I like it a great deal well this morning than I do last night . ''
`` I 'm glad of that ; and , even if we do n't find our fairy to-day , you will have find some sunshine , Daisy , and that be almost as good .
Now put in the berry , and we 'll go on . ''
How they hunt !
They climb tree to peep into squirrel-holes and bird ' - nest ; they chase bee and butterfly to ask for news of the elf ; they wad in the brook , hop to catch a water-sprite ; they run after thistle-down , fancy a fairy might be astride ; they search the flower and fern , question sun and wind , listen to robin and thrush ; but no one could tell them any thing of the little people , though all have gay and charm bit of news about themselves .
And Daisy think the world get young and happy every minute .
When they come in to breakfast , papa and mamma look at Daisy , and then nod with a smile at Aunt Wee ; for , though Daisy 's frock be soil , her boot wet , and her hair tumble , her cheek be rosy , eye bright , and voice so cheerful that they think it well music than any in the summer world without .
`` Hunting fairy be a pleasant play , be n't it , Daisy ? ''
say papa , a he taste the berry , and admire the green basket .
`` Oh , yes !
and we be go again to-morrow .
Aunt Wee say we must try seven day at least .
I like it , and mean to keep on till I really find my fairy . ''
`` I think you will find something good than ` little vanisher , ' dear , '' say mamma , fill up the bowl of bread and milk which Daisy be fast emptying ; for she certainly have find an appetite .
`` There it be again ! ''
cry Daisy , fly out of bed the next morning still earlier than the day before .
Yes , there it be , the fairy music , a blithe and sweet a ever ; and the morning-glories ring their delicate bell a if keep time .
Daisy felt rather sleepy , but remember her promise to Aunt Wee , and splash into her tub , sing the bob-o-link 's song a she bath .
`` Where shall we go to-day ? ''
she ask , a they go out into the garden .
`` I think we 'd better try a new place ; so we 'll go to the farmyard ; and , while we fee the hen , I 'll listen to their chat , and perhaps can learn something from it , '' reply Wee soberly .
`` Do hen know about fairy ?
I think they be very dull thing , and do n't care for any thing but eat corn and laying egg , '' say Daisy , surprise .
`` Oh , dear , no !
they be very sensible creature , and see a deal of the world in their daily walk .
Hunting for insect give them an excellent chance to see fairy , if there be any .
Here be some corn for the biddy ; and , after we have feed them , we will look for egg , and so may find a brownie or two . ''
Such a clatter a there be when they come to the barnyard ; for every thing be just awake , and in the best spirit .
Ducks be paddle off to the pond ; geese to the meadow ; and meek gray guinea-hens trip away to hunt bug in the garden .
A splendid cock stand on the wall , and crow so loud and clear that all the neighboring chanticleers reply .
The motherly hen cluck and scratch with their busy brood about them , or sat and scold in the coop because the chick would gad abroad .
Doves coo on the sunny roof , and smooth their gleaming feather .
Daisy 's donkey nibble a thistle by the wall , and a stately peacock march before the door with all his plumage spread .
It make Daisy laugh to see the air the fowl put on a she scatter corn , and throw meal and water to the chick .
Some push and gobble ; some stand meekly outside the crowd , and get what they could ; others seize a mouthful , and run away to eat it in a corner .
The chick get into the pan entirely , and tumble one over the other in their hurry to eat ; but the mamma saw that none go hungry .
And the polite cock wait upon them in the most gentlemanly manner , make queer little cluck and gurgle a if he say : `` Allow me , madam , to offer you this kernel ; '' or , `` Here , my dear , try that bit . ''
And sometimes he peck a little , with a loud quaver , evidently say , `` Come , come , child , behave yourselves , and do n't eat like pig . ''
`` What be she say ? ''
ask Daisy , point to an old gray hen in a black turban , who be walk about alone , mutter to herself , a hen often do in their promenade .
`` She say a cat have make a nest , and hatch three kit up on the loft , near her own nest ; and she do n't like it , because their mewing annoy her , '' say Wee , after listen a minute .
`` How nice !
let 's go and find them .
But do you learn anything about the fairy from the hen 's chat ? ''
`` No : they have be so busy set , they have have no time for picnic yet .
But they will let u know , if they discover any . ''
In the barn , the cow be be milk ; and Daisy have a mugful of it , warm and sweet , out of the foaming pail .
`` We 'll take some to Mrs. Purr ; for , I dare say , she do n't like to leave the kit long , and will enjoy a sip of something comfortable , '' say Wee , a Daisy climb the ladder , and go rustle over the hay to a corner , whence come a joyful `` Mew ! ''
What a charm sight it be , to be sure !
a snow-white cat lie in a cosy nest , and , by her , three snow-white kit , wag three very small gray tail .
`` There never be any thing so lovely ! ''
cry Daisy , a she sit with the three downy ball in her lap , while the mamma gratefully lap the new milk from Aunt Wee 's cup .
`` Are they good than fairy ? ''
`` Almost : for I know about pussy , and can cuddle them ; but I could n't a fairy , you know , and they might be afraid of me .
These dear be not afraid , and I shall have such fun with them a they grow up .
What shall we name them , auntie ? ''
`` Snowball , Patpaw , and Wagtail would do , I think , '' say Wee , stroke the cat , who rub against her , purr very loud .
`` Yes : I like those name for my pet .
But what be Mrs. Purr say , with her mouth up to your ear ? ''
ask Daisy , who firmly believe that Aunt Wee know every thing .
`` She tell me that when she go on a grasshopper hunt the other day , a she run through the meadow , she saw some lovely creature all in blue , with gauze wing , fly about over the river , and sit in the water-lilies .
She think they may be fairy , and advise u to go and look . ''
`` So we will to-morrow , '' say Daisy .
`` Ask her , please , if I may take the kit into the house , if I 'll be very careful and give them a nice big bed to sleep in . ''
`` She say you may ; but she must go too , else the kit will cry , '' say Wee , after listen to Pussy 's purr a minute .
Much pleased with her new pet , Daisy take them in her apron , and , follow by their confiding mamma , march to the house , and establish them in the old cradle which use to be hers .
Pussy get in also ; and , when they be settle on a soft cushion , Daisy rock them gently to and fro .
At first Mrs. Purr open her yellow eye , and look rather anxious : but , a nothing uncomfortable happen , she compose herself , and soon quite like the motion ; for she fell asleep , and make a pretty picture a she lay with her downy white baby on her downy white breast .
When the sun rise next morning , he saw Daisy and Wee float down the river in their boat .
`` Bless me !
here 's company , '' say the sun , and begin at once to make them welcome in his most charming manner .
He set the wave to sparkle with a sudden shimmer ; he shoot long ray of light through the dark hemlock , till they look like fairy tree ; he touch Daisy 's hair and it turn to gold ; he chase away the shadow that lurk among the hill ; he draw up the misty curtain that hover over the river ; and , with the warmth of his kiss , wake the sleeping lily .
`` Look , look , Aunt Wee !
how they open , one by one , a the light shine on them !
We sha n't have to wait any long ; for they get up with the sun , a you do . ''
As she speak , Daisy catch a half-open lily , and draw it up , fragrant and drip , fresh from it sleep .
`` They look like a fleet of fairy ship , anchor in this quiet harbor , with sails half furl , and crew asleep .
See the little sailor , in their yellow jacket , lift up their head a the wind blow it whistle , like a boatswain , to ` pipe all hand . ' ''
Daisy laugh at Aunt Wee 's fancy , and stir up the crew of the Water-sprite , a she call her flower , till the white sail be all set , and it be ready for a summer voyage .
`` It be time we saw the fairy in blue , unless old Madam Purr deceive u .
I hope we shall find one ; for , though I enjoy every thing we see , I do want my elf too . ''
`` What be that ? ''
cried Wee ; and Daisy fly up so quickly that the boat rock like a cradle .
A slender creature , in a blue dress , with gauzy wing , dart by , and vanish among the rush that nod by the bank .
`` Go nearer , -- softly !
softly !
-- and maybe it will fly out again .
I really think it be a fairy ; for I never saw any thing like it before , '' whisper Daisy , much excite .
Wee row in among the green rush and purple water-weeds , and out flew half-a-dozen of the blue-bodied creature .
They do n't seem afraid , but skim about the boat , a if curious to see what it be ; and Daisy sit , and star with all her might .
Presently one of the lovely thing lit on the lily in her hand , and she hold her breath to watch it .
A little shadow of disappointment pass over her face a she look ; but it be go at once , and her voice be full of delight a she say softly : `` It 's not a fairy , Aunt Wee ; but it be very beautiful , with it slender blue body , it lacy wing , and bright eye .
What name do it have ? ''
`` We call it a dragon-fly ; and it could tell you a pretty little story about itself , could you understand it .
In May the tiny egg be drop on the water , and sink to the bottom , where little creature be bear , -- ugly , brown thing , with six leg and no wing .
They fee on water-insects , and for a long time swim about in this state .
When ready , they climb up the stem of some plant , and sit in the sun till the ugly brown shell drop away , and the lovely winged creature appear .
They grow in an hour to be perfect dragon-flies , and float away to lead happy life in the sunshine by the river . ''
As if only wait till the story be do , the dragon-fly fly off with a whirr , and dart to and fro , hunt for it breakfast , glitter splendidly a it flash among the leaf or dart close above the water .
Daisy forget her disappointment in a minute , and go fish for lily ; while the turtle come up to sun themselves on the rock , the merry little tadpole wiggle in the shallow place , and a wild duck paddle by with a brood of duckling follow in her wake .
`` Oh , dear !
it rain ; and we ca n't go fairy-hunting at all , '' say Daisy next morning , a the patter on the window-pane woke her up , and Aunt Wee come in to dress her .
`` Yes , we can , dear ; jump up , and see what a funny place I 'll take you to . ''
Daisy think the rain would be a capital excuse for lie in bed ; for she still like to cuddle and drowse in her cosey , warm nest .
But she be curious to know where the curious place be ; so she get up and follow .
`` Why , Aunt Wee , this be the garret ; and there be n't any thing nice or funny here , '' she say , a they climb the stair , and come into the big attic , fill with all manner of old thing .
`` Is n't there ?
We 'll soon see . ''
And so they do : for Aunt Wee begin to play ; and presently Daisy be shout with fun a she sit on an old saddle , with a hair-covered trunk for a horse , a big old-fashioned bonnet on her head , and a red silk petticoat for a habit .
Then they go to sea in a great chest , and get wreck on a desert island , where they build a fort with box and bag , hunt bear with rusty gun , and have to eat dried berry , herb and nut ; for no other food could be find .
Aunt Wee get an old fiddle , and have a dancing-school , where Daisy caper till she be tire .
So they rummage out some dusty book , and look at picture so quietly that a little mouse come out of a drawer and peep about , think no one be there .
`` Let 's find the nest , since we do n't find any fairy , '' say Wee ; and , open the drawer , she turn over the thing till she come to a pair of old velvet shoe ; and there in the toe of one , nicely cuddle under a bit of flannel , lay four pink mite , which wake up , and stretch their tiny leg , and squeak such small squeak one could hardly hear them .
`` How cunning they be !
I wish they would let me put them with the kit , and have a nursery full of baby .
Would n't it be nice to see them all grow up ? ''
say Daisy .
`` I 'm afraid they would n't grow up , if Mrs. Purr live with them , '' begin Wee , but get no far ; for just then the cat bounce into the drawer , and eat up the mouselings in four mouthful .
Daisy scream ; the mother-mouse give a doleful squeak , and run into a hole ; and Aunt Wee try to save the little one .
But it be too late : Purr have get her breakfast , and sit wash her face after it , a if she have enjoy it .
`` Never mind , Daisy : she would have catch them by and by , and it 's as well to have them take care of before they do any harm .
There be the bell : do n't cry , but come and tell papa what a fine romp we 've have . ''
`` It do n't rain , but it 's dreadfully wet ; so we 'll go to the dairy , and see if any sprite be hide there , '' say Wee next day ; and to the dairy they go .
A pleasant place it be , -- so clean and cool , and a full of sweet odor a if the ghost of buttercup and clover still haunt the milk which they have help to make .
Dolly be churn , and Polly be make up butter in nice little pat .
Both be very kind , and let Daisy peep everywhere .
All round on white shelf stand the shining pan , full of milk ; the stone floor be wet ; and a stream of water ran along a narrow bed through the room , and in it stand jar of butter , pot of cream , and can of milk .
The window be open , and hop-vines shake their green bell before it .
The bird sing outside , and maid sing inside , a the churn and the wooden spatter keep time : `` Brindle and Bess , White-star and Jess -- Come , butter , come !
Eat cowslip fine , Red columbine -- Come , butter , come !
Grasses green and tall , Clover , best of all , -- Come , butter , come !
And give every night Milk sweet and white -- Come , butter , come !
Make the churn go , See the lump grow !
-- Come , butter , come ! ''
Daisy sang also , and turn the handle till she be tire ; then she help Polly with the butter , and make four little pat , -- one stamp with a star for papa , one with a rise for mamma , a strawberry for Aunt Wee , and a cow for herself .
She skim a pitcher of cream with a shallow shell , and like the work so much she ask to have a little pan of milk put by for her to take care of every day .
Dolly promise , and give her a small shell and a low shelf all to herself .
When she go in , she carry her pretty pat in one hand , the cream-pot in the other , and enter the breakfast room look a brisk and rosy a a little milkmaid .
It be a lovely morning when Daisy be next rouse by the fairy music , and the pony be stand at the door .
`` Are we go far ? ''
she ask , a Wee put on her riding-skirt , and tie back her hair .
`` Up to the mountain-top : it 's only a mile ; and we shall have time , if we ride fast , '' answer Wee .
Away they go , through the green lane , over the bridge , and up the steep hillside where the sheep fed and colt frisk a they pass by .
Higher and high climb Dandy and Prance , the pony ; and gayer and gayer grow Daisy and Wee , a the fresh air blow over them , and the morning-red glowed on their face .
When they reach the top , they sit on a tall stone , and look down into the valley on either side .
`` This seem like a place to find giant , not fairy , it be so high and big and splendid up here , '' say Daisy , a her eye roam over river , forest , town , and hill .
`` There be giant here ; and I bring you up to see them , '' answer Wee .
`` Mercy , me !
where be they ? ''
cry Daisy , look very curious and rather frighten .
`` There be one of them . ''
And Wee point to the waterfall that go dash and foam down into the valley .
`` That giant turn the wheel of all the mill you see .
Some of them grind grain for our bread , some help to spin cloth for our clothes , some make paper , and others saw tree into board .
That be a beautiful and busy giant , Daisy . ''
`` So it be , and some day we 'll go and see it work .
Show me the others : I like your giant ` most as well a those in the fairy-books . ''
`` On this side you 'll see another , call Steam .
He be a very strong fellow ; for , with the help of gunpowder , he will break the granite mountain in piece , and carry it away .
He work in the other mill , and take heavy load of stone , cloth , paper , and wood all over the country .
Then , on the right of u be a third giant , call Electricity .
He run along those wire , and carry message from one end of the world to the other .
He go under the sea and through the air ; he bring news to every one ; run day and night , yet never tire ; and often help sick people with his lively magic . ''
`` I like him best , I think ; for he be more like a real , wonderful giant .
Is there any on that side of u ? ''
ask Daisy , turn round to look behind her .
`` Yes : the best and most powerful of all life in that big house with the bell on the roof , '' say Wee , smile .
`` Why , that 's only the schoolhouse . ''
`` Education be a long word , dear ; but you know what it mean , and , a you grow old , you will see what wonder it can work .
It be a noble giant ; for in this country rich and poor be help by it , and no one need suffer for it unless they choose .
It work more wonder than any other : it change little child into wise , good men and woman , who rule the world , and make happy home everywhere ; it help write book , sing song , paint picture , do good deed , and beautify the world .
Love and respect it , my little Daisy , and be glad that you live now when such giant lend a hand to dwarf like u . ''
Daisy sit still a long time , look all about her on the mountain-top ; and , when she ride away , she carry a new thought in her mind , which she never forget .
`` This be the last day of the seven , and no fairy have be find .
Do you think I ever shall see one ? ''
say Daisy , on the Sunday morning that end her week 's hunt .
`` Not the kind you think of , for there be none such , Daisy ; but you have find two good and more beautiful one than any fanciful sprite , '' say Wee .
`` Have I ?
Where be they ?
What be their name ? ''
Aunt Wee draw her to the glass , and say , a she point to Daisy 's face : `` Here they be , and their name be Health and Happiness .
There be many way of lose them , and they be hard to catch when once lose .
I want you to keep both , and try to show you how .
A happy , healthful hour in the morning sweetens and brighten the whole day ; and there be no fairy-book half so wonderful a the lovely world all about u , if we only know how to read it . ''
`` Then all these morning we be hunt after health and happiness , instead of fairy , be we ? ''
`` Yes : have n't you enjoy it , and do n't you think you have catch my fairy ? ''
Daisy look from a little picture of herself , which Wee have draw some time ago , to her image in the glass .
One be dull and sad , pale and cross ; the other , rosy , gay , and smiling , -- the likeness of a happy , hearty little girl , wide-awake and in good tune .
She understand the kind joke ; and , turn , kiss Aunt Wee , a she say , gratefully : `` I think I have catch your elf , and I 'll try to keep them all my life .
But tell me one thing : be the music that wake me all a joke too ? ''
`` No , dear : here it be , and now it be your own ; for you have learn to wake and listen to it . ''
Daisy look , and saw Aunt Wee lean from the window , and take out of a hollow nook , in the old tree close by , a little box .
She set it on the table , touch a spring , and the airy music sound more beautiful than ever .
`` Is it mine , all mine ? ''
cry Daisy .
`` Yes : I hide it while I try my little plan , and now you shall have it for your own .
See , here be the best elf I can give you , and she will dance whenever you call her . ''
Wee push a golden pin , and up spring a tiny figure , all crimson and gold , with shin wing , and a garland on it dainty head .
Softly play the hidden music , and airily dance the little sylph till the silvery chime die away ; then , fold her delicate arm , she sink from sight , leave Daisy breathless with delight .
V. SHADOW-CHILDREN .
Ned , Polly , and Will sit on the step one sun-shiny morning , do nothing , except wish they have something pleasant to do .
`` Something new , something never hear of before , -- would n't that be jolly ? ''
say Ned , with a great yawn .
`` It must be an amusing play , and one that we do n't get tired of very soon , '' add Polly gravely .
`` And something that do n't be wrong , else mamma would n't like it , '' say little Will , who be very good for a small boy .
As no one could suggest any thing to suit , they all sit silent a few minute .
Suddenly Ned say , rather crossly , `` I wish my shadow would n't mock me .
Every time I stretch or gape it do the same , and I do n't like it . ''
`` Poor thing , it ca n't help that : it have to do just what you do , and be your slave all day .
I 'm glad I ai n't a shadow , '' say Polly .
`` I try to run away from mine sometimes , but I ca n't ever .
It will come after me ; and in the night it scar me , if it get big and black , '' say Will , look behind him .
`` Would n't it be fun to see shadow go about alone , and do thing like people ? ''
ask Polly .
`` I just wish they would .
I 'd like to see ours cut caper ; that would be a jolly new game , would n't it ? ''
say Ned .
No one have time to speak ; for suddenly the three little shadow on the sunny wall behind them stand up straight , and begin to bow .
`` Mercy , me ! ''
cry Polly , star at them .
`` By Jove , that 's odd ! ''
say Ned , look queer .
`` Are they alive ? ''
ask Will , a little frightened .
`` Do n't be alarm : they wo n't hurt you , '' say a soft voice .
`` To-day be midsummer-day , and whoever wish a wish can have it till midnight .
You want to see your shadow by themselves ; and you can , if you promise to follow them a they have follow you so long .
They will not get you into harm ; so you may safely try it , if you like .
Do you agree for the day to do a they do , and so have your wish ? ''
`` Yes , we promise , '' answer the child .
`` Tell no one till night , and be faithful shadow to the shadow . ''
The voice be silent , but with more funny little bow the shadow begin to move off in different direction .
The child know their own : for Ned 's be the tall , and have it hand in it pocket ; Polly 's have a frock on , and two bow where it hair be tie up ; while Will 's be a plump little shadow in a blouse , with a curly head and a pug nose .
Each child go after it shadow , laughing , and enjoy the fun .
Ned 's master go straight to the shed , take down a basket , and march away to the garden , where it begin to move it hand a if busily pick pea .
Ned stop laugh when he saw that , and look rather ashamed ; for he remember that his mother have ask him to do that little job for her , and he have answer , -- `` Oh , bother the old pea !
I 'm busy , and I ca n't . ''
`` Who tell you about this ? ''
he ask , begin to work .
The shadow shake it head , and point first to Ned 's new jacket , then to a set of nice garden tool near by , and then seem to blow a kiss from it shadowy finger towards mamma , who be just pass the open gate .
`` Oh !
you mean that she do lot for me ; so I ought to do what I can for her , and love her dearly , '' say Ned , get a pleasanter face every minute .
The shadow nod , and work away as busily a the bee , tumble heel over head in the great yellow squash blossom , and get as dusty a little miller .
Somehow Ned rather like the work , with such an odd comrade near by ; for , though the shadow do n't really help a bit , it seem to try , and set an excellent example .
When the basket be full , the shadow take one handle , and Ned the other ; and they carry it in .
`` Thank you , dear .
I be afraid we should have to give up our peas to-day : I 'm so busy , I ca n't stop , '' say mamma , look surprised and pleased .
Ned could n't stop to talk ; for the shadow run away to the woodpile , and begin to chop with all it might .
`` Well , I suppose I must ; but I never saw such a fellow for work a this shadow be .
He be n't a bit like me , though he 's be with me so long , '' say Ned , swing the real hatchet in time with the shadowy one .
Polly 's new mistress go to the dining-room , and fell to wash up the breakfast cup .
Polly hat that work , and sulkily begin to rattle the spoon and knock the thing about .
But the shadow would n't allow that ; and Polly have to do just what it do , though she grumble all the while .
`` She do n't splash a bit , or make any clatter ; so I guess she 's a tidy creature , '' say Polly .
`` How long she do rub each spoon and glass .
We never shall get do .
What a fuss she make with the napkin , lay them all even in the drawer .
And now she 's at the salt-cellars , do them just a mamma like .
I wish she 'd live here , and do my work for me .
Why , what 's that ? ''
And Polly stop fret to listen ; for she seem to hear the sound of singing , -- so sweet , and yet so very faint she could catch no word , and only make out a cheerful little tune .
`` Do you hear any one singing , mamma ? ''
she ask .
`` No : I wish I do . ''
And mamma sigh ; for baby be poorly , pile of sew lay wait for her , Biddy be turn thing topsy-turvy in the kitchen for want of a word from the mistress , and Polly be look sullen .
The little girl do n't say any more , but work quietly and watch the shadow , feel sure the faint song come from it .
Presently she begin to hum the tune she catch by snatch ; and , before she know it , she be sing away like a blackbird .
Baby stop cry , and mamma say , smile : `` Now I hear somebody singing , and it 's the music I like best in the world . ''
That please Polly ; but , a minute after , she stop smile , for the shadow go and take baby , or seem to , and Polly really do .
Now , baby be heavy , and cross with it teeth ; and Polly do n't feel like tend it one bit .
Mamma hurry away to the kitchen ; and Polly walk up and down the room with poor baby hanging over her arm , cry dismally , with a pin in it back , a wet bib under it chin , and nothing cold and hard to bite with it hot , ache gum , where the little teeth be try to come through .
`` Do stop , you naughty , fretty baby .
I 'm tired of your screaming , and it 's high time you go to sleep .
Bless me !
what 's Miss Shadow do with her baby ? ''
say Polly .
Miss Shadow take out the big pin and lay it away , put on a dry bib , and give her baby a nice ivory ring to bite ; then begin to dance up and down the room , till the shadowy baby clap it hand and kick delightedly .
Polly laugh , and do the same , feel sorry she have be so pettish .
Presently both baby grow quiet , go to sleep , and be lay in the cradle .
`` Now , I hope we shall rest a little , '' say Polly , stretch her arm .
But , no : down sit the shadow , and begin to sew , make her needle fly like a real little seamstress .
`` Oh , dear ! ''
groan Polly .
`` I promise to hem those handkerchief for Ned , and so I must ; but I do think handkerchief be the most pokey thing in the world to sew .
