I dare say you think you can sew fast than I can .
Just wait a bit , and see what I can do , miss , '' she say to the shadow .
It take some time to find her thimble and needle and spool , for Polly be n't a very neat little girl ; but she get settle at last , and stitch away a if bent on beat her dumb friend .
Little Will 's shadow go up to the nursery , and stop before a basin of water .
`` Oh !
ah !
ai n't this drefful ? ''
cry Will , with a shiver ; for he know he 'd get to have his face wash , because he would n't have it do properly when he get up , but run away .
Now , Will be a good child ; but this one thing be his great trouble , and sometimes he could n't bear it .
Jane be so rough .
She let soap get in his eye , and water run down his neck , and she pinch his nose when she wipe him , and brush his hair so hard that really it be dreadful ; and even a big boy would have find it hard to bear .
He shiver and sigh : but Jane come in ; and , when he saw that the shadow stand still and take the scrubbing like a little hero , he try to do the same , and succeed so well that Jane actually pat his head and call him `` a deary ; '' which be something new , for old Nurse Jane be always very busy and rather cross .
Feeling that nothing worse could possibly happen to him , Will run after his shadow , a it flit away into the barn , and begin to fee the chicken .
`` There , now !
I forgetted all about my chickeys , and the shadow ` membered 'em ; and I 'm glad of it , '' say Will , scatter dab of meal and water to the chirping , downy little creature who peck and flutter at his foot .
Little shadow hunt for egg , drive the turkey out of the garden , and pick a basket of chip : then it go to play with Sammy , a neighbor 's child ; for , be a small shadow , it have n't many job to do , and plenty of active play be good for it .
Sammy be a rough little boy and rather selfish : so , when they play ball , he want to throw all the time ; and , when Will object , he grow angry and strike him .
The blow do n't hurt Will 's cheek much , but it do his little feeling ; and he lift his hand to strike back , when he saw his shadow go and kiss Sammy 's shadow .
All his anger be go in a minute , and he just put his arm round Sammy 's neck and kiss him .
This kiss for a blow make him so ashamed that he begin to cry , and could n't be comfort till he have give Will his best marble and a ride on his pony .
About an hour before dinner , the three shadow and the child meet in the garden , and have a grand game of play , after they have tell each other what they have be do since they part .
Now , the shadow do n't forget baby even then , but get out the wagon , and Miss Baby , all fresh from her nap , sit among her pillow like a queen , while Ned be horse , Polly footman , and Will driver ; and in this way she travel all round the garden and barn , up the lane and down to the brook , where she be much delight with the water sparkle along and the fine splash of the stone they throw in .
When the dinner-bell rang , mamma saw four clean , rosy face and four smooth head at the table ; for the shadow-children make themselves neat , without be tell .
Every one be merry and hungry and good-natured .
Even poor baby forget her teeth , and play a regular rub-a-dub with her spoon on her mug , and try to tell about the fine thing she saw on her drive .
The child say nothing about the new play , and no one observe the queer action of their shadow but themselves .
They saw that there be no gobbling , or stretch over , or spill of thing , among the shadow ; but that they wait to be help , serve others first , and eat tidily , which be a great improvement upon the usual state of thing .
It be Saturday afternoon : the day be fine , and mamma tell them they could go for a holiday frolic in the wood .
`` Do n't go to the pond , and be home early , '' she say .
`` Yes , mamma ; we 'll remember , '' they answer , a they scamper away to get ready .
`` We shall go through the village , and Mary King will be look out ; so I shall wear my best hat .
Mamma wo n't see me , if I slip down the back way ; and I do so want Mary to know that my hat be pretty than hers , '' say Polly , up in her little room .
Now Polly be rather vain , and like to prink ; so she get out the new hat , and spend some time in smooth her braid and put on her blue ribbon .
But when all be ready , and the boy get impatient , she find her shadow , with a sun-bonnet on , stand by the door , a if to prevent her go out .
`` You tiresome thing !
do you mean that I must n't wear my hat , but that old bonnet ? ''
ask Polly .
The shadow nod and beckon , and pat it head , a if it be all right .
`` I wish I have n't promise to do a you do ; then I could do a I like , and not make a fright of myself , '' say Polly , rather sulkily , a she put away the hat , and tie on the old bonnet with a jerk .
Once out in the lovely sunshine , she soon forget the little disappointment ; and , a they do n't go through the village , but by a green lane , where she find some big blackberry , she be quite content .
Polly have a basket to hold fruit or flower , Ned his jackknife , and Will a long stick on which he ride , fancy that this sort of horse would help his short leg along ; so they pick , whittle , and trot their way to the wood , find all manner of interesting thing on the road .
The wood be full of pleasant sight and sound ; for wild rose bloom all along the path , fern and scarlet berry fill the little dell , squirrel chatter , bird sing , and pine whisper musically overhead .
`` I 'm go to stop here and rest , and make a wreath of these pretty wild rose for baby : it 's her birthday , and it will please mamma , '' say Polly , sit down on a mound of moss , with a lapful of flower .
`` I 'm go to cut a fishing-pole , and will be back in a minute . ''
And Ned go crash into the thick part of the wood .
`` I shall see where that rabbit go to , and maybe I 'll find some berry , '' say Will , trot down the path the wild rabbit have go .
The sound of the boy ' step die away , and Polly be wonder how it would seem to live all alone in the wood , when a little girl come trudging by , with a great pail of berry on her arm .
She be a poor child : her foot be bare , her gown be rag , she wear an old shawl over her head , and walk a if lame .
Polly sit behind the fern , and the child do not see her till Polly call out .
The sudden sound startle her ; and she drop her pail , spill the berry all over the path .
The little girl begin to cry , and Polly to laugh , say , in a scornful tone : `` How silly to cry for a few berry ! ''
`` I 've be all day pick 'em , '' say the girl ; `` and I 'm so tired and hungry ; 'cause I do n't dare to go home till my pail be full , -- mother scold if I do , -- and now they 're all spoilt .
Oh , dear !
dear me ! ''
And she cry so hard that great tear fell on the moss .
Polly be sorry now , and sit look at her till she saw her shadow down on it knee , pick up the berry ; then it seem to fold it little handkerchief round the girl 's bruised foot , and give her something from it pocket .
Polly jump up and imitate the kind shadow , even to give the great piece of gingerbread she have bring for fear she should be hungry .
`` Take this , '' she say gently .
`` I 'm sorry I frighten you .
Here be the berry all pick up , and none the bad for fall in the grass .
If you 'll take them to the white house on the hill , my mamma will buy them , and then your mother wo n't scold you . ''
`` Oh , thank you , miss !
It 's ever so good .
I 'll take the berry to your mother , and bring her more whenever she like , '' say the child gratefully , a she walk away munch the gingerbread , and smile till there be little rainbow in her tear .
Meanwhile Ned have poke about in the bush , look for a good pole .
Presently he saw a willow down by the pond , and think that would give him a nice , smooth pole .
He forget his promise , and down he go to the pond ; where he cut his stick , and be whittle the end , when he saw a boat by the shore .
It be untied , and oar lay in it , a if wait for some one to come and row out .
`` I 'll just take a little pull across , and get those cardinal-flowers for Polly , '' he say ; and go to the boat .
He get in , and be about to push off , when he saw his shadow stand on the shore .
`` Do n't be a fool ; get in , and come along , '' he say to it , remember his promise now , but decide to break it , and ask pardon afterwards .
But the shadow shook it head ; point to the swift stream that run between the bank , the rock and mud on the opposite side , and the leaky boat itself .
`` I ai n't afraid : mamma wo n't mind , if I tell her I 'm sorry ; and it will be such fun to row alone .
Be a good fellow , and let me go , '' say Ned , beckoning .
But the shadow would not stir , and Ned be oblige to mind .
He do so very reluctantly , and scold the shadow well a he go back to Polly ; though all the time he felt he be do right , and know he should be glad afterwards .
Will trot after the rabbit , but do n't find it ; he find a bird 's - nest instead with four little bird in it .
He have an empty cage at home , and long for something to put in it ; for kitten do n't like it , and caterpillar and beetlebugs get away .
He choose the big bird , and , hold him carefully , walk away to find Polly .
The poor mother-bird chirped and flutter in great distress ; but Will keep on till his little shadow come before him , and try to make him turn back .
`` No , no , I want him , '' say Will .
`` I wo n't hurt him , and his mother have three left : she wo n't mind if I take one . ''
Here the mother-bird chirp so loud it be impossible to help see that she do care very much ; and the shadow stamp it foot and wave it hand , a if order the young robber to carry back the baby-bird .
Will stand still , and think a minute ; but his little heart be a very kind one , and he soon turn about , say pleasantly : `` Yes , it be naughty , and I wo n't do it .
I 'll ask mamma to get me a canary , and will let this birdie stay with his brother . ''
The shadow pat him on the shoulder , and seem to be delight a Will put the bird in the nest and walk on , feel much happy than if he have keep it .
A bush of purple berry grow by the path , and Will stop to pick some .
He do n't know what they be , and mamma have often tell him never to eat strange thing .
But they smelt so good , and look so nice , he could n't resist , and lift one to his mouth , when little shadow motion for him to stop .
`` Oh , dear !
you do n't let me do any thing I want to , '' sigh Will .
`` I shall ask Polly if I tar n't eat these ; and , if she say I may , I shall , so now . ''
He run off to ask Polly ; but she say they be poisonous , and beg him to throw them away .
`` Good little shadow , to keep me safe ! ''
cry Will .
`` I like you ; and I 'll mind good next time , 'cause you be always right . ''
The shadow seem to like this , and bob about so comically it make Will laugh till his eye be full of tear .
Ned come back , and they go on , have grand time in the wood .
They find plenty of berry to fill the basket ; they swing down on slender birch , and get roll of white bark for canoe ; they saw all sort of wild-wood insect and bird ; and frolic till they be tire .
As they cross a field , a cow suddenly put down her head and ran at them , a if she be afraid they mean to hurt her calf .
All turn , and run as fast a they could toward the wall ; but poor Will in his fright tumble down , and lay screaming .
Ned and Polly have reach the wall , and , look back , saw that their shadow have not follow .
Ned 's stood before Will , brandish his pole ; and Polly 's be flap a shadowy sun-bonnet with all it might .
As soon a they saw that , back they go , -- Ned to threaten till he break his pole , and Polly to flap till the string come off .
As if anxious to do it part , the bonnet fly up in the air , and come down lit on the cross cow 's head ; which so astonish her that she run away as hard a she could pelt .
`` Was n't that funny ? ''
say Will , when they have tumble over the wall , and lay laugh in the grass on the safe side .
`` I 'm glad I wear the old bonnet ; for I suppose my best hat would have go just the same , '' say Polly thankfully .
`` The calf do n't know it own mother with that thing on , '' laugh Ned .
`` How brave and kind you be to come back and save me !
I 'd have be deaded if you have n't , '' say Will , look at his brother and sister with his little face full of grateful admiration .
They turn towards home after this flurry , feel quite like hero .
When they come to the corner where two road meet , Ned propose they should take the river-road ; for , though the long , it be much the pleasantest .
`` We sha n't be home at supper-time , '' say Polly .
`` You wo n't be able to do your job , Ned , nor I mine , and Will 's chicken will have to go to bed hungry . ''
`` Never mind : it 's a holiday , so let 's enjoy it , and not bother , '' answer Ned .
`` We promise mamma we 'd come home early , '' say Will .
They stand look at the two road , -- one sandy , hot , and hilly ; the other green and cool and level , along the river-side .
They all choose the pleasant path , and walk on till Ned cry out , `` Why , where be our shadow ? ''
They look behind , before , and on either side ; but nowhere could they see them .
`` They be with u at the corner , '' say Will .
`` Let 's run back , and try to find them , '' say Polly .
`` No , let 'em go : I 'm tired of mind mine , and do n't care if I never see it again , '' say Ned .
`` Do n't say so ; for I remember hear about a man who sell his shadow , and then get into lot of trouble because he have none .
We promise to follow them , and we must , '' say Polly .
`` I wish , '' begin Ned in a pet ; but Polly clap her hand over his mouth , say : `` Pray , do n't wish now ; for it may come to pass a the man 's wish in the fairy tale do , and the black pudding fly up and stick tight to his wife 's nose . ''
This make Ned laugh , and they all turn back to the corner .
Looking up the hilly road , they saw the three shadow trudge along , a if bent on get home in good time .
Without say a word , the child follow ; and , when they get to the garden gate , they all say at once : `` Are n't you glad you come ? ''
Under the elm-tree stand a pretty tea-table , cover with bread and butter , custard , and berry , and in the middle a fine cake with sugar-roses on the top ; and mamma and baby , all nicely dress , be wait to welcome them to the birthday feast .
Polly crown the little queen , Ned give her a willow whistle he have make , and Will some pretty , bright pebble he have find ; and Miss Baby be as happy a a bird , with her treasure .
A pleasant supper-time ; then the small duty for each one ; and then the go-to-bed frolic .
The nursery be a big room , and in the even a bright wood fire always burn there for baby .
Mamma sit before it , softly rub baby 's little rosy limb before she go to bed , sing and tell story meanwhile to the three child who prance about in their long nightgown .
This evening they have a gay time ; for the shadow amuse them by all sort of antic , and keep them laugh till they be tire .
As they sit rest on the big sofa , they hear a soft , sweet voice singing .
It be n't mamma ; for she be only talk to baby , and this voice sing a real song .
Presently they saw mamma 's shadow on the wall , and find it be the shadow-mother singing to the shadow-children .
They listen intently , and this be what they hear : `` Little shadow , little shadow , Dancing on the chamber wall , While I sit beside the hearthstone Where the red flame rise and fall .
Caps and nightgown , cap and nightgown , My three antic shadow wear ; And no sound they make in playing , For the six small foot be bare .
`` Dancing gayly , dance gayly , To and fro all together , Like a family of daisy Blown about in windy weather ; Nimble fairy , nimble fairy , Playing prank in the warm glow , While I sing the nursery ditties Childish phantom love and know .
`` Now what happen , now what happen ?
One small shadow 's tumble down : I can see it on the carpet , Softly rub it hurt crown .
No one whimper , no one whimper ; A brave-hearted sprite be this : See !
the others offer comfort In a silent , shadowy kiss .
`` Hush !
they 're creep ; hush !
they 're creep , Up about my rocking-chair : I can feel their loving finger Clasp my neck and touch my hair .
Little shadow , little shadow , Take me captive , hold me tight , As they climb and cling and whisper , ` Mother dear , good night !
good night ! ' ''
As the song end , the real child , as well a the shadow , lovingly kiss mamma , and say `` Good-night ; '' then go away into their room , say their prayer , and nestle down into their bed .
Ned sleep alone in the room next that which Polly and Will have ; and , after lie quiet a little while , he call out softly : `` I say , Polly , be you asleep ? ''
`` No : I 'm think what a queer day we 've have , '' answer Polly .
`` It 's be a good day , and I 'm glad we try our wish ; for the shadow show u , as well a they could , what we ought to do and be .
I sha n't forget it , shall you ? ''
say Ned .
`` No : I 'm much oblige for the lesson . ''
`` So be I , '' call out Will , in a very earnest , but rather a sleepy , little voice .
`` I wonder what mamma will say , when we tell her about it , '' say Ned .
`` And I wonder if our shadow will come back to u at midnight , and follow u a they use to do , '' add Polly .
`` I shall be very careful where I lead my shadow ; 'cause he 's a good little one , and set me a righter zarmple than ever I do him , '' say Will , and then drop asleep .
The others agree with him , and resolve that their shadow should not be ashamed of them .
All be fast asleep ; and no one but the moon saw the shadow come steal back at midnight , and , have dance about the little bed , vanish a the clock strike twelve .
VI .
POPPY 'S PRANKS .
She be n't a wilfully naughty child , this harum-scarum Poppy , but very thoughtless and very curious .
She want to see every thing , do every thing , and go every where : she fear nothing , and so be continually get into scrape .
Her prank begin early ; for , when she be about four , her mamma one day give her a pair of green shoe with bright button .
Poppy thought there never be any thing so splendid , and immediately want to go to walk .
But mamma be busy , and Poppy could n't go alone any farther than the garden .
She show her shoe to the servant , the cat , the dove , and the flower ; and then open the gate that the people in the street might see the trim little foot she be so proud of .
Now Poppy have be forbidden to go out ; but , when she saw Kitty Allen , her neighbor , play ball down the street , she forget every thing but the desire to show her new shoe ; and away she go march primly along as vain a a little peacock , a she watch the bright button twinkle , and hear the charming creak .
Kitty saw her coming ; and , be an ill-natured little girl , take no notice , but call out to her brother Jack : `` Ai n't some folk grand ?
If I could n't have red shoe for my best , I would n't have any , would you ? ''
They both laugh , and this hurt Poppy 's feeling dreadfully .
She toss her head , and try to turn up her nose ; but , it be so very small , it could n't be very scornful .
She say nothing , but walk gravely by , a if she be go on an errand , and have n't hear a word .
Round the corner she go , think she would wait till Kitty be go ; a she do n't like to pass again , fear Jack might say something equally try .
An organ-man with a monkey be play near by ; and Poppy be soon so busy listen to the music , and watch the sad-looking monkey , that she forget home , shoe , and Kitty altogether .
She follow the man a long way ; and , when she turn to go back , she take the wrong street , and find herself by the park .
Being fond of dandelion , Poppy go in , and gather her hand full , enjoy herself immensely ; for Betsy , the maid , never let her play in the pond , or roll down the hill , or make dirt-pies , and now she do all these thing , besides play with strange child and talk with any one she please .
If she have not have her luncheon just before she start , she would have be very hungry ; for dinner-time come , without her know it .
By three o'clock , she begin to think it be time to go home , and boldly start off to find it .
But poor little Poppy do n't know the way , and go all wrong .
She be very tired now , and hot and hungry , and want to see mamma , and wonder why she do n't come to the brown house with the white garden-gate .
On and on she go , up street and down , amuse herself with look in the shop-windows , and sit to rest on doorstep .
Once she ask a pleasant-faced little girl to show her the way home ; but , a she do n't know in what street it be , and say her father 's name be `` papa , '' the girl could n't help her : so she give her a bun and go away .
Poppy ate her bun , and begin to wonder what would become of her ; for night be come on , and there do n't seem to be any prospect of find mamma or home or bed .
Her courage be all go now ; and , come to a quiet place , she sit down on some high step , and cry till her little `` hankchif , '' a she call it , be all wet .
Nobody mind her : and she felt very forlorn till a big black dog come by , and seem to understand the matter entirely ; for he smelt of her face , lick her hand , and then lay down by her with such a friendly look in his brown eye that Poppy be quite comfort .
She tell him her story , pat his big head ; and then , be fairly tire out , lay her wet cheek on his soft back , and fell fast asleep .
It be quite dark when she wake ; but a lamp be light near by , and stand under it be a man ring a great bell .
Poppy sit up , and wonder if anybody 's supper be ready .
The man have a paper ; and , when people stop at the sound of the bell , he read in a loud voice : `` Lost !
a little girl , four year old ; curly brown hair , blue eye ; have on a white frock and green shoe ; call herself Poppy . ''
He get no farther ; for a little voice cry out of the dark , in a tone of surprise : `` Why , dats me ! ''
The people all turn to look ; and the big man put his bell in his pocket , take her up very kindly , and say he 'd carry her home .
`` Is it far away ? ''
ask Poppy , with a little sob .
`` Yes , my dear ; but I be go to give you some supper fust , along of my little girl .
I live close by ; and , when we 've have a bite , we 'll go find your ma . ''
Poppy be so tired and hungry , she be glad to find herself take care of , and let the man do a he like .
He take her to a funny little house , and his wife give her bread and molasses on a new tin plate with letter all round the edge .
Poppy think it very fine , and enjoy her supper , though the man 's little girl star at her all the time with eye as blue a her mug .
While she eat , the man send word to her father that she be find ; and , when both papa and mamma come hurry in all out of breath with joy , there sat Miss Poppy talk merrily , with her face well daub with molasses , her gown torn , her hand very dirty , and her shoe -- ah , the pretty new shoe !
-- all spoil with mud and dust , scratch , and half wear out , the button dull , and the color quite go .
No one care for it that night ; for little runaway be kiss and pet , and take home to her own cosey bed as tenderly a if she have do nothing naughty , and never frighten her parent out of their wit in her life .
But the next day , -- dear me !
what a sad time it be , to be sure !
When Poppy wake up , there hang the spoilt shoe over the mantle-piece ; and , as soon a she be dress , papa come in with a long cord , one end of which he tie round Poppy 's waist , and the other to the arm of the sofa .
`` I 'm very sorry to have to tie you up , like a little dog ; but I must , or you will forget , and run away again , and make mamma ill . ''
Then he go away without his morning kiss , and Poppy be so very unhappy she could hardly eat her breakfast .
She felt well by and by , and try to play ; but the cord keep pull her back .
She could n't get to the window ; and , when she hear mamma pass the door , she try to run and meet her , but have to stop halfway , for the cord jerk her over .
Cousin Fanny come up , but Poppy be so ashamed to be tie that she creep under the sofa and hid .
All day she be a prisoner , and be a very miserable little girl ; but at night she be untied , and , when mamma take her in her lap for the first time that day , Poppy hold her fast , and sob very penitently -- `` O mamma !
I drefful sorry I runned away .
Fordive me one time more , and I never will adain ; '' and she never do .
Two or three year after this , Poppy go to live in the country , and try some new prank .
One day she go with her sister Nelly to see a man plough , for that sort of thing be new to her .
While the man work , she saw him take out a piece of something brown , and bite off a bit .
`` What 's that ? ''
ask Poppy .
`` Tobaccer , '' say the man .
`` Is it nice ? ''
ask Poppy .
`` Prime , '' say the man .
`` Could you let me taste it ? ''
ask curious Poppy .
`` It will make you sick , '' say the man , laugh .
`` It do n't make you sick .
I 'd like to try , '' say Poppy , nothing daunt .
He give her a piece ; and Poppy eat it , though it do n't taste good at all .
She do it because Cy , her favorite playfellow , tell her she 'd die if she do , and try to frighten her .
`` You dars n't eat any more , '' he say .
`` Yes , I dare .
See if I do n't . ''
And Poppy take another piece , just to show how brave she be .
Silly little Poppy !
`` I ai n't sick , and I sha n't die , so now . ''
And Poppy prance about as briskly a ever .
But the man shake his head , Nelly watch her anxiously , and Cy keep say : `` Ai n't you sick yet , say ? ''
For a little while Poppy felt all right ; but presently she grow rather pale , and begin to look rather pensive .
She stop run , and walk slow and slow , while her eye get dizzy , and her hand and foot very cold .
`` Ai n't you sick now , say ? ''
repeat Cy ; and Poppy try to answer , `` Oh , dear !
no ; '' but a dreadful feeling come over her , and she could only shake her head , and hold on to Nelly .
`` Better lay down a spell , '' say the man , look a little troubled .
`` I do n't wish to dirty my clean frock , '' say Poppy faintly , a she glance over the wide-ploughed field , and long for a bit of grass to drop on .
She keep on bravely for another turn ; but suddenly stop , and , quite regardless of the clean pink gown , drop down in a furrow , look so white and queer that Nelly begin to cry .
Poppy lay a minute , then turn to Cy , and say very solemnly : `` Cy , run home , and tell my mother I 'm die . ''
Away rush Cy in a great fright , and burst upon Poppy 's mamma , exclaim breathlessly : `` O ma'am !
Poppy 's be and eat a lot of tobacco ; and she 's sick , layin ' in the field ; and she say ` Come quick , 'cause she 's dyin . ' ''
`` Mercy on u !
what will happen to that child next ? ''
cry poor mamma , who be use to Poppy 's mishap .
Papa be away , and there be no carriage to bring Poppy home in ; so mamma take the little wheelbarrow , and trundle away to get the suffering Poppy .
She could n't speak when they get to her ; and , only stop to give the man a lecture , mamma pick up her silly little girl , and the procession move off .
First come Cy , as grave a a sexton ; then the wheelbarrow with Poppy , white and limp and speechless , all in a bunch ; then mamma , look amuse , anxious and angry ; then Nelly , weep a if her tender heart be entirely break ; while the man watch them , with a grin , say to himself : `` Twar n't my fault .
The child be a reg ` lar fool to swaller it . ''
Poppy be dreadfully sick all night , but next day be ready for more adventure and experiment .
She swing on the garret stair , and tumble down , nearly break her neck .
She rub her eye with red pepper , to see if it really would make them smart , a Cy say ; and be lead home quite blind and roar with pain .
She get into the pigsty to catch a young piggy , and be take out in a sad state of dirt .
She slip into the brook , and be half drown ; break a window and her own head , swing a little flat-iron on a string ; drop baby in the coal-hod ; bury her doll , and spoil her ; cut off a bit of her finger , chop wood ; and break a tooth , try to turn heel over head on a haycock .
These be only a few of her prank , but one be nearly her last .
She want to go bare-footed , a the little country boy and girl do ; but mamma be n't willing , and Poppy be much afflict .
`` It do n't hurt Cy , and it wo n't hurt me , just for a little while , '' she say .
`` Say no more , Poppy .
I never wish to see you barefooted , '' reply mamma .
`` Well , you need n't : I 'll go and do it in the barn , '' mutter Poppy , a she walk away .
Into the barn she go , and play country girl to her heart 's content , in spite of Nelly 's warning .
Nelly never get into scrape , be a highly virtuous young lady ; but she enjoy Poppy 's prank , and wept over her misfortune with sisterly fidelity .
`` Now I 'll be a bear , and jump at you a you go by , '' say Poppy , when they be tire of play steam-engine with the old winnowing machine .
So she get up on a beam ; and Nelly , with a peck measure on her head for a hat , and a stick for a gun , go bear-hunting , and bang away at the swallow , the barrel , and the hencoop , till the bear be ready to eat her .
Presently , with a loud roar , the bear leap ; but Nelly be n't eat that time , for Poppy cry out with pain : `` Oh !
I jump on a pitchfork , and it 's in my foot !
Take it out !
take it out ! ''
Poor little foot !
There be a deep purple hole in the sole , and the blood come , and Poppy faint away , and Nelly scream , and mamma ran , and the neighbor rush in , and there be such a flurry .
Poppy be soon herself again , and lay on the sofa , with Nelly and Cy to amuse her .
`` What do the doctor say to mamma in the other room about me ? ''
whisper Poppy , feel very important at have such a bustle make on her account .
Nelly sniff , but say nothing ; Cy , however , speak up briskly : `` He say you might have lockjaw . ''
`` Is that bad ? ''
ask Poppy gravely .
`` Oh , ai n't it , though !
Your mouth shut up , and you ca n't open it ; and you have fit and die . ''
`` Always ? ''
say Poppy , look scar , and feeling of her mouth . ''
` Most always , I guess .
That 's why your ma cry , and Nelly keep kissin ' you . ''
Cy felt sorry , but rather enjoy the excitement , and be sure , that , if any one ever could escape die , it would be Poppy , for she always `` come alive '' again after her bad mishap .
She look very solemn for a few minute , and keep opening and shut her mouth to see if it be n't stiff .
Presently she say , in a serious tone and with a pensive air : `` Nelly , I 'll give you my bead-ring : I sha n't want it any more .
And Cy may have the little horse : he lose his tail ; but I put on the lamb 's tail , and he be as good a ever .
I wish to give away my thing ` fore I die ; and , Nelly , wo n't you bring me the scissors ? ''
`` What for ? ''
say Nelly , sniff more than ever .
`` To cut off my hair for mamma .
She 'll want it , and I like to cut thing . ''
Nelly get the scissors ; and Poppy cut away all she could reach , give direction about her property while she snip .
`` I wish papa to have my picture and my piece of poetry I make .
Give baby my dolly and the quacking duck .
Tell Billy , if he want my collection of bright button , he can have 'em ; and give Hattie the yellow plaster dog , with my love . ''
Here mamma come in with a poultice , and could n't help laughing , though tear stand in her eye , a she saw Poppy 's cropped head and hear her last wish .
`` I do n't think I shall lose my little girl yet , so we wo n't talk of it .
But Poppy must keep quiet , and let Nelly wait on her for a few day . ''
`` Are fits bad , mamma ?
and do it hurt much to die ? ''
ask Poppy thoughtfully .
`` If people be good while they live , it be not hard to die , dear , '' say mamma , with a kiss ; and Poppy hug her , say softly : `` Then I 'll be very good ; so I wo n't mind , if the jawlock do come . ''
And Poppy be good , -- oh , dreadfully good !
for a week .
Quite an angel be Poppy ; so meek and gentle , so generous and obedient , you really would n't have know her .
She love everybody , forgive her playmate all their sin against her , let Nelly take such of her precious treasure a she like , and pensively hop baby would remember her when she be go .
She hop about with a crutch , and felt a if she be an object of public interest ; for all the old lady send to know how she be , the child look at her with respectful awe a one set apart and doom to fit , and Cy continually beg to know if her mouth be stiff .
Poppy do n't die , though she get all ready for it ; and felt rather disappointed when the foot heal , the jaw remain as active a ever , and the fit do n't come .
I think it do her good ; for she never forget that week , and , though she be near die several time after , she never be so fit to go a she be then .
`` Burney 's make jelly : let 's go and get our scraping , '' say Poppy to Nellie once , when mamma be away .
But Burney be busy and cross , and cook be not as patient a mother ; so when the child appear , each arm with a spoon , and demand their usual feast , she would n't hear of it , and order them off .
`` But we only want the scraping of the pan , Burney : mamma always let u have them , when we help her make jelly ; do n't she , Nelly ? ''
say Poppy , try to explain the case .
`` Yes ; and make u our little potful too , '' add Nelly , persuasively .
`` I do n't want your help ; so be off .
Your ma can fuss with your pot , if she choose .
I 've no time . '' ''
I think Burney 's the cross woman in the world .
It 's mean to eat all the scraping herself ; be n't it Nelly ? ''
say Poppy , very loud , a the cook shut the door in their face .
`` Never mind : I know how to pay her , '' she add , in a whisper , a they sit on the stair bewail their wrong .
`` She 'll put her old jelly in the big closet , and lock the door ; but we can climb the plum tree , and get in at the window , when she take her nap . ''
`` Should we dare to eat any ? ''
ask Nelly , timid , but long for the forbidden fruit . ''
I should ; just as much a ever I like .
It 's mamma 's jelly , and she wo n't mind .
I do n't care for old cross Burney , '' say Poppy , slide down the banister by way of soothe her ruffled spirit .
So when Burney go to her room after dinner , the two rogue climb in at the window ; and , each take a jar , sit on the shelf , dip in their finger and revel rapturously .
But Burney be n't asleep , and , hear a noise below , creep down to see what mischief be go on .
Pausing in the entry to listen , she hear whisper , clatter of glass , and smacking of lip in the big closet ; and in a moment knew that her jelly be lose .
She try the door with her key ; but sly Poppy have bolt it on the inside , and , feel quite safe , defied Burney from among the jelly-pots , entirely reckless of consequence .
Short-sighted Poppy !
she forget Cy ; but Burney do n't , and send him to climb in at the window , and undo the door .
Feeling hurt that the young lady have n't ask him to the feast , Cy harden his heart against them , and deliver them up to the enemy , regardless of Poppy 's threat and Nelly 's prayer .
`` Poppy propose it , she break the jar , and I do n't eat much .
O Burney !
do n't hurt her , please , but let me ` splain it to mamma when she come , '' sob Nelly , a Burney seize Poppy , and give her a good shaking .
`` You go wash your face , Miss Nelly , and leave this naughty , naughty child to me , '' say Burney ; and take Poppy , kick and scream , into the little library , where she -- oh , dreadful to relate !
-- give her a good spanking , and lock her up .
Mamma never whip , and Poppy be in a great rage at such an indignity .
The minute she be leave alone , she look about to see how she could be revenge .
A solar lamp stand on the table ; and Poppy coolly tip it over , with a fine smash , call out to Burney that she 'd have to pay for it , that mamma would be very angry , and that she , Poppy , be go to spoil every thing in the room .
But Burney be go , and no one come near her .
She kick the paint off the door , rattle the latch , call Burney a `` pig , '' and Cy `` a badder boy than the man who smother the little prince in the Tower . ''
Poppy be very fond of that story , and often play it with Nelly and the doll .
Having relieve her feeling in this way , Poppy rest , and then set about amuse herself .
Observing that the spilt oil make the table shine , she take her handkerchief and polish up the furniture , a she have see the maid do .
`` Now , that look nice ; and I know mamma will be pleased 'cause I 'm so tidy , '' she say , survey her work with pride , when she have thoroughly grease every table , chair , picture-frame , book-back , and ornament in the room .
Plenty of oil still remain ; and Poppy finish off by oil her hair , till it shone finely , and smelt -- dear me , how it do smell !
If she have be a young whale , it could n't have be bad .
Poppy be n't particular about smell ; but she get some in her mouth , and do n't like the taste .
There be no water to wash in ; and her hand , face , and pinafore be in a high state of grease .
She be rather lonely too ; for , though mamma have get home , she do n't come to let Poppy out : so the young rebel think it be about time to surrender .
She could write pretty well , and be fond of send penitent note to mamma , after be naughty : for mamma always answer them so kindly , and be so forgiving , that Poppy 's naughty mood be conquer by them sooner than by any punishment ; and Poppy keep the note carefully in a little cover , even after she be grow up .
There be pen , ink , and paper in the room ; so , after various trial , Poppy write her note : -- `` dear Mamma .
`` i be sorry i Took bernys gelli .
i have brake The lamP .
The oyl maks A bad smel .
i tHink i wil Bee sik iF i stay HeRe anny More .
i LoVe yoU -- your Trying To Bee GooD popy . ''
When she have finish , she lower her note by a string , and bob it up and down before the parlor window till Nelly saw and take it in .
Every one laugh over it ; for , besides the bad spelling and the funny period , it be cover with oil-spots , blot , and tear mark ; for Poppy get tender-hearted toward the end , and cry a few very repentant tear when she say , `` I love you ; your trying-to-be-good Poppy . ''
Mamma go up at once , and order no further punishment , but a thorough scrubbing ; which Poppy underwent very meekly , though Betsey put soap in her eye , pull her hair , and scold all the time .
They be not allow any jelly for a long while ; and Cy tease Poppy about her hair-oil till the joke be quite wear out , and even cross Burney be satisfy with the atonement .
When Poppy be eight , she get so very wild that no one could manage her but mamma , and she be ill ; so Poppy be send away to grandpa 's for a visit .
Now , grandpa be a very stately old gentleman , and every one treat him with great respect ; but Poppy be n't at all afraid , and ask all manner of impolite question .
`` Grandpa , why do n't you have any hair on the top of your head ? ''
-- `` O grandpa !
you do snore so loud when you take nap ! ''
-- `` What make you turn out your foot so , when you walk ? ''
and such thing .
If grandpa have n't be the best-natured old gentleman in the world , he would n't have like this : but he only laugh at Poppy , especially when she speak of his leg ; for he be rather proud of them , and always wore long black silk stocking , and tell every one that the leg be so handsome an artist put them in a picture of General Washington ; which be quite true , a any one may see when they look at the famous picture in Boston .
Well , Poppy behave herself respectably for a day or two ; but the house be rather dull , she miss Nelly , want to run in the street , and long to see mamma .
She amuse herself as well a she could with picture-books , patchwork , and the old cat ; but , not be a quiet , proper , little Rosamond sort of a child , she get tired of hem neat pocket-handkerchief , and put her needle carefully away when she have do .
She want to romp and shout , and slide down the banister , and riot about ; so , when she could n't be quiet another minute , she go up into a great empty room at the top of the house , and cut up all sort of caper .
Her great delight be to lean out of the window as far a she could , and look at the people in the street , with her head upside down .
It be very dangerous , for a fall would have kill her ; but the danger be the fun , and Poppy hang out till her hand touch the ledge below , and her face be as red a any real poppy 's .
She be enjoy herself in this way one day , when an old gentleman , who live near , come home to dinner , and saw her .
`` What in the world be that hang out of the colonel 's upper window ? ''
say he , put on his spectacle .
`` Bless my soul !
that child will kill herself .
Hallo , there !
little girl ; get in this minute ! ''
he call to Poppy , flourish his hat to make her see him .
`` What for ? ''
answer Poppy , star at him without move an inch .
`` You 'll fall , and break your neck ! ''
scream the old gentleman .
`` Oh , no , I sha n't ! ''
return Poppy , much flatter by his interest , and hang out still far .
`` Stop that , instantly , or I 'll go in and inform the colonel ! ''
roar the old gentleman , get angry .
`` I do n't care , '' shout Poppy ; and she do n't , for she know grandpa be n't at home .
`` Little gipsy !
I 'll settle her , '' mutter the old man , bustle up to the step , and ring the bell , a if the house be on fire .
No one be in but the servant ; and , when he 'd tell old Emily what the matter be , she go up to `` settle '' Poppy .
But Poppy be already settle , demurely play with her doll , and look quite innocent .
Emily scold ; and Poppy promise never to do it again , if she might stay and play in the big room .
Being busy about dinner , Emily be glad to be rid of her , and leave her , to go and tell the old gentleman it be all right .
`` Ai n't they crosspatches ? ''
say Poppy to her doll .
`` Never mind , dear : you shall hang out , if I ca n't .
I guess the old man wo n't order you in , any way . ''
Full of this idea , Poppy take her long-suffering dolly , and , tie a string to her neck , dance her out of the window .
Now this dolly have be through a great deal .
Her head have be cut off -LRB- and put on again -RRB- ; she have be wash , bury , burnt , tear , soil , and bang about till she be a mournful object .
Poppy love her very much ; for she be two foot tall , and have once be very handsome : so her trial only endear her to her little mamma .
Away she go , skip and prance like mad , -- a funny sight , for Poppy have take off her clothes , and she have n't a hair on her head .
Poppy go to another window of the room for this performance , because in the opposite house live five or six child , and she think they would enjoy the fun .
So they do , and so do the other people ; for it be a boarding-house , and all the people be at home for dinner .
They come to the window , and look and laugh at dolly 's caper , and Poppy be in high feather at the success of her entertainment .
All of a sudden she saw grandpa come down the street , hand behind his back , foot turn out , gold-headed cane under his arm , and the handsome leg in the black silk stocking march along in the most stately manner .
Poppy whisk dolly in before grandpa saw her , and dodge down a he go by .
This make the people laugh again , and grandpa wonder what the joke be .
The minute he go in out flew dolly , dance more frantically than ever ; and the child shout so loud that grandpa go to see what the matter be .
The street be empty ; yet there stand the people , star out and laugh .
Yes ; they be actually look and laugh at his house ; and he do n't see what there be to laugh at in that highly respectable mansion .
He do n't like it ; and , clap on his hat , he go out to learn what the matter be .
He look over at the house , up at the sky , down at the ground , and through the street ; but nothing funny appear , for Poppy and dolly be hide again , and the old gentleman be puzzle .
He go in and sit down to watch , feel rather disturb .
Presently the fun begin again : the child clap their hand , the people laugh , and every one look over at the house , in what he think a very impertinent way .
This make him angry ; and out he rush a second time , say , a he march across the street : `` If those saucy young fellow be make game of me , I 'll soon stop it . ''
Up to the door he go , give a great pull at the bell , and , when the servant come , he demand why every one be laugh at his house .
One of the young men come and tell him , and ask him to come in and see the fun .
Poppy do n't see grandpa go in , for she hide , and when she look out he be go : so she boldly begin the dancing ; but , in the midst of a lively caper , dolly go bounce into the garden below , for the string fell from Poppy 's hand when she suddenly saw grandpa at the window opposite , laugh as heartily a any one at her prank .
She star at him in a great fright , and look so amazed that every one enjoyed that joke good than the other ; and poor Poppy do n't hear the last of it for a long time .
Her next performance be to fall into the pond on the Common .
She be drive hoop down the hill , and go so fast she could n't stop herself ; so splash into the water , hoop and all .
How dreadful it be to feel the cold wave go over her head , shut out the sun and air !
The ground be go , and she could find no place for her foot , and could only struggle and choke , and go down , down , with a loud roaring sound in her ear .
That would have be the end of Poppy , if a little black boy have n't jump in and pull her out .
She be sick and dizzy , and look like a drowned kitten ; but a kind lady take her home in a carriage .
After that mishap grandpa think he would n't keep her any longer , for fear she should come to some bad harm .
So Miss Poppy be send home , much to her delight and much to mamma 's also ; for no matter where she go , or how naughty she be , mamma be always glad to see the little wanderer back , and to forgive and forget all Poppy 's prank .
VII .
WHAT THE SWALLOWS DID .
A man lay on a pile of new-made hay , in a great barn , look up at the swallow who dart and twitter above him .
He envy the cheerful little creature ; for he be n't a happy man , though he have many friend , much money , and the beautiful gift of write song that everybody love to sing .
He have lose his wife and little child , and would not be comfort ; but live alone , and go about with such a gloomy face that no one like to speak to him .
He take no notice of friend and neighbor ; neither use his money for himself nor others ; find no beauty in the world , no happiness anywhere ; and write such sad song it make one 's heart ache to sing them .
As he lay alone on the sweet-smelling hay , with the afternoon sunshine streaming in , and the busy bird chirp overhead , he say sadly to himself : `` Happy swallow , I wish I be one of you ; for you have no pain nor sorrow , and your care be very light .
All summer you live gayly together ; and , when winter come , you fly away to the lovely South , unseparated still . ''
`` Neighbors , do you hear what that lazy creature down there be say ? ''
cry a swallow , peep over the edge of her nest , and address several others who sit on a beam near by .
`` We hear , Mrs . Skim ; and quite agree with you that he know very little about u and our affair , '' answer one of the swallow with a shrill chirp , like a scornful laugh .
`` We work hard than he do any day .
Did he build his own house , I should like to know ?
Does he get his daily bread for himself ?
How many of his neighbor do he help ?
How much of the world do he see , and who be the happier for his be alive ? ''
`` Cares indeed ! ''
cry another ; `` I wish he 'd undertake to fee and teach my brood .
Much he know about the anxiety of a parent . ''
And the little mother bustle away to get supper for the young one , whose bill be always gap wide .
`` Sorrows we have , too , '' softly say the fourth swallow .
`` He would not envy me , if he know how my nest fell , and all my child be kill ; how my dear husband be shoot , and my old mother die of fatigue on our spring journey from the South . ''
`` Dear neighbor Dart , he would envy you , if he know how patiently you bear your trouble ; how tenderly you help u with our little one ; how cheerfully you serve your friend ; how faithfully you love your lost mate ; and how trustfully you wait to meet him again in a lovely country than the South . ''
As Skim speak , she lean down from her nest to kiss her neighbor ; and , a the little beaks met , the other bird give a grateful and approve murmur , for Neighbor Dart be much beloved by all the inhabitant of Twittertown .
`` I , for my part , do n't envy him , '' say Gossip Wing , who be fond of speak her mind .
`` Men and woman call themselves superior being ; but , upon my word , I think they be vastly inferior to u .
Now , look at that man , and see how he waste his life .
There never be any one with a good chance for do good , and be happy ; and yet he mop and dawdle his time away most shamefully . ''
`` Ah !
he have have a great sorrow , and it be hard to be gay with a heavy heart , an empty home ; so do n't be too severe , Sister Wing . ''
And the white tie of the little widow 's cap be stir by a long sigh a Mrs. Dart glance up at the nook where her nest once stand .
`` No , my dear , I wo n't ; but really I do get out of patience when I see so much real misery which that man might help , if he 'd only forget himself a little .
It 's my opinion he 'd be much happy than he now be , wander about with a dismal face and a sour temper . ''
`` I quite agree with you ; and I dare say he 'd thank any one for tell him how he may find comfort .
Poor soul !
I wish he could understand me ; for I sympathize with him , and would gladly help him if I could . ''
And , a she speak , kind-hearted Widow Dart skim by him with a friendly chirp , which do comfort him ; for , be a poet , he could understand them , and lay listening , well please while the little gossip chatter on together .
`` I be so tie at home just now , that I know nothing of what be go on , except the bit of news Skim bring me ; so I enjoy your chat immensely .
I 'm interested in your view on this subject , and beg you 'll tell me what you 'd have that man do to better himself , '' say Mrs . Skim , settle herself on her egg with an attentive air .
`` Well , my dear , I 'll tell you ; for I 've see a deal of the world , and any one be welcome to my experience , '' reply Mrs. Wing , in an important manner ; for she be proud of her `` view , '' and very fond of talk .
`` In my daily flight about the place , I see a great deal of poverty and trouble , and often wish I could lend a hand .
Now , this man have plenty of money and time ; and he might do more good than I can tell , if he 'd only set about it .
Because he be what they call a poet be no reason he should go moan up and down , a if he have nothing to do but make song .
We sing , but we work also ; and be wise enough to see the necessity of both , thank goodness ! ''
`` Yes , indeed , we do , '' cry all the bird in a chorus ; for several more have stop to hear what be go on .
`` Now , what I say be this , '' continue Mrs. Wing impressively .
`` If I be that man , I 'd make myself useful at once .
There be poor little Will get more and more lame every day , because his mother ca n't send him where he can be cure .
A trifle of that man 's money would do it , and he ought to give it .
Old Father Winter be half starved , alone there in his miserable hovel ; and no one think of the good old man .
Why do n't that lazy creature take him home , and care for him , the little while he have to live ?
Pretty Nell be work day and night , to support her father , and be too proud to ask help , though her health and courage be go fast .
The man might make hers the gay heart alive , by a little help .
There in a lonely garret live a young man study his life away , long for book and a teacher .
The man have a library full , and might keep the poor boy from despair by a little help and a friendly word .
He mourn for his own lose baby : I advise him to adopt the orphan whom nobody will own , and who lie wail all day untended on the poor-house floor .
Yes : if he want to forget sorrow and find peace , let him fill his empty heart and home with such a these , and life wo n't seem dark to him any more . ''
`` Dear me !
how well you express yourself , Mrs. Wing ; it 's quite a pleasure to hear you ; and I heartily wish some person could hear you , it would do 'em a deal of good , '' say Mrs . Skim ; while her husband give an approving nod a he dive off the beam , and vanish through the open door .
`` I know it would comfort that man to do these thing ; for I have try the same cure in my small way , and find great satisfaction in it , '' begin little Madame Dart , in her soft voice ; but Mrs. Wing break in , say with a pious expression of countenance : `` I fly into church one day , and sit on the organ enjoy the music ; for every one be sing , and I join in , though I do n't know the air .
Opposite me be two great tablet with golden letter on them .
I can read a little , thanks to my friend , the learned raven ; and so I spell out some of the word .
One be , ` Love thy neighbor ; ' and a I sit there , look down on the people , I wonder how they could see those word week after week , and yet pay so little heed to them .
Goodness know , I do n't consider myself a perfect bird ; far from it ; for I know I be a poor , err fowl ; but I believe I may say I do love my neighbor , though I be ` an inferior creature . ' ''
And Mrs. Wing bridle up , a if she resent the phrase immensely .
`` Indeed you do , gossip , '' cry Dart and Skim ; for Wing be an excellent bird , in spite of the good opinion she have of herself .
`` Thank you : well , then , such be the know fact , I may give advice on the subject a one have authority ; and , if it be possible , I 'd give that man a bit of my mind . ''
`` You have , madam , you have ; and I shall not forget it .
Thank you , neighbor , and good night , '' say the man , a he leave the barn , with the first smile on his face which it have wear for many day .
`` Mercy on u !
I do believe the creature heard every thing we say , '' cry Mrs. Wing , nearly tumble off the beam , in her surprise .
`` He certainly do ; so I 'm glad I be guard in my remark , '' reply Mrs . Skim , laugh at her neighbor 's dismay .
`` Dear me !
dear me !
what do I say ? ''
cried Mrs. Wing , in a great twitter .
`` You speak with more than your usual bluntness , and some of your expression be rather strong , I must confess ; but I do n't think any harm will come of it .
We be of too little consequence for our criticism or opinion to annoy him , '' say Mrs. Dart consolingly .
`` I do n't know that , ma'am , '' return Mrs. Wing , sharply : for she be much ruffle and out of temper .
`` A cat may look at a king ; and a bird may teach a man , if the bird be the wise .
He may destroy my nest , and take my life ; but I feel that I have do my duty , and shall meet affliction with a firmness which will be an example to that indolent , ungrateful man . ''
In spite of her boasted firmness , Mrs. Wing drop her voice , and peep over the beam , to be sure the man be go before she call him names ; and then fly away , to discover what he mean to do about it .
For several day , there be much excitement in Twittertown ; for news of what have happen flew from nest to nest , and every bird be anxious to know what revenge the man would take for the impertinent remark which have be make about him .
Mrs. Wing be in a dreadful state of mind , expect an assault , and the destruction of her entire family .
Every one blame her .
Her husband lecture ; the young bird chirp , `` Chatterbox , chatterbox , '' a she pass ; and her best friend be a little cool .
All this make her very meek for a time ; and she scarcely open her bill , except to eat .
A guard be set day and night , to see if any danger approach ; and a row of swallow might be see on the ridgepole at all hour .
If any one enter the barn , dozen of little black head peep cautiously over the edge of the nest , and there be much flying to and fro with report and rumor ; for all the bird in the town soon know that something have happen .
The day after the imprudent conversation , a chimney-swallow come to call on Mrs. Wing ; and , the moment she be seat on the beam , she begin : `` My dear creature , I feel for you in your try position , -- indeed I do , and come over at once to warn you of your danger . ''
`` Mercy on u !
what be come ? ''
cried Mrs. Wing , cover her brood with tremble wing , and look quite wild with alarm .
`` Be calm , my friend , and bear with firmness the consequence of your folly , '' reply Mrs. Sooty-back , who do n't like Mrs. Wing , because she pride herself on her family , and rather look down on chimney-swallows .
`` You know , ma'am , I live at the great house , and be in the way of see and hear all that go on there .
No fire be light in the study now ; but my landlord still sit on the hearth , and I can overhear every word he say .
Last evening , after my darling be asleep , and my husband go out , I go down and sit on the andiron , a I often do ; for the fireplace be full of oak bough , and I can peep out unseen .
My landlord sit there , look a trifle more cheerful than usual , and I hear him say , in a very decided tone : '' ` I 'll catch them , one and all , and keep them here ; that be good than pull the place down , a I plan at first .
Those swallow little know what they have do ; but I 'll show them I do n't forget . ' ''
On hear this a general wail arose , and Mrs. Wing faint entirely away .
Madam Sooty-back be quite satisfied with the effect she have produce , and depart , say loftily : `` I 'm sorry for you , Mrs. Wing , and forgive your rude speech about my be relate to chimney-sweeps .
One ca n't expect good manner from person bring up in mud house , and entirely shut out from good society .
If I hear any thing more , I 'll let you know . ''
Away she fly ; and poor Mrs. Wing would have have another fit , if they have n't tickle her with a feather , and fan her so violently that she be nearly blow off her nest by the breeze they raise .
`` What shall we do ? ''
she cry .
`` Nothing , but wait .
I dare say , Mrs. Sooty-back be mistake ; at any rate , we ca n't get away without leave our child , for they ca n't fly yet .
Let u wait , and see what happen .
If the bad come , we shall have do our duty , and will all die together . ''
As no one could suggest any thing better , Mrs. Dart 's advice be take , and they wait .
On the afternoon of the same day , Dr. Banks , a sand-swallow , who live in a subterranean village over by the great sand-bank , look in to see Mrs. Wing , and cheer her by the following bit of news : `` The man be down at the poor-house to-day , and take away little Nan , the orphan baby .
I saw him carry her to Will 's mother , and hear him ask her to take care of it for a time .
He pay her well , and she seem glad to do it ; for Will need help , and now he can have it .
An excellent arrangement , I think .
Bless me , ma'am !
what 's the matter ?
Your pulse be altogether too fast , and you look feverish . ''
No wonder the doctor look surprised ; for Mrs. Wing suddenly give a skip , and flap her wing , with a shrill chirp , exclaim , a she look about her triumphantly : `` Now , who be right ?
Who have do good , not harm , by what you call ` gossip ' ?
Who have be a martyr , and patiently bear all kind of blame , injustice , and disrespect ?
Yes , indeed !
the man saw the sense of my word ; he take my advice ; he will show his gratitude by some good turn yet ; and , if half a dozen poor soul be help , it will be my do , and mine alone . ''
Here she have to stop for breath ; and her neighbor all look at one another , feel undecided whether to own they be wrong , or to put Mrs. Wing down .
Every one twitter and chirp , and make a great noise ; but no one would give up , and all go to roost in a great state of uncertainty .
But , the next day , it become evident that Mrs. Wing be right ; for Major Bumble-bee come buzz in to tell them that old Daddy Winter 's hut be empty , and his white head have be see in the sunny porch of the great house .
After this the swallow give in ; and , a no harm come to them , they have a jubilee in honor of the occasion .
Mrs. Wing be president , and receive a vote of thanks for the good she have do , and the credit she have bestow upon the town by her wisdom and courage .
She be much elate by all this ; but her fright have be of service , and she bore her honor more meekly than one would have suppose .
To be sure , she cut Mrs. Sooty-back when they meet ; assume an injured air , when some of her neighbor pass her ; and say , `` I tell you so , '' a dozen time a day to her husband , who get so many curtain lecture that he take to sleep on the high rafter , pretend that the child 's noise disturb him .
All sort of charm thing happen after that , and such a fine summer never be know before ; for not only do the bird rejoice , but people also .
A good spirit seem to haunt the town , leave help and happiness wherever it pass .
Some unseen hand scatter crumb over the barn floor , and leave food at many door .
No dog or boy or gun mar the tranquillity of the bird , insect , and flower who live on the great estate .
No want , care , or suffering , that love or money could prevent , befall the poor folk whose cottage stand near the old house .
Sunshine and peace seem to reign there ; for it gloomy master be a changed man now , and the happiness he earn for himself , by give it to others , flow out in beautiful , blithe song , and go sing away into the world , make him friend , and bring him honor in high place as well a low .
He do not forget the wife and little child whom he have love so well ; but he mourn no longer , for cheerful daisy grow above their graf , and he know that he should meet them in the lovely land where death can never come .
So , while he wait for that happy time to come , he make his life a cheery song , -- a every one may do , if they will ; and go about drop kind word and deed a silently and sweetly a the sky drop sunshine and dew .
Every one be his friend , but his favorite be the swallow .
Every day he go to see them , carry grain and crumb , hear their chat , share their joy and sorrow , and never tiring of their small friendship ; for to them , he think , he owe all the content now his .
When autumn leaf be red , and autumn wind blew cold , the inhabitant of Twittertown prepare for their journey to the South .
They linger long than usual this year , feel sorry to leave their friend .
But the field be bare , the frost begin to pinch , and the young one long to see the world ; so they must go .
The day they start , the whole flock fly to the great house , to say good-by .
Some dive and dart round and round it , some hop to and fro on the sere lawn , some perch on the chimney-tops , and some clung to the window ledge ; all twitter a loving farewell .
Chirp , Dart , and Wing peep everywhere , and everywhere find something to rejoice over .
In a cosey room , by a bright fire , sit Daddy Winter and Nell 's old father , tell story of their youth , and bask in the comfortable warmth .
In the study , surround by the book he love , be the poor young man , happy a a king now , and learn many thing which no book could teach him ; for he have find a friend .
Then , down below be Will 's mother , work like a bee ; for she be housekeeper , and enjoy her task as much a any mother-bird enjoys fill the little mouth of her brood .
Close by be pretty Nell , pretty than ever now ; for her heavy care be go , and she sing a she sew , think of the old father , whom nothing could trouble any more .
But the pleasant sight the three gossip saw be the man with Baby Nan on his arm and Will at his side , play in the once dreary nursery .
How they laugh and dance !
for Will be up from his bed at last , and hop nimbly on his crutch , know that soon even they would be unneeded .
Little Nan be a plump and rosy a a baby should be , and babble like a brook , a the man go to and fro , cradle her in his strong arm , feeling a if his own little daughter have come back when he hear the baby voice call him father .
`` Ah , how sweet it be ! ''
cried Mrs. Dart , glad to see that he have find comfort for his grief .
`` Yes ; indeed : it do one 's heart good to see such a happy family , '' add Mrs . Skim , who be a very motherly bird .
`` I do n't wish to boast ; but I will say that I be satisfied with my summer 's work , and go South feel that I leave an enviable reputation behind me . ''
And Mrs. Wing plume herself with an air of immense importance , a she nod and bridle from her perch on the window-sill .
The man saw the three , and hasten to fee them for the last time , know that they be about to go .
Gratefully they eat , and chirp their thanks ; and then , a they fly away , the little gossip hear their friend sing his good-by : `` Swallow , swallow , neighbor swallow , Starting on your autumn flight , Pause a moment at my window , Twitter softly your good-night ; For the summer day be over , All your duty be well do , And the happy home you build Have grow empty , one by one .
`` Swallow , swallow , neighbor swallow , Are you ready for your flight ?
Are all the feather cloak complete ?
Are the little cap all right ?
Are the young wing strong and steady For the journey through the sky ?
Come again in early spring-time ; And till then , good-by , good-by ! ''
VIII .
LITTLE GULLIVER .
Up in the light-house tower live Davy , with Old Dan the keeper .
Most little boy would have find it very lonely ; but Davy have three friend , and be as happy a the day be long .
One of Davy 's friend be the great lamp , which be light at sunset , and burn all night , to guide the ship into the harbor .
To Dan it be only a lamp ; but to the boy it seem a living thing , and he love and tend it faithfully .
Every day he help Dan clear the big wick , polish the brass work , and wash the glass lantern which protect the flame .
Every evening he go up to see it light , and always fell asleep , thinking , `` No matter how dark or wild the night , my good Shine will save the ship that pass , and burn till morning . ''
Davy 's second friend be Nep , the Newfoundland , who be wash ashore from a wreck , and have never leave the island since .
Nep be rough and big , but have such a loyal and love heart that no one could look in his soft brown eye and not trust him .
He follow Davy 's step all day , slept at his foot all night , and more than once have save his life when Davy fell among the rock , or get catch by the rise tide .
But the dear friend of all be a sea-gull .
Davy find him , with a broken wing , and nurse him carefully till he be well ; then let him go , though he be very fond of `` Little Gulliver , '' a he call him in fun .
But the bird never forget the boy , and come daily to talk with him , tell all manner of wild story about his wandering by land and sea , and whiling away many an hour that otherwise would have be very lonely .
Old Dan be Davy 's uncle , -- a grim , gray man , who say little , do his work faithfully , and be both father and mother to Davy , who have no parent , and no friend beyond the island .
That be his world ; and he lead a quiet life among his playfellow , -- the wind and wave .
He seldom go to the main land , three mile away ; for he be happy at home .
He watch the sea-anemones open below the water , look like fairy-plants , brilliant and strange .
He find curious and pretty shell , and sometimes more valuable treasure , wash up from some wreck .
He saw little yellow crab , ugly lobster , and queer horse-shoes with their stiff tail .
Sometimes a whale or a shark swam by , and often sleek black seal come up to bask on the warm rock .
He gather lovely sea-weeds of all kind , from tiny red cobweb to great scalloped leaf of kelp , long than himself .
He hear the wave dash and roar unceasingly ; the wind howl or sigh over the island ; and the gull scream shrilly a they dip and dive , or sail away to follow the ship that come and go from all part of the world .
With Nep and Gulliver he roam about his small kingdom , never tire of it wonder ; or , if storm rag , he sit up in the tower , safe and dry , watch the tumult of sea and sky .
Often in long winter night he lay awake , listen to the wind and rain , that make the tower rock with their violence ; but he never be afraid , for Nep nestle at his foot , Dan sit close by , and overhead the great lamp shone far out into the night , to cheer and guide all wanderer on the sea .
Close by the tower hang the fog-bell , which , be wind up , would ring all night , warningly .
One day Dan find that something among the chain be break ; and , have vainly try to mend it , he decide to go to the town , and get what be need .
He go once a week , usually , and leave Davy behind ; for in the daytime there be nothing to do , and the boy be not afraid to stay .
`` A heavy fog be blow up : we shall want the bell to-night , and I must be off at once .
I shall be back before dark , of course ; so take care of yourself , boy , '' say Dan .
Away go the little boat ; and the fog shut down over it , a if a misty wall have part Davy from his uncle .
As it be dull weather , he sit and read for an hour or two ; then fell asleep , and forget everything till Nep 's cold nose on his hand wake him up .
It be nearly dark ; and , hop to find Dan have come , he run down to the landing-place .
But no boat be there , and the fog be thicker than ever .
Dan never have be go so long before , and Davy be afraid something have happen to him .
For a few minute he be in great trouble ; then he cheer up , and take courage .
`` It be sunset by the clock ; so I 'll light the lamp , and , if Dan be lose in the fog , it will guide him home , '' say Davy .
Up he go , and soon the great star shone out above the black-topped light-house , glimmer through the fog , a if eager to be see .
Davy have his supper , but no Dan come .
He wait hour after hour , and wait all in vain .
The fog thicken , till the lamp be hardly see ; and no bell ring to warn the ship of the dangerous rock .
Poor Davy could not sleep , but all night long wander from the tower to the door , watch , call , and wondering ; but Dan do not come .
At sunrise he put out the light , and , have trim it for the next night , eat a little breakfast , and rove about the island hop to see some sign of Dan .
The sun draw up the fog at last ; and he could see the blue bay , the distant town , and a few fishing-boats go out to sea .
But nowhere be the island-boat with gray Old Dan in it ; and Davy 's heart grow heavy and heavy , a the day pass , and still no one come .
In the afternoon Gulliver appear : to him Davy tell his trouble , and the three friend take counsel together .
`` There be no other boat ; and I could n't row so far , if there be : so I ca n't go to find Dan , '' say David sorrowfully .
`` I 'd gladly swim to town , if I could ; but it 's impossible to do it , with wind and tide against me .
I 've howl all day , hop some one would hear me ; but no one do , and I 'm discouraged , '' say Nep , with an anxious expression .
`` I can do something for you ; and I will , with all my heart .
I 'll fly to town , if I do n't see him in the bay , and try to learn what have become of Dan .
Then I 'll come and tell you , and we will see what be to be do next .
Cheer up , Davy dear : I 'll bring you tidings , if any can be have . ''
With these cheerful word , away sail Gulliver , leave Nep and his master to watch and wait again .
The wind blow hard , and the broken wing be not quite well yet , else Gulliver would have be able to steer clear of a boat that come swiftly by .
A sudden gust drive the gull so violently against the sail that he drop breathless into the boat ; and a little girl catch him , before he could recover himself .
`` Oh , what a lovely bird !
See his black cap , his white breast , dove-colored wing , red leg and bill , and soft , bright eye .
I want a gull ; and I 'll keep this one , for I do n't think he be much hurt . ''
Poor Gulliver struggle , peck and scream ; but little Dora hold him fast , and shut him in a basket till they reach the shore .
Then she put him in a lobster pot , -- a large wooden thing , something like a cage , -- and leave him on the lawn , where he could catch glimpse of the sea , and watch the light-house tower , a he sit alone in this dreadful prison .
If Dora have know the truth , she would have let him go , and do her best to help him ; but she could not understand his speech , a Davy do , for very few people have the power of talk with bird , beast , insect , and plant .
To her , his prayer and cry be only harsh scream ; and , when he sit silent , with droop head and ruffle feather , she think he be sleepy : but he be mourn for Davy , and wonder what his little friend would do .
For three long day and night he be a prisoner , and suffer much .
The house be full of happy people , but no one take pity upon him .
Ladies and gentleman talk learnedly about him ; boy poke and pull him ; little girl admire him , and beg his wing for their hat , if he die .
Cats prowl about his cage ; dog bark at him ; hen cackle over him ; and a shrill canary jeer at him from the pretty pagoda in which it hang , high above danger .
In the evening there be music ; and the poor bird 's heart ache a the sweet sound come to him , remind him of the airier melody he love .
Through the stillness of the night , he hear the wave break on the shore ; the wind come sing up from the sea ; the moon shone kindly on him , and he saw the water-fairies dance on the sand .
But for three day no one speak a friendly word to him , and he pin away with a broken heart .
On the fourth night , when all be quiet , little Gulliver saw a black shadow steal across the lawn , and hear a soft voice say to him : `` Poor bird , you 'll die , if yer stay here ; so I ` se gwine to let yer go .
Specs little missy 'll scold dreffle ; but Moppet 'll take de scoldin for yer .
Hi , dere !
you be peart nuff now , kase you 's in a hurry to go ; but jes wait till I gits de knot out of de string dat tie de door , and den away you fly . ''
`` But , dear , kind Moppet , wo n't you be hurt for do this ?
Why do you care so much for me ?
I can only thank you , and fly away . ''
As Gulliver speak , he look up at the little black face bent over him , and saw tear in the child 's sad eye ; but she smile at him , and shake her fuzzy head , a she whisper kindly : `` I do n't want no tank , birdie : I love to let you go , kase you 's a slave , like I be once ; and it 's a dreffle hard ting , I know .
I get away , and I mean you shall .
I ` se watch you , deary , all dese day ; and I try to come ` fore , but dey do n't give me no chance . ''
`` Do you live here ?
I never see you play with the other child , '' say the gull , a Moppet 's nimble finger pick away at the knot .
`` Yes : I live here , and help de cook .
You do n't see me , kase I never play ; de chilen do n't like me . ''
`` Why not ? ''
ask Gulliver , wonder .
`` I ` se black , '' say Moppet , with a sob .
`` But that 's silly in them , '' cry the bird , who have never hear of such a thing .
`` Color make no difference ; the peep be gray , the seal black , and the crab yellow ; but we do n't care , and be all friend .
It be very unkind to treat you so .
Have n't you any friend to love you , dear ? ''
`` Nobody in de world keres fer me .
Dey sell me way from my mammy when I be a baby , and I ` se knock roun eber since .
De oder chilen have folks to lub an kere fer em , but Moppet 's get no friend ; '' and here the black eye grow so dim with tear that the poor child could n't see that the last knot be out .
Gulliver saw it , and , push up the door , fly from his prison with a glad cry ; and , hop into Moppet 's hand , look into the little dark face with such grateful confidence that it clear at once , and the bright smile it have wear for month break over it a the bird nestle it soft head against her cheek , say gently : `` I 'm your friend , dear ; I love you , and I never shall forget what you have do for me to-night .
How can I thank you before I go ? ''
For a minute , Moppet could only hug the bird , and cry ; for these be the first kind word she have hear for a long time , and they go straight to her lonely little heart .
`` O my deary !
I ` se pay by dem word , and I do n't want no tank .
Jes lub me , and come sometimes to see me ef you can , it 's so hard livin ' in dis yere place .
I do n't tink I 'll bar it long .
I wish I be a bird to fly away , or a oyster safe in de mud , and free to do a I 's a mind . ''
`` I wish you could go and live with Davy on the island ; he be so kind , so happy , and as free a the wind .
Ca n't you get away , Moppet ? ''
whisper Gulliver , long to help this poor , friendless little soul .
He tell her all his story ; and they agree that he should fly at once to the island , and see if Dan be there ; if not , he be to come back , and Moppet would try to get some one to help find him .
When this be do , Davy and Dan be to take Moppet , if they could , and make her happy on the island .
Full of hope and joy , Gulliver say good-by , and spread his wing ; but , ala for the poor bird !
he be too weak to fly .
For three day he have hardly eat any thing , have find no salt water to bathe in , and have sit moping in the cage till his strength be all go .
`` What shall I do ?
what shall I do ? ''
he cry , flutter his feeble wing , and run to and fro in despair .
`` Hush , birdie , I 'll take kere ob you till you 's fit to fly .
I know a nice , quiet little cove down yonder , where no one go ; and dare you kin stay till you 's good .
I 'll come and fee you , and you kin paddle , and rest , and try your wing , safe and free , honey . ''
As Moppet speak , she take Gulliver in her arm , and steal away in the dim light , over the hill , down to the lonely spot where nothing go but the wind and wave , the gull , and little Moppet , when hard word and blow make heart and body ache .
Here she leave the bird , and , with a love `` Good-night , '' creep home to her bed in the garret , feel as rich a a queen , and much happy ; for she have do a kind thing , and make a friend .
Next day , a great storm come : the wind blow a hurricane , the rain pour , and the sea thunder on the coast .
If he have be well , Gulliver would n't have mind at all ; but , be sick and sad , he spend an anxious day , sit in a cranny of the rock , thinking of Davy and Moppet .
It be so rough , even in the cove , that he could neither swim nor fly , so feeble be he ; and could find no food but such trifle a he could pick up among the rock .
At nightfall the storm rag fiercer than ever , and he give up see Moppet ; for he be sure she would n't come through the pelting rain just to fee him .
