So he put his head under his wing , and try to sleep ; but he be so wet and weak , so hungry and anxious , no sleep come .
`` What have happen to Davy alone on the island all this while ?
He will fall ill with loneliness and trouble ; the lamp wo n't be light , the ship will be wreck , and many people will suffer .
O Dan , Dan , if we could only find you , how happy we should be ! ''
As Gulliver speak , a voice cry through the darkness : `` Is you dere , honey ? ''
and Moppet come climb over the rock , with a basket full of such bit a she could get .
`` Poor birdie , be you starvin ' ?
Here , jes go at dis , and joy yourself .
Dere 's fish and ting I tink you 'd like .
How be you now , dear ? ''
`` Better , Moppet ; but , it 's so stormy , I ca n't get to Davy ; and I worry about him , '' begin Gulliver , peck away at his supper : but he stop suddenly , for a faint sound come up from below , a if some one call , `` Help , help ! ''
`` Hi !
what 's dat ? ''
say Moppet , listening .
`` Davy , Davy ! ''
call the voice .
`` It 's Dan .
Hurrah , we 've find him ! ''
and Gulliver dive off the rock so reckless that he go splash into the water .
But that do n't matter to him ; and he paddle away , like a little steamer with all the engine in full blast .
Down by the sea-side , between two stone , lay Dan , so bruised and hurt he could n't move , and so faint with hunger and pain he could hardly speak .
As soon a Gulliver call , Moppet scramble down , and feed the poor man with her scrap , bring him rain-water from a crevice near by , and bind up his wounded head with her little apron .
Then Dan tell them how his boat have be run down by a ship in the fog ; how he be hurt , and cast ashore in the lonely cove ; how he have lain there half dead , for no one hear his shout , and he could n't move ; how the storm bring him back to life , when he be almost go , and the sound of Moppet 's voice tell him help be near .
How glad they all be then !
Moppet dance for joy ; Gulliver scream and flap his wing ; and Dan smile , in spite of pain , to think he should see Davy again .
He could n't understand Gulliver ; but Moppet tell him all the story , and , when he hear it , he be more troubled for the boy than for himself .
`` What will he do ?
He may get kill or scar , or try to come ashore .
Is the lamp alight ? ''
he cry , try to move , and fall back with a moan of pain .
Gulliver fly up to the high rock , and look out across the dark sea .
Yes , there it be , -- the steady star shin through the storm , and say plainly , `` All be well . ''
`` Thank heaven !
if the lamp be burn , Davy be alive .
Now , how shall I get to him ? ''
say Dan .
`` Never you fret , massa : Moppet 'll see to dat .
You jes lay still till I come .
Dere 's folk in de house a 'll tend to you , ef I tell em who and where you be . ''
Off she run , and soon come back with help .
Dan be take to the house , and carefully tend ; Moppet be n't scold for be out so late ; and , in the flurry , no one thought of the gull .
Next morning , the cage be find blown over , and every one fancy the bird have fly away .
Dora be already tire of him ; so he be soon forget by all but Moppet .
In the morning it be clear ; and Gulliver fly gladly to the tower where Davy still watch and wait , with a pale face and heavy heart , for the three day have be very hard to bear , and , but for Nep and Shine , he would have lose his courage entirely .
Gulliver fly straight into his bosom , and , sit there , tell his adventure ; while Davy laugh and cry , and Nep stood by , wag his tail for joy , while his eye be full of sympathy .
The three have a very happy hour together , and then come a boat to carry Davy ashore , while another keeper take charge of the light till Dan be well .
Nobody ever know the best part of the story but Moppet , Davy , and Gulliver .
Other people do n't dream that the boy 's pet gull have any thing to do with the finding of the man , or the good fortune that come to Moppet .
While Dan lay sick , she tend him , like a loving little daughter ; and , when he be well , he take her for his own .
He do not mind the black skin : he only saw the loneliness of the child , the tender heart , the innocent , white soul ; and he be a glad to be a friend to her a if she have be a blithe and pretty a Dora .
It be a happy day when Dan and Davy , Moppet , Gulliver , and Nep sail away to the island ; for that be still to be their home , with stout young Ben to help .
The sun be set ; and they float through wave as rosy a the rosy sky .
A fresh wind fill the sail , and ruffle Gulliver 's white breast a he sit on the mast-head croon a cheery song to himself .
Dan hold the tiller , and Davy lay at his foot , with Nep bolt upright beside him ; but the happy face of all be Moppet 's .
Kneeling at the bow , she lean forward , with her lip apart , her fuzzy hair blow back , and her eye fix on the island which be to be her home .
Like a little black figure-head of Hope , she lean and look , a the boat fly on , bear her away from the old life into the new .
As the sun sunk , out shone the lamp with sudden brightness , a if the island bid them welcome .
Dan furl the sail ; and , drift with the tide , they float in , till the wave break softly on the shore , and leave them safe at home .
IX .
THE WHALE 'S STORY .
Freddy sit think on the seat under the tree .
It be a wide , white seat , about four foot long , slop from the side to the middle , something like a swing ; and be not only comfortable but curious , for it be make of a whale 's bone .
Freddy often sit there , and think about it for he be very much interested in it , and nobody could tell him any thing of it , except that it have be there a long time .
`` Poor old whale , I wonder how you get here , where you come from , and if you be a good and happy creature while you live , '' say Freddy , pat the old bone with his little hand .
It give a great creak ; and a sudden gust of air stir the tree , a if some monster groan and sigh .
Then Freddy hear a strange voice , very loud , yet crack and queer , a if some one try to talk with a broken jaw .
`` Freddy ahoy ! ''
call the big voice .
`` I 'll tell you all about it ; for you be the only person who ever pity me , or care to know any thing about me . ''
`` Why , can you talk ? ''
ask Freddy , very much astonish and a little frightened .
`` Of course I can , for this be a part of my jaw-bone .
I should talk good if my whole mouth be here ; but I 'm afraid my voice would then be so loud you would n't be able to hear it .
I do n't think any one but you would understand me , any way .
It be n't every one that can , you know ; but you be a thoughtful little chap , with a lively fancy as well a a kind heart , so you shall hear my story . ''
`` Thank you , I should like it very much , if you would please to speak a little low , and not sigh ; for your voice almost stun me , and your breath nearly blow me away , '' say Freddy .
`` I 'll try : but it 's hard to suit my tone to such a mite , or to help groan when I think of my sad fate ; though I deserve it , perhaps , '' say the bone , more gently .
`` Were you a naughty whale ? ''
ask Freddy .
`` I be proud , very proud , and foolish ; and so I suffer for it .
I dare say you know a good deal about u .
I see you read often , and you seem a sensible child . ''
`` No : I have n't read about you yet , and I only know that you be the big fish there be , '' reply Freddy .
The bone creak and shook , a if it be laugh , and say in a tone that show it have n't get over it pride yet : `` You 're wrong there , my dear ; we be not fish at all , though stupid mortal have call u so for a long time .
We ca n't live without air ; we have warm , red blood ; and we do n't lay egg , -- so we be not fish .
We certainly be the big creature in the sea and out of it .
Why , bless you !
some of u be nearly a hundred foot long ; our tail alone be fifteen or twenty foot wide ; the big of u weigh five hundred thousand pound , and have in them the fat , bone , and muscle of a thousand cattle .
The low jaw of one of my family make an arch large enough for a man on horseback to ride under easily , and my cousin of the sperm-family usually yield eighty barrel of oil . ''
`` Gracious me , what monsters you be ! ''
cry Freddy , take a long breath , while his eye get big and big a he listen .
`` Ah !
you may well say so ; we be a very wonderful and interesting family .
All our branch be famous in one way or another .
Fin-backs , sperm , and right be the large ; then come the norwhals , the dolphin , and porpoise , -- which last , I dare say , you 've see . ''
`` Yes : but tell me about the big one , please .
Which be you ? ''
cry Freddy .
`` I be a Right whale , from Greenland .
The Sperms live in warm place ; but to u the torrid zone be like a sea of fire , and we do n't pass it .
Our cousin do ; and go to the East Indies by way of the North Pole , which be more than your famous Parrys and Franklins could do . ''
`` I do n't know about that ; but I 'd like to hear what you eat , and how you live , and why you come here , '' say Freddy , who think the whale rather incline to boast .
`` Well , we have n't get any teeth , -- our branch of the family ; and we live on creature so small , that you could only see them with a microscope .
Yes , you may stare ; but it 's true , my dear .
The roof of our mouth be make of whalebone , in broad piece from six to eight foot long , arrange one against the other ; so they make an immense sieve .
The tongue , which make about five barrel of oil , lie below , like a cushion of white satin .
When we want to fee , we rush through the water , which be full of the little thing we eat , and catch them in our sieve , spurt the water through two hole in our head .
Then we collect the food with our tongue , and swallow it ; for , though we be so big , our throat be small .
We roam about in the ocean , leap and float , feed and spout , fly from our enemy , or fight bravely to defend our young one . ''
`` Have you get any enemy ?
I should n't think you could have , you be so large , '' say Freddy .
`` But we have , and many too , -- three who attack u in the water , and several more that men use against u .
The killer , the sword-fish , and the thrasher trouble u at home .
The killer fasten to u , and wo n't be shake off till he have worry u to death ; the sword-fish stab u with his sword ; and the thrasher whip u to death with his own slender , but strong and heavy body .
Then , men harpoon u , shoot or entrap u ; and make u into oil and candle and seat , and stiffen for gown and umbrella , '' say the bone , in a tone of scorn .
Freddy laugh at the idea , and ask , `` How about candle ?
I know about oil and seat and umbrella ; but I think candle be make of wax . ''
`` I ca n't say much on that point : I only know that , when a sperm whale be kill , they make oil out of the fat part a they do of ours ; but the Sperms have a sort of cistern in their head , full of stuff like cream , and rose-colored .
They cut a hole in the skull , and dip it out ; and sometimes get sixteen or twenty barrel .
This be make into what you call spermaceti candle .
We do n't have any such nonsense about u ; but the Sperms always be a light-headed set . ''
Here the bone laugh , in a cracked sort of roar , which send Freddy fly off the seat on to the grass , where he stay , laugh also , though he do n't see any joke .
`` I beg your pardon , child .
It be n't often that I laugh ; for I 've a heavy heart somewhere , and have know trouble enough to make me as sad a the sea be sometimes . ''
`` Tell me about your trouble ; I pity you very much , and like to hear you talk , '' say Freddy , kindly .
`` Unfortunately we be very easily kill , in spite of our size ; and have various affliction besides death .
We grow blind ; our jaw be deform sometimes ; our tail , with which we swim , get hurt ; and we have dyspepsia . ''
Freddy shout at that ; for he know what dyspepsia be , because at the sea-side there be many sickly people who be always groan about that disease .
`` It 's no laughing matter , I assure you , '' say the whale 's bone .
`` We suffer a great deal , and get thin and weak and miserable .
I 've sometimes think that 's the reason we be blue . ''
`` Perhaps , a you have no teeth , you do n't chew your food enough , and so have dyspepsia , like an old gentleman I know , '' say Freddy .
`` That 's not the reason ; my cousin , the Sperms , have teeth , and dyspepsia also . ''
`` Are they blue ? ''
`` No , black and white .
But I be go to tell you my trouble .
My father be harpoon when I be very young , and I remember how bravely he die .
The Rights usually run away when they see a whaler come ; not from cowardice , -- oh , dear , no !
-- but discretion .
The Sperms stay and fight , and be kill off very fast ; for they be a very headstrong family .
We fight when we ca n't help it ; and my father die like a hero .
They chase him five hour before they stick him ; he try to get away , and drag three or four boat and sixteen hundred fathom of line from eight in the morning till four at night .
Then they get out another line , and he tow the ship itself for more than an hour .
There be fifteen harpoon in him : he chew up a boat , pitch several men overboard , and damage the vessel , before they kill him .
Ah !
he be a father to be proud of . ''
Freddy sit respectfully silent for a few minute , a the old bone seem to feel a great deal on the subject .
Presently he go on again : `` The Sperms live in herd ; but the Rights go in pair , and be very fond of one another .
My wife be a charming creature , and we be very happy , till one sad day , when she be play with our child , -- a sweet little whaleling only twelve foot long , and weigh but a ton , -- my son be harpoon .
His mamma , instead of fly , wrap her fin round him , and dive as far a the line allow .
Then she come up , and dash at the boat in great rage and anguish , entirely regardless of the danger she be in .
The men strike my son , in order to get her , and they soon succeed ; but even then , in spite of her suffering , she do not try to escape , but clung to little Spouter till both be kill .
Alas !
ala ! ''
Here the poor bone creak so dismally , Freddy fear it would tumble to piece , and bring the story to an end too soon .
`` Do n't think of those sorrowful thing , '' he say ; `` tell me how you come to be here .
Were you harpoon ? ''
`` Not I ; for I 've be very careful all my life to keep out of the way of danger : I 'm not like one of my relation , who attack a ship , give it such a dreadful blow that he make a great hole , the water rush in , and the vessel be wreck .
But he pay dearly for that prank ; for a few month afterward another ship harpoon him very easily , find two spear still in him , and a wound in his head .
I forget to mention , that the Sperms have fine ivory teeth , and make ambergris , -- a sort of stuff that smell very nice , and cost a great deal .
I give you these little fact about my family , a you seem interested , and it 's always well to improve the mind of young people . ''
`` You be very kind ; but will you be good enough to tell about yourself ? ''
say Freddy again ; for the bone seem to avoid that part of the story , a if he do n't want to tell it .
`` Well , if I must , I must ; but I 'm sorry to confess what a fool I 've be .
You know what coral be , do n't you ? ''
`` No , '' say Freddy , wonder why it ask .
`` Then I must tell you , I suppose .
There be a bit in the house there , -- that rough , white , stony stuff on the table in the parlor .
It 's full of little hole , you know .
Well , those hole be the front door of hundred of little polypes , or coral worm , who build the great branch of coral , and live there .
They be of various shape and color , -- some like star ; some fine a a thread , and blue or yellow ; others like snail and tiny lobster .
Some people say the real coral-makers be shape like little oblong bag of jelly , close at one end , the other open , with six or eight little feeler , like a star , all around it .
The other creature be boarder or visitor : these be the real worker , and , when they sit in their cell and put out their feeler , they make all manner of lovely color under the water , -- crimson , green , orange , and violet .
But if they be take up or touch , the coral people go in door , and the beautiful hue disappear .
They say there be many coral reef and island build by these industrious people , in the South Seas ; but I ca n't go there to see , and I be content with those I find in the northern latitude .
I know such a community of coral builder , and use to watch them long ago , when they begin to work .
It be a charming spot , down under the sea ; for all manner of lovely plant grow there ; splendid fish sail to and fro ; wonderful shell lay about ; crimson and yellow prawn , long , glide green worm , and purple sea-urchins , be there .
When I ask the polypes what they be do , and they answer , ` Building an island , ' I laugh at them ; for the idea that these tiny , soft atom could make any thing be ridiculous .
` You may roar ; but you 'll see that we be right , if you live long enough , ' say they .
` Our family have build thousand of island and long reef , that the sea ca n't get over , strong a it be . '
That amuse me immensely ; but I would n't believe it , and laugh more than ever . ''
`` It do seem very strange , '' say Freddy , look at the branch of coral which he have bring out to examine .
`` Does n't it ?
and be n't it hard to believe ?
I use to go , now and then , to see how the little fellow get on , and always find them hard at it .
For a long while there be only a little plant without leaf , grow slowly taller and taller ; for they always build upward toward the light .
By and by , the small shrub be a tree : flying-fish roost in it branch ; sea-cows lay under it shadow ; and thousand of jolly little polypes live and work in it white chamber .
I be glad to see them get on so well ; but still I do n't believe in the island story , and use to joke them about their ambition .
They be very good-natured , and only answer me , ` Wait a little long , Friend Right . '
I have my own affair to attend to ; so , for year at a time , I forget the coral-workers , and spend most of my life up Greenland way , for warm climate do n't agree with my constitution .
When I come back , after a long absence , I be astonish to see the tree grown into a large umbrella-shaped thing , rise above the water .
Sea-weed have wash up and cling there ; sea-birds have make nest there ; land-birds and the wind have carry seed there , which have spring up ; trunk of tree have be cast there by the sea ; lizard , insect , and little animal come with the tree , and be the first inhabitant ; and , behold !
it be an island . ''
`` What do you say then ? ''
ask Freddy .
`` I be angry , and do n't want to own that I be wrong ; so I insist that it be n't a real island , without people on it .
` Wait a little long , ' answer the polypes ; and go on , build broad and broad foundation .
I flounce away in a rage , and do n't go back for a great while .
I hop something would happen to the coral builder and their island ; but I be so curious that I could n't keep away , and , on go back there , I find a settlement of fisherman , and the beginning of a thriving town .
Now I should have be in a towering passion at this , if in my travel I have n't discover a race of little creature a much small than polypes a a mouse be small than an elephant .
I hear two learn men talk about diatom , a they sail to Labrador ; and I listen .
They say these people live in both salt and fresh water , and be find in all part of the world .
They be a glassy shell , hold a soft , golden-yellow substance , and that they be so countless that bank be make of them , and that a town here in these United States be found on them .
They be the food of many little sea-animals , who , in turn , feed u big creature , and be very interesting and wonderful .
I save up this story ; and , when the polypes ask if they have n't do what they intend , I tell them I do n't think it so very remarkable , for the tiny diatom make city , and be far more astonishing animal than they .
I think that would silence them ; but they just turn round , and inform me that my diatom be plant , not animal , -- so my story be all humbug .
Then I be mad ; and could n't get over the fact that these little rascal have do what we , the king of the sea , could n't do .
I be n't content with be the big creature there : I want to be the most skilful also .
I do n't remember that every thing have it own place and use , and should be happy in do the work for which it be make .
I fret over the matter a long while , and at last decide to make an island myself . ''
`` How could you ? ''
ask Freddy .
`` I have my plan ; and think them very wise one .
I be so bent on outdo the polypes that I do n't much care what happen ; and so I go to work in my clumsy way .
I could n't pile up stone , or build million of cell ; so I just make an island of myself .
I swim up into the harbor yonder one night ; cover my back with sea-weed ; and lay still on the top of the water .
In the morning the gull come to see what it be , and peck away at the weed , tell me very soon that they know what I be after , and that I could n't gull them .
All the people on shore turn out to see the wonder also ; for a fisherman have carry the tidings , and every one be wild to behold the new island .
After star and chatter a long while , boat come off to examine the mystery .
Loads of scientific gentleman work away at me with microscope , hammer , acid , and all sort of test , to decide what I be ; and keep up such a fire of long word that I be ` most dead .
They could n't make up their mind ; and meanwhile news of the strange thing spread , and every sort of person come to see me .
The gull keep tell them the joke ; but they do n't understand , and I get on capitally .
Every night I din and fed and frolic till dawn ; then put on my sea-weeds , and lay still to be star at .
I want some one to come and live on me ; then I should be equal to the island of the polypes .
But no one come , and I be begin to be tire of fool people , when I be fool myself .
An old sailor come to visit me : he have be a whaler , and he soon guess the secret .
But he say nothing till he be safely out of danger ; then he get all ready , and one day , a I lay placidly in the sun , a horrible harpoon come fly through the air , and sunk deep into my back .
I forget every thing but the pain , and dive for my life .
Alas !
the tide be low ; the harbor-bar could n't be pass ; and I find hundred of boat chase me , till I be drive ashore down there on the flat .
Big and strong a we be , once out of water , and we be perfectly helpless .
I be soon despatch ; and my bone leave to whiten on the sand .
This be long ago ; and , one by one , all my relic have be carry off or wash away .
My jaw-bone have be use a a seat here , till it 's wear out ; but I could n't crumble away till I 'd tell some one my story .
Remember , child , pride goeth before a fall . ''
Then , with a great creak , the bone tumble to piece ; and find a peaceful grave in the long green grass .
X .
A STRANGE ISLAND .
One day I lay rock in my boat , read a very famous book , which all child know and love ; and the name of which I 'll tell you by and by .
So busily be I reading , that I never mind the tide ; and presently discover that I be float out to sea , with neither sail nor oar .
At first I be very much frighten ; for there be no one in sight on land or sea , and I do n't know where I might drift to .
But the water be calm , the sky clear , and the wind blow balmily ; so I wait for what should happen .
Presently I saw a speck on the sea , and eagerly watch it ; for it draw rapidly near , and seem to be go my way .
When it come closer , I be much amaze ; for , of all the queer boat I ever saw , this be the queer .
It be a great wooden bowl , very cracked and old ; and in it sit three gray-headed little gentleman with spectacle , all read busily , and let the boat go where it please .
Now , right in their way be a rock ; and I call out , `` Sir , sir , take care . ''
But my call come too late : crash go the bowl , out come the bottom , and down plump all the little gentleman into the sea .
I try not to laugh , a the book , wig , and spectacle fly about ; and , urge my boat nearer , I manage to fish them up , drip and sneezing , and look like drown kitten .
When the flurry be over , and they have get their breath , I ask who they be , and where they be go .
`` We be from Gotham , ma'am , '' say the fat one , wipe a very wet face on a very wet handkerchief .
`` We be go to that island yonder .
We have often try , but never get there : it 's always so , and I begin to think the thing ca n't be do . ''
I look where he point ; and , sure enough , there be an island where I have never see one before .
I rub my eye , and look again .
Yes : there it be , -- a little island , with tree and people on it ; for I saw smoke come out of the chimney of a queerly-shaped house on the shore .
`` What be the name of it ? ''
I ask .
The little old gentleman put his finger on his lip , and say , with a mysterious nod : `` I could n't tell you , ma'am .
It 's a secret ; but , if you manage to land there , you will soon know . ''
The other old men nod at the same time ; and then all go to read again , with the water still drop off the end of their nose .
This make me very curious ; and , a the tide drift u near and near , I look well about me , and saw several thing that fill me with a strong desire to land on the island .
The odd house , I find , be build like a high-heeled shoe ; and at every window I saw child 's head .
Some be eating broth ; some be cry ; and some have nightcap on .
I catch sight of a distracted old lady fly about , with a ladle in one hand , and a rod in the other ; but the house be so full of child -LRB- even up to the skylight , -- out of which they pop their head , and nod at me -RRB- that I could n't see much of the mamma of this large family : one seldom can , you know .
I have hardly get over my surprise at this queer sight , when I saw a cow fly up through the air , over the new moon that hung there , and come down and disappear in the wood .
I really do n't know what to make of this , but have no time to ask the old men what it mean ; for a cat , play a fiddle , be see on the shore .
A little dog stood by , listen and laugh ; while a dish and a spoon run away over the beach with all their might .
If the boat have not float up to the land , I think I should have swam there , -- I be so anxious to see what be go on ; for there be a great racket on the island , and such a remarkable collection of creature , it be impossible to help staring .
As soon a we land , three other gentleman come to welcome the one I have save , and seem very glad to see them .
They appear to have just land from a tub in which be a drum , rub-a-dub-dubbing all by itself .
One of the new men have a white frock on , and carry a large knife ; the second have dough on his hand , flour on his coat , and a hot-looking face ; the third be very greasy , have a bundle of candle under his arm , and a ball of wicking half out of his pocket .
The six shook hand , and walk away together , talk about a fair ; and leave me to take care of myself .
I walk on through a pleasant meadow , where a pretty little girl be look sadly up at a row of sheep 's tail hang on a tree .
I also saw a little boy in blue , asleep by a haycock ; and another boy take aim at a cock-sparrow , who clap his wing and fly away .
Presently I saw two more little girl : one sat by a fire warm her toe ; and , when I ask what her name be , she say pleasantly : `` Polly Flinders , ma'am . ''
The other one sat on a tuft of grass , eat something that look very nice ; but , all of a sudden , she drop her bowl , and run away , look very much frighten .
`` What 's the matter with her ? ''
I ask of a gay young frog who come trip along with his hat under his arm .
`` Miss Muffit be a fashionable lady , and afraid of spider , madam ; also of frog . ''
And he puff himself angrily up , till his eye quite goggle in his head .
`` And , pray , who be you , sir ? ''
I ask , star at his white vest , green coat , and fine cravat .
`` Excuse me , if I do n't give my name , ma'am .

With that , the frog skip away ; and I turn into a narrow lane , which seem to lead toward some music .

First come a splendid cock-a-doodle , all in black and gold , like a herald , blow his trumpet , and march with a very dignified step .
Then come a rook , in black , like a minister , with spectacle and white cravat .
A lark and bullfinch follow , -- friend , I suppose ; and then the bride and bridegroom .
Miss Wren be evidently a Quakeress ; for she wear a sober dress , and a little white veil , through which her bright eye shone .
The bridegroom be a military man , in his scarlet uniform , -- a plump , bold-looking bird , very happy and proud just then .
A goldfinch give away the bride , and a linnet be bridesmaid .
The ceremony be very fine ; and , as soon a it be over , the blackbird , thrush and nightingale burst out in a lovely song .
A splendid dinner follow , at which be nearly every bird that fly ; so you may imagine the music there be .
They have currant-pie in abundance ; and cherry-wine , which excite a cuckoo so much , that he become quite rude , and so far forgot himself a to pull the bride about .
This make the groom so angry that he beg his friend , the sparrow , to bring his bow and arrow , and punish the ruffian .
But , ala !
Sparrow have also take a drop too much : he aim wrong , and , with a dreadful cry , Mr. Robin sink die into the arm of his wife , little Jane .
It be too much for me ; and , take advantage of the confusion that follow , I leave the tragical scene a fast a possible .
A little farther on , I be shock to see a goose drag an old man down some step that lead to a little house .
`` Dear me !
what 's the matter here ? ''
I cry .

`` But perhaps he be never teach , '' say I . `` It 's never too late to learn : he 's have his chance ; he wo n't be pious and good , so away with him .
Do n't interfere , whatever you do : hold your tongue , and go about your business , '' scold the goose , who certainly have a dreadful temper .
I dare say no more ; and , when the poor old man have be drive away by this foul proceeding , I go up the step and peep in ; for I hear some one cry , and think the cross bird , perhaps , have hurt some one else .
A little old woman stand there , wring her hand in great distress ; while a small dog be bark at her with all his might .

the fashion have get even here , '' think I ; for the old woman be dress in the late style , -- or , rather , she have overdone it sadly ; for her gown be nearly up to her knee , and she be nearly as ridiculous an object a some of the young lady I have see at home .

say I , quite touch by her tear .
`` Lud a mercy , ma'am !

Somebody -- I think it 's a rogue of a peddler who sell me wooden nutmeg , and a clock that would n't go , and some pan that come to bits the first time I use them -- somebody cut my new gown and petticoat off all round , in the shameful way you see .

But , oh , ma'am !
doggy do n't know me ; and I ai n't myself , and I do n't know what to do . ''
`` He 's a foolish little beast ; so do n't mind him , but have a cup of tea , and go to bed .

This seem to comfort the old woman ; though doggy still bark .

`` He 's a remarkable beast ; and you 'd better stop to see him a you pass , ma'am .

The closet be empty , I observe ; but the dog sit smoke a pipe , look as grave a a judge .
`` Where be your mistress ? ''
ask I . `` Gone for some tripe , '' answer the dog , politely take the pipe out of his mouth , and add , `` I hope the smoke do n't annoy you . ''
`` I do n't approve of smoking , '' say I . `` Sorry to hear it , '' say the dog , coolly .
I be go to lecture him on this bad habit ; but I saw his mistress come with a dish in her hand , and , fear she might think me rude to peep in at her window , I walk on , wonder what we be come to when even four-legged puppy smoke .

As I turn into a road that lead toward a hill , a little boy , rid a dapple-gray pony , and an old lady on a white horse , with bell ring somewhere , trot by me , follow by a little girl , who wish to know where she could buy a penny bun .

`` Bless you , my dear !
it ma be make patty-cakes ; and put it up there to be out of the way of Tom Tinker 's dog .
I 'll soon hush it up , '' say the old woman ; and , trot it on her knee , she begin to sing : `` Hey !
my kitten , my kitten , Hey !
my kitten , my deary . ''
Feeling that the child be in good hand , I hurry away , for I saw something be go on upon the hill-top .
When I get to the hill-top , I be shock to find some people toss an old woman in a blanket .
I beg them to stop ; but one of the men , who , I find , be a Welchman , by the name of Taffy , tell me the old lady like it .
`` But why do she like it ? ''
I ask in great surprise .
`` Tom , the piper 's son , will tell you : it 's my turn to toss now , '' say the man .
`` Why , you see , ma'am , '' say Tom , `` she be one of those dreadfully nice old woman , who be always fuss and scrubbing , and worry people to death , with everlastingly clean house .
Now and then we get so tire out with her that we propose to her to clean the sky itself .
She like that ; and , a this be the only way we can get her up , we toss till she stick somewhere , and then leave her to sweep cobweb till she be ready to come back and behave herself . ''
`` Well , that be the odd thing I ever hear .
I know just such an old lady , and when I go home I 'll try your plan .
It seem to me that you have a great many queer old lady on this island , '' I say to another man , whom they call Peter , and who stand eat pumpkin all the time .
`` Well , we do have rather a nice collection ; but you have n't see the best of all .
We expect her every minute ; and Margery Daw be to let u know the minute she light on the island , '' reply Peter , with his mouth full .
`` Lights ? ''
say I , `` you speak a if she fly . ''
`` She rid on a bird .
Hurrah !
the old sweeper have light .
Now the cobweb will fly .
Do n't hurry back , '' shout the man ; and a faint , far-off voice answer , `` I shall be back again by and by . ''
The people fold up the blanket , look much relieve ; and I be examine a very odd house which be build by an ancient king call Boggen , when Margery Daw , a dirty little girl , come up the hill , screaming , at the top of her voice : `` She 's come !
she 's come ! ''
Every one look up ; and I saw a large white bird slowly fly over the island .
On it back sit the nice old woman that ever be see : all the others be nothing compare to her .
She have a point hat on over her cap , a red cloak , high-heeled shoe , and a crutch in her hand .
She smile and nod a the bird approach ; and every one ran and nod , and scream , `` Welcome !
welcome , mother ! ''
As soon a she touch the ground , she be so surrounded that I could only see the top of her hat ; for hundred and hundred of little child suddenly appear , like a great flock of bird , -- rosy , happy , pretty child ; but all look unreal , and among them I saw some who look like little people I have know long ago .
`` Who be they ? ''
I ask of a bonny lass , who be sit on a cushion , eat strawberry and cream .
`` They be the phantom of all the little people who ever read and love our mother 's song , '' say the maid .
`` What do she write ? ''
I ask , feel very queer , and a if I be go to remember something .
`` Songs that be immortal ; and you have them in your hand , '' reply the bonny maid , smile at my stupidity .
I look ; and there , on the cover of the book I have be read so busily when the tide carry me away , I saw the word `` Mother Goose 's Melodies . ''
I be so delighted that I have see her I give a shout , and try to get near enough to hug and kiss the dear old soul , a the swarm of child be do ; but my cry wake me , and I be so sorry to find it all a dream !
XI .
FANCY 'S FRIEND .
It be a wagon , shape like a great square basket , on low wheel , and draw by a stout donkey .
There be one seat , on which Miss Fairbairn the governess sit ; and all round her , lean over the edge of the basket , be child , with little wooden shovel and basket in their hand , go down to play on the beach .
Away they go , over the common , through the stony lane , out upon the wide , smooth sand .
All the child but one immediately fell to dig hole , and make pond , castle , or fort .
They do this every day , and be never tire of it ; but little Fancy make new game for herself , and seldom dug in the sand .
She have a garden of sea-weed , which the wave water every day : she have a palace of pretty shell , where she keep all sort of little water-creatures a fairy tenant ; she have friend and playmate among the gull and peep , and learn curious thing by watch crab , horse-shoes , and jelly-fishes ; and every day she look for a mermaid .
It be of no use to tell her that there be no mermaid : Fancy firmly believe in them , and be sure she would see one some day .
The other child call the seal mermaid ; and be content with the queer , shiny creature who play in the water , lay on the rock , and peep at them with soft , bright eye a they sail by .
Fancy be not satisfy with seal , -- they be not pretty and graceful enough for her , -- and she wait and watch for a real mermaid .
On this day she take a breezy run with the beach-birds along the shore ; she plant a pretty red weed in her garden ; and let out the water-beetles and snail who have pass the night in her palace .
Then she go to a rock that stand near the quiet nook where she play alone , and sit there look for a mermaid a the tide come in ; for it bring her many curious thing , and it might perhaps bring a mermaid .
As she look across the wave that come tumble one over the other , she saw something that be neither boat nor buoy nor seal .
It be a queer-looking thing , with a wild head , a long waving tail , and something like arm that seem to paddle it along .
The wave tumble it about , so Fancy could not see very well : but , the long she look , the surer she be that this curious thing be a mermaid ; and she wait eagerly for it to reach the shore .
Nearer and nearer it come , till a great wave throw it upon the sand ; and Fancy saw that it be only a long piece of kelp , tear up by the root .
She be very much disappointed ; but , all of a sudden , her face clear up , she clap her hand , and begin to dance round the kelp , say : `` I 'll make a mermaid myself , since none will come to me . ''
Away she run , high up the beach , and , after think a minute , begin her work .
Choosing a smooth , hard place , she draw with a stick the outline of her mermaid ; then she make the hair of the brown marsh-grass grow near by , arrange it in long lock on either side the face , which be make of her prettiest pink and white shell , -- for she pull down her palace to get them .
The eye be two gray pebble ; the neck and arm of large , white shell ; and the dress of sea-weed , -- red , green , purple , and yellow ; very splendid , for Fancy empty her garden to dress her mermaid .
`` People say that mermaid always have tail ; and I might make one out of this great leaf of kelp .
But it be n't pretty , and I do n't like it ; for I want mine to be beautiful : so I wo n't have any tail , '' say Fancy , and put two slender white shell for foot , at the low edge of the fringed skirt .
She lay a wreath of little star-fish across the brown hair , a belt of small orange-crabs round the waist , button the dress with violet snail-shells , and hang a tiny white pebble , like a pearl , in either ear .
`` Now she must have a glass and a comb in her hand , a the song say , and then she will be do , '' say Fancy , look about her , well pleased .
Presently she find the skeleton of a little fish , and his backbone make an excellent comb ; while a transparent jelly-fish serve for a glass , with a frame of cockle-shells round it .
Placing these in the hand of her mermaid , and some red coral bracelet on her wrist , Fancy pronounce her do ; and dance about her , singing : `` My pretty little mermaid , Oh !
come , and play with me : I 'll love you , I 'll welcome you ; And happy we shall be . ''
Now , while she have be work , the tide have creep higher and high ; and , a she sing , one wave run up and wet her foot .
`` Oh , what a pity I do n't put her farther up ! ''
cried Fancy ; `` the tide will wash her all away ; and I mean to keep her fresh , and show her to Aunt Fiction .
My poor mermaid !
-- I shall lose her ; but perhaps she will be happier in the sea : so I will let her go . ''
Mounting her rock , Fancy wait to see her work destroyed .
But the sea seem to pity her ; and wave after wave come up , without do any harm .
At last one break quite over the mermaid , and Fancy think that would be the end of her .
But , no : instead of scatter shell , stone , and weed , the wave lift the whole figure , without displace any thing , and gently bore it back into the sea .
`` Good by !
good by ! ''
cried Fancy , a the little figure float away ; then , a it disappear , she put her hand before her face , -- for she love her mermaid , and have give all her treasure to adorn her ; and now to lose her so soon seem hard , -- and Fancy 's eye be full of tear .
Another great wave come roll in ; but she do not look up to see it break , and , a minute after , she hear step trip toward her over the sand .
Still she do not stir ; for , just then , none of her playmate could take the place of her new friend , and she do n't want to see them .
`` Fancy !
Fancy ! ''
call a breezy voice , sweet than any she have ever hear .
But she do not raise her head , nor care to know who call .
The step come quite close ; and the touch of a cold , wet hand fell on her own .
Then she look up , and saw a strange little girl standing by her , who smile , show teeth like little pearl , and say , in the breezy voice : `` You want me to play with you , so I come . ''
`` Who be you ? ''
ask Fancy , wonder where she have see the child before .
`` I 'm your mermaid , '' say the child .
`` But the water carry her away , '' cry Fancy .
`` The wave only carry me out for the sea to give me life , and then bring me back to you , '' answer the new comer .
`` But be you really a mermaid ? ''
ask Fancy , begin to smile and believe .
`` I be really the one you make : look , and see if I 'm not ; '' and the little creature turn slowly round , that Fancy might be sure it be her own work .
She certainly be very like the figure that once lay on the sand , -- only she be not now make of stone and shell .
There be the long brown hair blow about her face , with a wreath of starry shell in it .
Her eye be gray , her cheek and lip rosy , her neck and arm white ; and from under her striped dress peep little bare foot .
She have pearl in her ear , coral bracelet , a golden belt , and a glass and comb in her hand .
`` Yes , '' say Fancy , draw near , `` you be my little mermaid ; but how do it happen that you come to me at last ? ''
`` Dear friend , '' answer the water-child , `` you believe in me , watch and wait long for me , shape the image of the thing you want out of your dear treasure , and promise to love and welcome me .
I could not help come ; and the sea , that be a fond of you a you be of it , help me to grant your wish . ''
`` Oh , I 'm glad , I 'm glad !
Dear little mermaid , what be your name ? ''
cried Fancy , kiss the cool cheek of her new friend , and put her arm about her neck .
`` Call me by my German cousin 's pretty name , -- Lorelei , '' answer the mermaid , kiss back as warmly a she could .
`` Will you come home and live with me , dear Lorelei ? ''
ask Fancy , still hold her fast .
`` If you will promise to tell no one who and what I be , I will stay with you as long a you love and believe in me .
As soon a you betray me , or lose your faith and fondness , I shall vanish , never to come back again , '' answer Lorelei .
`` I promise : but wo n't people wonder who you be ?
and , if they ask me , what shall I say ? ''
say Fancy .
`` Tell them you find me on the shore ; and leave the rest to me .
But you must not expect other people to like and believe in me a you do .
They will say hard thing of me ; will blame you for love me ; and try to part u .
Can you bear this , and keep your promise faithfully ? ''
`` I think I can .
But why wo n't they like you ? ''
say Fancy , look trouble .
`` Because they be not like you , dear , '' answer the mermaid , with salt tear in her soft eye .
`` They have not your power of see beauty in all thing , of enjoy invisible delight , and living in a world of your own .
Your Aunt Fiction will like me ; but your Uncle Fact wo n't .
He will want to know all about me ; will think I 'm a little vagabond ; and want me to be send away somewhere , to be make like other child .
I shall keep out of his way as much a I can ; for I 'm afraid of him . ''
`` I 'll take care of you , Lorelei dear ; and no one shall trouble you .
I hear Miss Fairbairn call ; so I must go .
Give me your hand , and do n't be afraid . ''
Hand in hand the two go toward the other child , who stop dig , and star at the new child .
Miss Fairbairn , who be very wise and good , but rather prim , star too , and say , with surprise : `` Why , my dear , where do you find that queer child ? ''
`` Down on the beach .
Is n't she pretty ? ''
answered Fancy , feel very proud of her new friend .
`` She have n't get any shoe on ; so she 's a beggar , and we must n't play with her , '' say one boy , who have be teach that to be poor be a very dreadful thing .
`` What pretty earring and bracelets she 's get ! ''
say a little girl , who think a great deal of her dress .
`` She do n't look a if she know much , '' say another child , who be keep study so hard that she never have time to dig and run , and make dirt-pies , till she fell ill , and have to be send to the sea-side .
`` What 's your name ?
and who be your parent ? ''
ask Miss Fairbairn .
`` I 've get no parent ; and my name be Lorelei , '' answer the mermaiden .
`` You mean Luly ; mind your pronunciation , child , '' say Miss Fairbairn , who correct every one she meet in something or other .
`` Where do you live ? ''
`` I have n't get any home now , '' say Lorelei , smile at the lady 's tone .
`` Yes , you have : my home be yours ; and you be go to stay with me always , '' cry Fancy , heartily .
`` She be my little sister , Miss Fairbairn : I find her ; and I 'm go to keep her , and make her happy . ''
`` Your uncle wo n't like it , my dear . ''
And Miss Fairbairn shake her head gravely .
`` Aunt will ; and Uncle wo n't mind , if I learn my lesson well , and remember the multiplication table all right .
He be go to give me some money , so I might learn to keep account ; but I 'll tell him to keep the money , and let me have Lorelei instead . ''
`` Oh , how silly ! ''
cry the boy who do n't like bare foot .
`` No , she be n't ; for , if she 's kind to the girl , maybe she 'll get some of her pretty thing , '' say the vain little girl .
`` Keeping account be a very useful and important thing .
I keep mine ; and mamma say I have great arth-met-i-cal talent , '' add the pale child , who study too much .
`` Come , child ; it 's time for dinner .
Fancy , you can take the girl to the house ; and your uncle will do what he think best about let you keep her , '' say Miss Fairbairn , pile them into the basket-wagon .
Fancy keep Lorelei close beside her ; and as soon a they reach the great hotel , where they all be stay with mother and father , uncle or aunt , she take her to kind Aunt Fiction , who be interested at once in the friendless child so mysteriously find .
She be satisfy with the little she could discover , and promise to keep her , -- for a time , at least .
`` We can imagine all kind of romantic thing about her ; and , by and by , some interesting story may be find out concern her .
I can make her useful in many way ; and she shall stay . ''
As Aunt Fiction lay her hand on the mermaid 's head , a if claim her for her own , Uncle Fact come stalk in , with his note-book in his hand , and his spectacle on his nose .
Now , though they be marry , these two person be very unlike .
Aunt Fiction be a graceful , picturesque woman ; who tell story charmingly , write poetry and novel , be very much beloved by young folk , and be the friend of some of the most famous people in the world .
Uncle Fact be a grim , grave , decide man ; whom it be impossible to bend or change .
He be very useful to every one ; know an immense deal ; and be always take note of thing he saw and heard , to be put in a great encyclopædia he be make .
He do n't like romance , love the truth , and want to get to the bottom of every thing .
He be always try to make little Fancy more sober , well-behaved , and learn ; for she be a freakish , dreamy , yet very lovable and charm child .
Aunt Fiction pet her to her heart 's content , and might have do her harm , if Uncle Fact have not have a hand in her education ; for the lesson of both be necessary to her , a to all of u .
`` Well , well , well !
who be this ? ''
he say briskly , a he turn his keen eye and powerful glass on the new comer .
Aunt Fiction tell him all the child have say ; but he answer impatiently : `` Tut , tut !
my dear : I want the fact of the case .
You be apt to exaggerate ; and Fancy be not to be rely on .
If the child be n't a fool , she must know more about herself than she pretend .
Now , answer truly , Luly , where do you come from ? ''
But the little mermaid only shake her head , and answer a before , `` Fancy find me on the beach , and want me to stay with her .
I 'll do her no harm : please , let me stay . ''
`` She have evidently be wash ashore from some wreck , and have forget all about herself .
Her wonderful beauty , her accent , and these ornament show that she be some foreign child , '' say Aunt Fiction , point to the earring .
`` Nonsense !
my dear : those be white pebble , not pearl ; and , if you examine them , you will find that those bracelet be the one you give Fancy a a reward for so well remember the fact I tell her about coral , '' say the uncle , who have turn Lorelei round and round , pinch her cheek , felt her hair , and examine her frock through the glass which nothing escape .
`` She may stay , and be my little playmate , may n't she ?
I 'll take care of her ; and we shall be very happy together , '' cry Fancy eagerly .
`` One ca n't be sure of that till one have try .
You say you will take care of her : have you get any money to pay her board , and buy her clothes ? ''
ask her uncle .
`` No ; but I think you 'd help me , '' answer Fancy wistfully .
`` Never say you 'll do a thing till you be sure you can , '' say Uncle Fact , a he take note of the affair , think they might be useful by and by .
`` I 've no objection to your keep the girl , if , after make inquiry about her , she prove to be a clever child .
She can stay awhile ; and , when we go back to town , I 'll put her in one of our charity school , where she can be teach to earn her living .
Can you read , Luly ? ''
`` No , '' say the mermaid , open her eye .
`` Can you write and cipher ? ''
`` What be that ? ''
ask Lorelei innocently .
`` Dear me !
what ignorance ! ''
cried Uncle Fact .
`` Can you sew , or tend baby ? ''
ask Aunt Fiction gently .
`` I can do nothing but play and sing , and comb my hair . ''
`` I see !
I see !
-- some hand-organ man 's girl .
Well , I 'm glad you keep your hair smooth , -- that 's more than Fancy do , '' say Uncle Fact .
`` Let u hear you sing , '' whisper his little niece ; and , in a voice as musical a the sound of ripple break on the shore , Lorelei sing a little song that make Fancy dance with delight , charm Aunt Fiction , and soften Uncle Fact 's hard face in spite of himself .
`` Very well , very well , indeed : you have a good voice .
I 'll see that you have proper teaching ; and , by and by , you can get your living by give singing-lessons , '' he say , turn over the leaf of his book , to look for the name of a skilful teacher ; for he have list of every useful person , place , and thing under the sun .
Lorelei laugh at the idea ; and Fancy think sing for gold , not love , a hard way to get one 's living .
Inquiries be make ; but nothing more be discover , and neither of the child would speak : so the strange child live with Fancy , and make her very happy .
The other child do n't care much about her ; for with them she be shy and cold , because she know , if the truth be tell , they would not believe in her .
Fancy have always play a good deal by herself , because she never find a mate to suit her ; now she have one , and they enjoy each other very much .
Lorelei teach her many thing besides new game ; and Aunt Fiction be charm with the pretty story Fancy repeat to her , while Uncle Fact be astonish at the knowledge of marine plant and animal which she gain without any book .
Lorelei teach her to swim , like a fish ; and the two play such wonderful prank in the water that people use to come down to the beach when they bath .
In return , Fancy try to teach her friend to read and write and sew ; but Lorelei could n't learn much , though she love her little teacher dearly , and every evening sing her to sleep with beautiful lullaby .
There be a great deal of talk about the curious stranger ; for her way be odd , and no one know what to make of her .
She would eat nothing but fruit and shell-fish , and drink nothing but salt water .
She do n't like tight clothes ; but would have run about in a loose , green robe , with bare foot and fly hair , if Uncle Fact would have allow it .
Morning , noon , and night , she plunge into the sea , -- no matter what the weather might be ; and she would sleep on no bed but one stuffed with dried sea-weed .
She make lovely chain of shell ; find splendid bit of coral ; and dive where no one else dare , to bring up wonderful plant and moss .
People offer money for these thing ; but she give them all to Fancy and Aunt Fiction , of whom she be very fond .
It be curious to see the sort of people who like both Fancy and her friend , -- poet , artist ; delicate , thoughtful child ; and a few old people , who have keep their heart young in spite of care and time and trouble .
Dashing young gentleman , fine young lady , worldly-minded and money-loving men and woman , and artificial , unchildlike child , the two friend avoid carefully ; and these person either make fun of them , neglect them entirely , or seem to be unconscious that they be alive .
The others they know at a glance ; for their face warm and brighten when the child come , they listen to their song and story , join in their play , and find rest and refreshment in their sweet society .
`` This will do for a time ; a Fancy be get strong , and not entirely waste her day , thanks to me !
But our holiday be nearly over ; and , as soon a I get back to town , I 'll take that child to the Ragged Refuge , and see what they can make of her , '' say Uncle Fact , who be never quite satisfied about Lorelei ; because he could find out so little concern her .
He be walk over the beach a he say this , after a hard day 's work on his encyclopædia .
He sit down on a rock in a quiet place ; and , instead of enjoy the lovely sunset , he fell to study the course of the cloud , the state of the tide , and the temperature of the air , till the sound of voice make him peep over the rock .
Fancy and her friend be play there , and the old gentleman wait to see what they be about .
Both be sit with their little bare foot in the water ; Lorelei be string pearl , and Fancy plait a crown of pretty green rush .
`` I wish I could go home , and get you a string of finer pearl than these , '' say Lorelei ; `` but it be too far away , and I can not swim now a I use to do . ''
`` I must look into this .
The girl evidently know all about herself , and can tell , if she choose , '' mutter Uncle Fact , get rather excite over this discovery .
`` Never mind the pearl : I 'd rather have you , dear , '' say Fancy lovingly .
`` Tell me a story while we work , or sing me a song ; and I 'll give you my crown . ''
`` I 'll sing you a little song that have get what your uncle call a moral to it , '' say Lorelei , laugh mischievously .
Then , in her breezy little voice , she sing the story of -- THE ROCK AND THE BUBBLE .
Oh !
a bare , brown rock Stood up in the sea , The wave at it foot Dancing merrily .
A little bubble Came sailing by , And thus to the rock Did it gayly cry , -- `` Ho !
clumsy brown stone , Quick , make way for me : I 'm the fair thing That float on the sea .
`` See my rainbow-robe , See my crown of light , My glitter form , So airy and bright .
`` O'er the water blue , I 'm float away , To dance by the shore With the foam and spray .
`` Now , make way , make way ; For the wave be strong , And their rippling foot Bear me fast along . ''
But the great rock stand Straight up in the sea : It look gravely down , And say pleasantly , -- `` Little friend , you must Go some other way ; For I have not stir This many a long day .
`` Great billow have dash , And angry wind blow ; But my sturdy form Is not overthrow .
`` Nothing can stir me In the air or sea ; Then , how can I move , Little friend , for thee ? ''
Then the wave all laugh , In their voice sweet ; And the sea-birds look , From their rocky seat , At the bubble gay , Who angrily cry , While it round cheek glow With a foolish pride , -- `` You shall move for me ; And you shall not mock At the word I say , You ugly , rough rock !
`` Be silent , wild bird !
Why stare you so ?
Stop laughing , rude wave , And help me to go !
`` For I be the queen Of the ocean here , And this cruel stone Can not make me fear . ''
Dashing fiercely up , With a scornful word , Foolish bubble break ; But rock never stir .
Then say the sea-birds , Sitting in their nest , To the little one Leaning on their breast , -- `` Be not like Bubble , Headstrong , rude , and vain , Seeking by violence Your object to gain ; `` But be like the rock , Steadfast , true , and strong , Yet cheerful and kind , And firm against wrong .
`` Heed , little birdlings , And wiser you 'll be For the lesson learn To-day by the sea . ''
`` Well , to be sure the song have get a moral , if that silly Fancy only see it , '' say Uncle Fact , pop up his bald head again a the song end .
`` I thank you : that 's a good little song for me .
But , Lorelei , be you sorry you come to be my friend ? ''
cried Fancy ; for , a she bend to lay the crown on the other 's head , she saw that she be look wistfully down into the water that kiss her foot .
`` Not yet : while you love me , I be happy , and never regret that I cease to be a mermaid for your sake , '' answer Lorelei , lay her soft cheek against her friend 's .
`` How happy I be the day my play-mermaid change to a real one ! ''
say Fancy .
`` I often want to tell people all about that wonderful thing , and let them know who you really be : then they 'd love you a I do , instead of call you a little vagabond . ''
`` Few would believe our story ; and those that do would wonder at me , -- not love me a you do .
They would put me in a cage , and make a show of me ; and I should be so miserable I should die .
So do n't tell who I be , will you ? ''
say Lorelei earnestly .
`` Never , '' cry Fancy , cling to her .
`` But , my deary , what will you do when uncle sends you away from me , a he mean to do as soon a we go home ?
I can see you sometimes ; but we can not be always together , and there be no ocean for you to enjoy in the city . ''
`` I shall bear it , if I can , for your sake ; if I can not , I shall come back here , and wait till you come again next year . ''
`` No , no !
I will not be part from you ; and , if uncle take you away , I 'll come here , and be a mermaid with you , '' cry Fancy .
The little friend throw their arm about each other , and be so full of their own feeling that they never saw Uncle Fact 's tall shadow flit across them , a he steal away over the soft sand .
Poor old gentleman !
he be in a sad state of mind , and do n't know what to do ; for in all his long life he have never be so puzzled before .
`` A mermaid indeed ! ''
he mutter .
`` I always think that child be a fool , and now I 'm sure of it .
She think she be a mermaid , and have make Fancy believe it .
I 've tell my wife a dozen time that she let Fancy read too many fairy tale and wonder-books .
Her head be full of nonsense , and she be just ready to believe any ridiculous story that be tell her .
Now , what on earth shall I do ?
If I put Luly in an asylum , Fancy will break her heart , and very likely they will both run away .
If I leave them together , Luly will soon make Fancy as crazy a she be herself , and I shall be mortify by have a niece who insist that her playmate be a mermaid .
Bless my soul !
how absurd it all be ! ''
Aunt Fiction have go to town to see her publisher about a novel she have write , and he do n't like to tell the queer story to any one else ; so Uncle Fact think it over , and decide to settle the matter at once .
When the child come in , he send Fancy to wait for him in the library , while he talk alone with Lorelei .
He do his best ; but he could do nothing with her , -- she dance and laugh , and tell the same tale a before , till the old gentleman confess that he have hear their talk on the rock : then she grow very sad , and own that she be a mermaid .
This make him angry , and he would n't believe it for an instant ; but tell her it be impossible , and she must say something else .
Lorelei could say nothing else , and weep bitterly when he would not listen ; so he lock her up and go to Fancy , who felt a if something dreadful be go to happen when she saw his face .
He tell her all he know , and insist that Lorelei be foolish or naughty to persist in such a ridiculous story .
`` But , uncle , I really do make a mermaid ; and she really do come alive , for I saw the figure float away , and then Lorelei appear , '' say Fancy , very earnestly .
`` It 's very likely you make a figure , and call it a mermaid : it would be just the sort of thing you 'd do , '' say her uncle .
`` But it be impossible that any come alive take place , and I wo n't hear any such nonsense .
You do n't see this girl come out of the water ; for she say you never look up , till she touch you .
She be a real child , who come over the beach from somewhere ; and you fancy she look like your figure , and believe the silly tale she tell you .
It be my belief that she be a sly , bad child ; and the sooner she be send away the good for you . ''
Uncle Fact be so angry and talk so loud , that Fancy felt frightened and bewilder ; and begin to think he might be right about the mermaid part , though she hat to give up the little romance .
`` If I agree that she be a real child , wo n't you let her stay , uncle ? ''
she say , forget that , if she lose her faith , her friend be lose also .
`` Ah !
then you have begin to come to your sens , have you ?
and be ready to own that you do n't believe in mermaid and such rubbish ? ''
cried Uncle Fact , stop in his tramp up and down the room .
`` Why , if you say there never be and never can be any , I suppose I must give up my fancy ; but I 'm sorry , '' sigh the child .
`` That 's my sensible girl !
Now , think a minute , my dear , and you will also own that it be best to give up the child as well a the mermaid , '' say her uncle briskly .
`` Oh !
no : we love one another ; and she be good , and I ca n't give her up , '' cry Fancy .
`` Answer me a few question ; and I 'll prove that she be n't good , that you do n't love her , and that you can give her up , '' say Uncle Fact , and number off the question on his finger a he speak .
`` Did n't Luly want you to deceive u , and every one else , about who she be ? ''
`` Yes , sir . ''
`` Do n't you like to be with her good than with your aunt or myself ? ''
`` Yes , sir . ''
`` Had n't you rather hear her song and story than learn your lesson ? ''
`` Yes , sir . ''
`` Is n't it wrong to deceive people , to love stranger more than those who be a father and mother to you , and to like silly tale well than useful lesson ? ''
`` Yes , sir . ''
`` Very well .
Then , do n't you see , that , if Luly make you do these wrong and ungrateful thing , she be not a good child , nor a fit playmate for you ? ''
Fancy do n't answer ; for she could n't feel that it be so , though he make it seem so .
When Uncle Fact talk in that way , she always get confused and give up ; for she do n't know how to argue .
He be right in a certain way ; but she felt a if she be right also in another way , though she could not prove it : so she hang her head , and let her tear drop on the carpet one by one .
Uncle Fact do n't mean to be unkind , but he do mean to have his own way ; and , when he saw the little girl 's sad face , he take her on his knee , and say , more mildly : `` Do you remember the story about the German Lorelei , who sing so sweetly , and lure people to death in the Rhine ? ''
`` Yes , uncle ; and I like it , '' answer Fancy , look up .
`` Well , my dear , your Lorelei will lead you into trouble , if you follow her .
Suppose she be what you think her , -- a mermaid : it be her delight to draw people into the water , where , of course , they drown .
If she be what I think her , -- a sly , bad child , who see that you be very simple , and who mean to get take care of without do any thing useful , -- she will spoil you in a bad way than if you follow her into the sea .
I 've get no little daughter of my own , and I want to keep you a safe and happy a if you be mine .
I do n't like this girl , and I want you to give her up for my sake .
Will you , Fancy ? ''
While her uncle say these thing , all the beauty seem to fall away from her friend , all the sweetness from their love , and all her faith in the little dream which have make her so happy .
Mermaids become treacherous , unlovely , unreal creature ; and Lorelei seem like a naughty , selfish child , who deceive her , and make her do wrong thing .
Her uncle have be very kind to her all her life ; and she love him , be grateful , and want to show that she be , by please him .
But her heart clung to the friend she have make , trust , and love ; and it seem impossible to give up the shadow , even though the substance be go .
She put her hand before her face for a moment ; then lay her arm about the old man 's neck , and whisper , with a little sob : `` I 'll give her up ; but you 'll be kind to her , because I be fond of her once . ''
As the last word leave Fancy 's lip , a long , sad cry sound through the room ; Lorelei sprung in , give her one kiss , and be see to run swiftly toward the beach , wring her hand .
Fancy fly after ; but , when she reach the shore , there be nothing to be see but the scattered pebble , shell , and weed that make the mock mermaid , float away on a receding wave .
`` Do you believe now ? ''
cried Fancy , weep bitterly , a she point to the wreck of her friend , and turn reproachfully toward Uncle Fact , who have follow in great astonishment .
The old gentleman look well about him ; then shake his head , and answer decidedly : `` No , my dear , I do n't .
It 's an odd affair ; but , I 've no doubt , it will be clear up in a natural way sometime or other . ''
But there he be mistake ; for this mystery never be clear up .
Other people soon forget it , and Fancy never speak of it ; yet she make very few friend , and , though she learn to love and value Uncle Fact as well a Aunt Fiction , she could not forget her dear playmate .
Year after year she come back to the sea-side ; and the first thing she always do be to visit the place where she use to play , and stretch her arm toward the sea , cry tenderly : `` O my little friend !
come back to me ! ''
But Lorelei never come again .
THE END .

Chapter 1 -- Two Girls Rose sit all alone in the big best parlor , with her little handkerchief lay ready to catch the first tear , for she be think of her trouble , and a shower be expect .
She have retire to this room a a good place in which to be miserable ; for it be dark and still , full of ancient furniture , sombre curtain , and hang all around with portrait of solemn old gentleman in wig , severe-nosed lady in top-heavy cap , and star child in little bob-tailed coat or short-waisted frock .
It be an excellent place for woe ; and the fitful spring rain that patter on the window-pane seem to sob , `` Cry away : I 'm with you . ''
Rose really do have some cause to be sad ; for she have no mother , and have lately lose her father also , which leave her no home but this with her great-aunt .
She have be with them only a week , and , though the dear old lady have try their best to make her happy , they have not succeed very well , for she be unlike any child they have ever see , and they felt very much a if they have the care of a low-spirited butterfly .
They have give her the freedom of the house , and for a day or two she have amuse herself roam all over it , for it be a capital old mansion , and be full of all manner of odd nook , charm room , and mysterious passage .
Windows break out in unexpected place , little balcony overhung the garden most romantically , and there be a long upper hall full of curiosity from all part of the world ; for the Campbells have be sea-captains for generation .
Aunt Plenty have even allow Rose to rummage in her great china closet a spicy retreat , rich in all the `` goody '' that child love ; but Rose seem to care little for these toothsome temptation ; and when that hope fail , Aunt Plenty give up in despair .
Gentle Aunt Peace have try all sort of pretty needle-work , and plan a doll 's wardrobe that would have win the heart of even an old child .
But Rose take little interest in pink satin hat and tiny hose , though she sew dutifully till her aunt catch her wiping tear away with the train of a wedding-dress , and that discovery put an end to the sew society .
Then both old lady put their head together and pick out the model child of the neighbourhood to come and play with their niece .
But Ariadne Blish be the bad failure of all , for Rose could not bear the sight of her , and say she be so like a wax doll she long to give her a pinch and see if she would squeak .
So prim little Ariadne be send home , and the exhausted aunty leave Rose to her own device for a day or two .
Bad weather and a cold kept her in-doors , and she spend most of her time in the library where her father 's book be store .
Here she read a great deal , cry a little , and dream many of the innocent bright dream in which imaginative child find such comfort and delight .
This suit her good than anything else , but it be not good for her , and she grow pale , heavy-eyed and listless , though Aunt Plenty give her iron enough to make a cooking-stove , and Aunt Peace pet her like a poodle .
Seeing this , the poor aunty rack their brain for a new amusement and determine to venture a bold stroke , though not very hopeful of it success .
They say nothing to Rose about their plan for this Saturday afternoon , but let her alone till the time come for the grand surprise , little dreaming that the odd child would find pleasure for herself in a most unexpected quarter .
Before she have time to squeeze out a single tear a sound break the stillness , make her prick up her ear .
It be only the soft twitter of a bird , but it seem to be a peculiarly gift bird , for while she listen the soft twitter change to a lively whistle , then a trill , a coo , a chirp , and end in a musical mixture of all the note , a if the bird burst out laughing .
Rose laugh also , and , forget her woe , jump up , say eagerly , `` It be a mocking-bird .
Where be it ? ''
Running down the long hall , she peep out at both door , but saw nothing feather except a draggle-tailed chicken under a burdock leaf .
She listen again , and the sound seem to be in the house .
Away she go , much excite by the chase , and follow the changeful song , it lead her to the china-closet door .
`` In there ?
How funny ! ''
she say .
But when she enter , not a bird appear except the everlastingly kissing swallow on the Canton china that line the shelf .
All of a sudden Rose 's face brighten , and , softly open the slide , she peer into the kitchen .
But the music have stop , and all she saw be a girl in a blue apron scrub the hearth .
Rose star about her for a minute , and then ask abruptly , `` Did you hear that mocking-bird ? ''
`` I should call it a phebe-bird , '' answer the girl , look up with a twinkle in her black eye .
`` Where do it go ? ''
`` It be here still . ''
`` Where ? ''
`` In my throat .
Do you want to hear it ? ''
`` Oh , yes !
I 'll come in . ''
And Rose creep through the slide to the wide shelf on the other side , be too hurry and puzzle to go round by the door .
The girl wipe her hand , cross her foot on the little island of carpet where she be strand in a sea of soap-suds , and then , sure enough , out of her slender throat come the swallow 's twitter , the robin 's whistle , the blue-jay 's call , the thrush 's song , the wood-dove 's coo , and many another familiar note , all end a before with the musical ecstacy of a bobolink singing and swinging among the meadow grass on a bright June day .
Rose be so astonished that she nearly fell off her perch , and when the little concert be over clapped her hand delightedly .
`` Oh , it be lovely !
Who teach you ? ''
`` The bird , '' answer the girl , with a smile , a she fell to work again .
`` It be very wonderful !
I can sing , but nothing half so fine a that .
What be your name , please ? ''
`` Phebe Moore . ''
`` I 've hear of phebe-birds ; but I do n't believe the real one could do that , '' laugh Rose , add , a she watch with interest the scattering of dab of soft soap over the brick , `` May I stay and see you work ?
It be very lonely in the parlor . ''
`` Yes , indeed , if you want to , '' answer Phebe , wring out her cloth in a capable sort of way that impress Rose very much .
`` It must be fun to swash the water round and dig out the soap .
I 'd love to do it , only aunt would n't like it , I suppose , '' say Rose , quite take with the new employment .
`` You 'd soon get tired , so you 'd better keep tidy and look on . ''
`` I suppose you help your mother a good deal ? ''
`` I have n't get any folk . ''
`` Why , where do you live , then ? ''
`` I 'm go to live here , I hope .
Debby want some one to help round , and I 've come to try for a week . ''
`` I hope you will stay , for it be very dull , '' say Rose , who have take a sudden fancy to this girl , who sing like a bird and work like a woman .
`` Hope I shall ; for I 'm fifteen now , and old enough to earn my own living .
You have come to stay a spell , have n't you ? ''
ask Phebe , look up at her guest and wonder how life could be dull to a girl who wear a silk frock , a daintily frilled apron , a pretty locket , and have her hair tie up with a velvet snood .
`` Yes , I shall stay till my uncle come .
He be my guardian now , and I do n't know what he will do with me .
Have you a guardian ? ''
`` My sake , no !
I be leave on the poor-house step a little mite of a baby , and Miss Rogers take a liking to me , so I 've be there ever since .
But she be dead now , and I take care of myself . ''
`` How interesting !
It be like Arabella Montgomery in the ` Gypsy 's Child . '
Did you ever read that sweet story ? ''
ask Rose , who be fond of tale of found-lings , and have read many .
`` I do n't have any book to read , and all the spare time I get I run off into the wood ; that rest me good than story , '' answer Phebe , a she finish one job and begin on another .
Rose watch her a she get out a great pan of bean to look over , and wonder how it would seem to have life all work and no play .
Presently Phebe seem to think it be her turn to ask question , and say , wistfully , `` You 've have lot of school , I suppose ? ''
`` Oh , dear me , yes !
I 've be at board school nearly a year , and I 'm almost dead with lesson .
The more I get , the more Miss Power give me , and I be so miserable that I ` most cried my eye out .
Papa never give me hard thing to do , and he always teach me so pleasantly I love to study .
Oh , we be so happy and so fond of one another !
But now he be go , and I be leave all alone . ''
The tear that would not come when Rose sit wait for it come now of it own accord two of them in fact and roll down her cheek , tell the tale of love and sorrow well than any word could do it .

Phebe 's last word make Rose smile in spite of her tear , and she look out from behind her apron with an April face , say in a tone of comic distress , `` That 's one of my trouble !

Phebe laugh with her a she say encouragingly , `` Everyone call it so , and it 's a real good name , for all the Mrs. Campbells live handy by , and keep come up to see the old lady . ''
`` I could stand the aunt , but there be dozen of cousin , dreadful boy all of them , and I detest boy !
Some of them come to see me last Wednesday , but I be lie down , and when auntie come to call me I go under the quilt and pretend to be asleep .
I shall have to see them some time , but I do dread it so . ''
And Rose give a shudder , for , have live alone with her invalid father , she know nothing of boy , and consider them a specie of wild animal .

I 'm afraid of horse , and boat make me ill , and I hate boy ! ''
And poor Rose wring her hand at the awful prospect before her .
One of these horror alone she could have bear , but all together be too much for her , and she begin to think of a speedy return to the detested school .
